III 
 
 
==PAGE:4==
IV 
ABSTRACT 
As the building industry moves towards a more sustainable future the need for adequate solar 
shading and solar control selection is increasing [1][2]. Today the selection process is only 
based on energy efficiency and visual comfort for internal conditions, leaving the visual effi-
ciency and performance of a view unexplored. To make more quality-conscious selections the 
foundation of decision-making should be expanded. Moving towards an index for characteri-
zation of view out in office buildings, this research paper’s main objective is to investigate the 
research-question “Is photometric values and amount of view applicable for characteriza-
tion of view out with the purpose of appropriate solar shading and solar control selection?”. 
In search for the most adequate solar shading selection-method, the thought of greenery as 
shading substituting greenery in the view was developed. This is because a view containing 
natural green obtains a higher view quality than those without. Views are in general not 
changeable, and greenery as shading could have the potential of upgrading the quality of a 
view without changing the actual view. The secondary objective of this paper is therefore to 
investigate the research question “Can greenery as shading substitute natural greenery in 
the view?”. 
Both the main and secondary research-question were investigated based on a subjective user 
assessment in virtual reality, encapsulating visual- perception and comfort of totally 38 
scenes. Each scene was modelled in Rhino, rendered with Radiance, Tone Mapped, and set 
up in Unity. To answer the main research-question the photometric values, achromatic con-
trast, chromatic contrast, achromatic complexity & chromatic complexity were determined 
for each scene along with the amount of view. The scenes assessed represents 5 shading con-
ditions. The contrast in each scene was calculated based on the RAMMG methodology devel-
oped by Rizzi et al. and the visual complexity was calculated based on the RMS methodology 
developed by Pavel et al. [3][4]. Lastly, the amount of view was calculated based on a con-
nectedness script developed by Mikkel Kofod Pedersen. Based on hypothesis- and effect size 
testing it was discovered that people prefer high visual complexity, high amounts of view, and 
low chromatic contrast. Since a significant relation between subjective responses and the var-
iables chromatic contrast, visual complexity, and amount of view were found, they are con-
==PAGE:5==
V 
cluded as applicable for characterization of view out. To answer the secondary research-ques-
tion, the shading conditions “no shading with trees” and “shutters with greenery and no 
trees” were investigated. Based on hypothesis testing it was discovered that there is no sta-
tistical difference in the distribution of subjective responses comparing greenery as shading 
and natural green in the view. Hence it was concluded that greenery as shading has the po-
tential of substituting natural green in the view. 
 
Keywords: Solar Shading; Solar Control; Subjective User Assessment; Virtual Reality; Unity; 
Visual Perception; Visual Comfort; Radiance Renderings; Tone Mapping; Contrast, Visual 
Complexity, Amount of View, Greenery as Shading.

ACKNOWLEDGEMENT 
This entire thesis was conducted on support, encouragement, and assistance from several key 
individuals. Our highest gratitude goes to our supervisor Mandana Sarey Khanie and co-su-
pervisor Mikkel Kofod Pedersen who have both been contributors and great supporters 
throughout all stages of our thesis.  
We want to thank Jiaqi Xu, Maria Christina Prokou and Maria Stefaniotou who has con-
ducted the thesis papers “A Parametric Study on Façade Geometries for Optimized View Access Using Ad-
vanced Rendering Technique” and “Exploring Combined Effects of Visual and Audio Environment in Educational 
Buildings Using Virtual Reality”, for their inspiring work which is the basis of this thesis project. 
Throughout the project, Kynthia Chamilothori guided us through the procedure of sky map-
ping. Furthermore, talking with Jan Wienold we became aware of the greatest strengths and 
weaknesses of our study. We want to thank both for their guidance.  
Creating a C# script for setting up the VR-environments in Unity, Software Engineer Student 
Asbjørn Olling assisted us. Therefore, we would like to thank him for and acknowledge the 
time and effort he put into working with us. 
For development of both English and Danish VR-experiment questionnaires, Cognitive Science 
student and writer Astrid Olling helped us prof read and select appropriate choice of words, 
that captures the exact same understanding of the question in both English and Danish. 
Hence, we want to thank her for the time and the effort she put into the work. 
As a part of improving the outline of the study, Louis Andersen and Peder Olesen has put a 
great effort and a lot of time into proofreading this study. This we want to thank them for. 
We want to thank IT-supporter Jan Karolini who have provided us with access to additional 
DTU computers and assisted us throughout the entire process. Additionally, we want to thank 
the DTU HPC Support Team for their assistance regarding use of HPC services. 
Finally, we owe family, friends, and acquaintances a great thanks for not only supporting us 
all the way through our thesis but also for taking time to participate in our VR-experiment. 
==PAGE:7==
VII 
PREFACE  
The thesis was conducted at the International Center for Indoor Environment and Energy, at 
the Technical University of Denmark – department of Civil Engineering. It was carried out as 
a last part of our educational MSc program in Architectural Engineering and reflects an effort 
of 30 ETCS credits. The topic of this thesis “Characterization of View-Out towards an index for appropri-
ate selection of solar-control systems” was developed in collaboration with our supervisor Mandana 
Sarey Khanie to reflect our interests and a current “hot topic” of the industry. The previous 
master thesis studies “A Parametric Study on Façade Geometries for Optimized View Access Using Advanced 
Rendering Technique” conducted by Jiaqi Xu and “Exploring Combined Effects of Visual and Audio Environ-
ment in Educational Buildings Using Virtual Reality” conducted by Maria Christina Prokou and Maria 
Stefaniotou, treats subjects related to the topic of this thesis, hence their research act as the 
foundation for this thesis.

==PAGE:24==
INTRODUCTION – CHAPTER 1 
1 
 
1 INTRODUCTION 
In the following sections, the content and topic of this thesis will be presented. Firstly, the 
relevance of this study is described based on currently available methods, theory, and stand-
ards. Secondly the thesis objectives are described and hereafter the main and secondary re-
search-question are defined.  
 
1.1 Relevance 
As the world moves towards a more sustainable future, higher demands are placed on the 
building industry and their building projection approaches[1]. Over the years buildings have 
gradually become more airtight and more insulated[1], while the popularity of large window 
areas has increased. Windows are important since they provide access to daylight and view, 
which are crucial for the satisfaction, wellbeing, and performance of occupants[5].  
 With more airtight and insulated buildings, and with the increased popularity of large window 
areas, the need for adequate solar shading and solar control selection have grown bigger[1] 
[2]. An essential part of solar shading and solar control strategies is to protect a building from 
any unwanted solar gains during summer and reflected- or direct sunlight causing glare mainly 
during winter[2]. The wrong choice of shading strategy could potentially result in thermal- or 
visual discomfort. Visual comfort is not only defined by glare, since the sensitivity of a per-
ceived view changes along with the quality of the view [6] [7]. How interesting the view is play 
a huge role in the level of visual comfort [8]. Moreover, daylight and facade patterns in a room 
have a huge impact on occupants subjective and psychological experience of the room, which 
is closely related to the choice of solar shading [9]. In commercial buildings strict demands on 
thermal- and visual indoor climate are a necessity due to the wide range of user preferences, 
hence solar shading and solar control selection plays an important role. 
So far methods have been developed to quantify energy efficiency and to some extent visual 
comfort, however only a limited range of methods are available for quantification of visual 
efficiency and performance of a view. The relevance of tangible methods to assess the overall 
==PAGE:25==
INTRODUCTION – CHAPTER 1 
2 
 
visual experience of the view, have become even clearer from the newly approved EU stand-
ards under the EU committee CEN/TC 169 - Light and lighting. Standards like DS/EN 
17037:2018 [10] describe simple methods for assessing the view that becomes increasingly 
more difficult when assessing complex geometries [10]. However, the standard offers no 
method for detailed quantification of the view content. Also, vertical view angles are not con-
sidered nor is the effect shading devices have on the perceived view related to content, light, 
and color.  
W. H. Ko et al. have established a framework for assessing the view and its overall quality 
based on three categories: View Content, View Access and View Clarity [11]. Tangible meth-
ods are available for quantification of View Content and View Access, but since there is a clear 
gap in research no methods are available that fully covers the View Clarity aspect.  
To determine the quality of a view based on View Content an assessment method has been 
developed by Hellinga and Hordijk specified as ‘view quality’ [12]. With the method, a view is 
rated based on physical and psychological approaches from low- to high quality, where nine 
parameters are assessed covering for ‘elements of the view’ and ‘attractiveness factors of the 
view’.  
Irmak Turan and C. F. Reinhart [13] have developed a method for internal visual connectivity 
and it was tested as a potential method for assessing View Access by Jiaqi Xu [14]. This 
method can quickly assess 2D horizontal view angles for most shading conditions. However, 
it lacks the ability to assess vertical view angles or even assessing the total amount of view in 
a 3D space, which is necessary for evaluating views with complex or organic architectural 
shading patterns. Further development of this method will result in a more tangible way to 
assess the total amount of view in a 3D space for all shading types, building geometries, and 
window location(s) & shape(s).  
Solar shading devices interrupts the view, altering visibility, light, and color, either by ob-
structing or distorting the view [11]. These changes of the perceived view are linked to human 
perception and might be caused by fundamental functions of the Human Visual System [15]. 
Therefore, contrast, sharpness and visibility of the luminous and chromatic environment 
might be suitable indicators for partial view evaluation. Hence quantification of photometric 
values might prove useful for characterization of a view out. 
==PAGE:26==
INTRODUCTION – CHAPTER 1 
3 
 
This thesis tries to explore quantifiable indicators and methods for assessing the holistic ex-
perience of view quality for any type of geometries both simple and complex. With this re-
search and future supporting research, the aim is to develop an index for an appropriate solar 
shading and solar control selection. 
 
When assessing the above-mentioned gap in research the great importance of View Content 
and selection of a shading device were underlined. A view containing natural green are more 
positively evaluated than an almost identical view without [12]. Hence a tool has even been 
developed for evaluation of ‘visual contact to greenery in the view’ described as ‘Greenery-
View factor’ [16]. View is often fixed and cannot be changed according to user preferences, 
whereas shading types and shading control is optional and changeable. Yet the potential of 
substituting natural green in the view with greenery as shading has still not been disclosed. 
Since a view is evaluated more positive when containing natural green, natural greenery as 
shading could potentially heighten the quality of a view in settings where there is no natural 
green available. A secondary aim of this study is therefore to investigate the basic potential 
of greenery as shading substituting natural green in the view, as a baseline for future devel-
opment of shading devices containing greenery.  
 
1.2 Thesis Objectives 
To close the gap in the field of research regarding visual efficiency and performance of a view, 
the main objective of this thesis is to detect whether photometric values and amount of view 
are appropriate measures for characterization of view out. Amount of view in combination 
with photometric values have been selected for further investigation since it is possible with 
amount of view to assess View Access, and since photometric values has the potential of 
assessing both View Clarity and View Content in collaboration with view quality ratings. To 
assess this objective the following main research-question is set up for investigation: “Is pho-
tometric values and amount of view applicable for characterization of view out with the 
purpose of appropriate solar shading and solar control selection?” 
 
==PAGE:27==
INTRODUCTION – CHAPTER 1 
4 
 
The secondary objective of this study is to determine whether there is a potential for devel-
opment of shading devices containing greenery, since it could lead to a higher view quality 
obtained where no natural green is found in the view. To assess this objective the following 
secondary research-question is set up for investigation: “Can greenery as shading substitute 
natural greenery in the view?”  
 
1.3 Thesis Structure 
This chapter introduced the relevance of the thesis followed by a description of the thesis 
objectives and research-questions investigated throughout this report. In the following sec-
tion the structure of the thesis is presented to orient the reader. The structure displayed pre-
sents the relationship both within and between sections in the main chapters: CHAPTER 2 – 
BACKGROUND, CHAPTER 3 – METHODOLOGY and CHAPTER 4 - RESULTS.  
 
CHAPTER 2 – BACKGROUND: In the background chapter the foundation of this thesis is de-
scribed. This covers for a description of view quality as a whole and different available meth-
ods for quantification of a view. Additionally, the human visual system is explained along with 
potential assessment methods for additional characterization of a view. Finally, is the poten-
tial of VR-experiments disclosed along with its obvious benefits. 
CHAPTER 3 – METHODOLOGY: In the methodology chapter all methodologies conducted in 
this thesis are presented. As a first thing, two pilot studies are presented since they make up 
the basis of the cause of actions taken in the methodology section. 
CHAPTER 4 – RESULTS: In the result chapter all results are presented. First the demographic 
data is introduced to get a better understanding of the subjects, whereafter the results of the 
study is presented through hypothesis testing. 
CHAPTER 4 – 9 descripes respectively the results, the discussions, the conclussions, the 
limitations of the study, and suggested future work.  
 
==PAGE:28==
INTRODUCTION – CHAPTER 1 
5 
 
 
 
==PAGE:29==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
6 
 
2 BACKGROUND 
In the background chapter relevant theories that relate to the research-questions and objec-
tives are precented. First, an overall description of view and its quality is provided followed 
by related assessment methods and theories. Since the perceived view is complex, a de-
scription of the human visual system is provided followed by related assessment methods. 
 
2.1 View Outside of the Window 
A window view quality assessment framework based on recommendations from building 
standards, green certification systems and scientific literature, defines the full view quality 
out of a window as the correlation between content, access and clarity of a view  [11]. 
 
 
 
Figure 1: Overview of parameters and subcategories determining the quality of a view outside of a window. 
==PAGE:30==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
7 
 
2.1.1 View Content 
The view content covers the visual features within the frame(s) of the view(s) [11]. The sub-
categories defining the view content is described below.  
Nature and urban features: In general, a view containing elements of nature is preferred over 
a view containing urban scenes. The urban scenes have shown to be more desirable if they 
contain features as landmarks. Furthermore, the perception of urban scenes is, in general, 
depending on age, maintenance, and complexity [11] also known as the attractiveness of the 
view [12]. 
Since natural green has a positive effect on people, an urban view becomes more attractive 
when natural green is visible[17]. Views containing nature has shown to be more desirable if 
they contain water and have shown to have a positive impact on psychological well-being 
[11]. 
Horizontal stratification: A view of different layers can be divided into three sections; ground 
(human activity), landscape (buildings, nature, or horizon only) and sky (weather) which de-
picts different layers of information [11][10]. The more layers visible in a view the more infor-
mation is provided [12] which is why the preference of a view increases with the number of 
layers visible [18]. People find a complete view with as much interesting information as pos-
sible preferable [7] corresponding to a highly diverse view.  
Content distance: The content distance covers the distance between the occupant and the 
scenes in the view where longer distances are preferred [11]. This is because more infor-
mation is provided when the content of the view is further away [12]. Longer distances relieve 
eyestrain symptoms and cause the muscles in the eye to relax. Relaxing the eyes by a view 
can especially be beneficial in commercial buildings, where occupants might experience eye-
strain when focusing on a computer screen to long. The long distances are only shown pref-
erably in urban scenes [11].  
Dynamic features: Dynamic features in a view captures the attention of the occupant and is 
preferable. The perception of the dynamic features is most likely affected by the scale, dura-
tion and frequency of the moving objects [11]. Nevertheless, traffic too close to the windows 
does as well as nearby buildings harm people’s perception of the view [6].  
==PAGE:31==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
8 
 
 
2.1.2 View Access  
The view access is the amount of view available from the position of the occupant and is 
determined by the geometrical relation between the occupant and the view out of the win-
dow [11]. The subcategories defining the view access is described below.  
View angle of the window: The view angle of the window represents the horizontal and ver-
tical angles between the occupant and the view out of the window. This angle is constrained 
by any blocking furniture in the room. The preferred view angles are among other things de-
pending on the distance to the visual features in the view. The horizontal stratification is de-
termining the optimal window size in regards to view access, where the level of the skyline in 
the view determines the optimal window height [11]. The angle of the window is significantly 
affected by the occupant position in the room and thereby distance to the window. 
Spatial assessment of view access: The spatial assessment of view out is defined by the per-
centage of relevant floor area that is related to the view out of a certain window(s) [11].  
 
2.1.3 View Clarity 
The view clarity describes how clear the view is both before, inside and after the window, 
which depends on the design of the window(s), glazing type, shading type, and visual acuity 
of the occupant. Elements blocking the view or wrong choice of shading might lead to an 
unsatisfactory view clarity due to change in color and image perception [11]. The subcatego-
ries defining the view clarity is described below. 
Window design: The design of the window and the interruption in view out by mullions, can 
have a significant impact on the perceived scene by the occupant. Wrong placement of hori-
zontal mullions might interfere with the transition between horizontal stratification layers 
and therefore the relations in the view. Wrong placement of the vertical mullions interrupts 
the vertical relations of the view but can be repaired by the human stereoscopic vision. Since 
content in the view and access to the view is influencing the clarity of perception, a minimum 
amount and width of the mullions are preferred [11].  
==PAGE:32==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
9 
 
Glazing and shading materials: Façade materials and shading types are used to reduce the 
risk of glare, overheating and to obtain privacy. The optical properties of facades and shading 
systems have a substantial impact on how the occupant perceives a certain view. With tinted 
glass, the colors in the view change and thereby also the contrast intensity. The view clarity 
depends on building envelope shape, openness, and directional transmittance.  
Temporal attributes of view clarity: Solar shading systems are used to prevent overheating 
and glare. How many hours a solar shading system is active can be determined from the cli-
mate-based daylight matrixes; Spatial Daylight Autonomy (sDA) or Annual Sunlight Exposure 
(ASE). When the shading system is activated it causes a change in perceived luminance con-
trast and view clarity by the occupant. The shading type should therefore optimally be deter-
mined based on how many hours the system is in use to achieve the best possible view quality 
registered by the occupant. 
 
2.2 View Content Assessment  
The view content and its quality are defined by nature and urban features, horizontal strati-
fication layers, content distance and dynamic features present in the view, as described in 
section 2.1.1. A quantification method that covers this specifically are presented in the fol-
lowing section. 
 
2.2.1 View Quality 
The research paper “The D&V analysis method: A method for analysis of daylight access and 
view quality” written by Hellinga and Hordijk describes an assessment method for quantifica-
tion of view content from a certain viewpoint [12]. Since this source describes the quality of 
the content in the view as “view quality”, this is what it will be referred as throughout the 
thesis. Based on physical and psychological approaches the accuracy of the scoring system 
ranking views from 0 points (low view quality, LVQ) to 12 points (high view quality, HVQ) was 
tested and finally approved [12]. The assessment method is tested for a scenario with no ob-
structing elements in front of the view. The quantification of the outside view is assessed 
through a flowchart covering the following 9 points:  
==PAGE:33==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
10 
 
View elements 
1. The character of the view 
2. Containment of natural green 
3. Visible layers of the view (ground, nearby content, distant content, and sky) 
4. Containment of natural water 
5. Nearby cars/traffic 
6. Diversity of the view 
 
Attractiveness of the view  
7. Domination of buildings with a similar character 
8. Maintenance of the building in the view 
9. Features of the building(s) 
 
In the pilot project “A Parametric Study on Facade Geometries for Optimized View Access Us-
ing Advanced Rendering Technique” conducted by Jiaqi Xu an assessment method is sug-
gested which combines scoring strategies of the above-mentioned study and the Danish 
Standard (DS) “Daylight in buildings” [10]. See section 3.1.1. In the DS the quality of the view 
from a certain position is described by: 
- 
Size of the daylight openings(s) 
- 
The width of the view (horizontal sight angle) 
- 
The outside distance of view  
- 
The number of layers  
- 
The quality of the environmental information of the view 
Most of these dependencies have been considered in the assessment of view quality con-
ducted by Hellinga and Hordijk and the remaining will be considered in the assessment of 
view access. Therefore, the view content is in this study quantified exclusively by the scoring 
system presented by Hellinga and Hordijk [12]. An overview of the scoring system is presented 
in Figure 2 below. 
==PAGE:34==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
11 
 
 
Figure 2: Scoring system for view out of a window. 
==PAGE:35==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
12 
 
2.3 View Access Assessment 
As described in section 2.1.2 view access is dependent on the view angle of the window and 
the spatial assessment of view access. Today methods available for View Access Assessment 
is limited to simple 2D horizontal geometries, hench they lack the ability to assess vertical or 
complex geometries. In the following a View Access Assessment method developed by Jiaqi 
Xu is described.  
 
2.3.1 Visual Connectivity 
The research paper “A New Framework for Evaluating Indoor Visual Connectivity in Open Plan 
Workspaces” written by Irmak Turan and C. F. Reinhart, describes an analysis framework for 
quantification of the internal visual connectivity (IVC) [13]. More specifically the raytracing 
method analyzes the connectedness between grid points in a three-dimensional grid and the 
occupant position at eye-level. This means that any obstructing objects will reduce the inter-
nal visual connectedness [13].  
Based on this concept, Jiaqi Xu have developed a 2D raytracing method for detection of con-
nectedness between an occupant and a view outside of a window [14]. The concept named 
‘Visual Connectivity’ describes the visibility percentage of a 360-degree horizontal grid where 
the view is visible. The method thereby accounts for any obstructing elements as solar shad-
ing, window reveals, and facade geometry in a 2D grid. This method however does not con-
sider any horizontal obstructions and are therefore only applicable for vertical façade- and 
shading geometries. The concept of ‘Visual Connectivity’ is visualized in Figure 3. 
 
 
==PAGE:36==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
13 
 
 
2.4 View Clarity Assessment 
As described in section 2.1.3 view clarity is dependent on window design, glazing & shading 
materials, and temporal attributes of view clarity. To this day there have yet not been devel-
oped a method for exclusively assessment of View Clarity. However, Aperture Based Day-
light modelling describes some fundamental principles that could potentially cover for some 
aspects of it.  
 
2.4.1 Aperture Based Daylight Modelling 
According to daylight modelling expert John Mardaljevic today’s non-simulation-based meth-
ods in guidelines for sunlight access into buildings is deficient and a lot of important aspects 
are neglected [19]. The variety of existing methodologies is lacking one or more of the follow-
ing perspectives: 
Figure 3: Illustration A describes the section from which rays can be drawn between the occupant and the view with no obstructing solar shad-
ing. Illustration B describes the section from which rays can be drawn between the occupant and the view with obstructing solar shading. 
==PAGE:37==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
14 
 
 
To improve from today’s methodologies by covering all the above-mentioned perspectives 
the Sunlight Beam Index (SBI) was developed and extended by the Aperture Skylight Index 
(ASI) and the concept View Lumen. From these, potentially new guidelines and standards 
could be developed. This is described in the following sections. 
 
Sunlight Beam Index 
The research paper “The Sunlight Beam Index: A new metric to quantify the sunlight potential 
of arbitrarily complex building apertures” written by John Mardaljevic and Nicolas Roy de-
scribes the idea of an SBI. 
The sunlight beam measure is an area measure that describes the average connectedness of 
an aperture to all the annual sun positions accounting for the location of the aperture and 
obstructing entities [19]. Changing sun positions causes a change in the cross-sectional area 
of the sunlight beam and the effect of shading entities (i.e. reveals), shown in Figure 4. To 
cover the sunlight beam potential the line-of-sight between grid points over an aperture and 
the sun is measured for all sun positions occurring over a year.  
[19] 
==PAGE:38==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
15 
 
 
If more apertures are connected to a room, the total SBI is calculated as a sum of all aperture’s 
sunlight beam potential. Likewise, the total sunlight beam potential of a building can be cal-
culated, which makes it possible to compare the SBI of different buildings. All of this with the 
SBI presented in: m2 hrs. Based on the principles of the SBI the Aperture Skylight Index was 
developed. 
 
Aperture Skylight Index 
The research paper “Aperture Sunlight and Skylight Indices” written by John Mardaljevic and 
Nicolas Roy describes the idea of an aperture skylight index ASI [20]. The aperture skylight 
measure is an area measure that describes the average connectedness of an aperture to the 
illumination of a uniform luminance sky dome. A uniform sky is chosen over a CIE overcast 
sky, since the CIE overcast sky is an extreme type of overcast sky and therefore a rarely oc-
curring phenomenon [20].  
A uniform sky provides 2000 lux on the horizontal corresponding to each m2 of an aperture 
connected to the sky receiving 2000 lumen [20]. The higher the zenith angle of an aperture 
Figure 4: Possible sunlight beam passing through a window at four different sun positions A, B and C. The cross-sectional 
area and shading caused by reveal changes with the position.  
==PAGE:39==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
16 
 
gets, the lower the normalized lux is. Any shading conditions reduces the connectedness of 
an aperture to the skylight and thereby the received lumen. 
As well as for the SBI it is possible to add up ABIs of an entire room which makes it possible 
to compare different rooms across a building (or room designs in general). The total API of a 
building makes it possible to compare the connectedness of different building designs [20]. 
From the ASI a new concept was developed. Aperture connectedness corresponds to the view 
potential of an aperture. This specific idea is the root of the concept View Lumen.  
 
View Lumen 
The research paper “Aperture-based daylight modelling: Introducing the ‘View Lumen’” writ-
ten by John Mardaljevic introduces the term View Lumen and describes a new method to 
determine a view out [21]. View Lumen describes the concept of measuring illuminance levels 
across an aperture to determine its ‘connectedness’ to the surrounding layers: ground, fore-
ground, and sky [21].  
In the case of the ASI, a m2 aperture at a zenith angle of 0° which is not exposed to any shading 
conditions has a connectedness of 2000 lumen to the sky. For the concept of View Lumen, a 
m2 aperture that is not exposed to any shading conditions receives 2000 lumen in total from 
the three layers. In a case with a vertical aperture (a zenith angle of 90°), a center perpendic-
ular to the horizontal line, no shading conditions, and no obstructions in the view, the aper-
ture receives 1000 lumen by both the sky and the ground. This is because each parameter 
covers half of the view. For an aperture under the same conditions, but this time including 
obstructing entities, the received lumen from each layer differs (the connectedness to each 
layer differs) depending on the location, size, and design of the obstruction. The total amount 
of lumens received at the aperture only changes when shading conditions occur (i.e. reveal, 
shading devises, overhang, etc.) [21]. 
The view potential at an aperture is preferably described based on measurements behind the 
concept View Lumen compared to measurements of the solid angle for several reasons.  
In research ‘illuminance effect produced by obstruction’ and ‘the solid angle subtended by 
obstruction’ is both described as a function of ‘the distance to the obstruction’. This showed 
==PAGE:40==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
17 
 
to follow a similar rate of change [21]. In other words, the solid angle drops at the same rate 
as the illuminance effect produced by the obstruction, to an increase in distance to an ob-
struction. The view angle does however not change because of different view directions of 
the obstruction whereas the illuminance effect does [21]. 
Comparing a point measure (solid angle) to the measurements behind the concept View Lu-
men, it is shown, that the point measure often overestimates how much lumen is received at 
an aperture. The only scenario where the point measure does not overestimate the total re-
ceived lumens, is where the view is perpendicular to the horizon, there is no effect of shading, 
and there are no obstructions [21].   
 
Since Aperture Based Daylight Modelling does not cover for all aspects of view clarity and since 
no tangible methods are described for execution, this theory was excluded from the study, but 
might still prove relevant for indication of some parameters of view clarity. This study is rather 
new and future development of it should be followed closely. 
 
2.5 Human Perception 
Human perception is depending on i.a. sight and thereby also the Human Visual System 
(HVS) and its functions. In the following a short introduction to the HVS is precented, fol-
lowed by a description of humans chromatic- and achromatic perception. Since the HVS is 
depending on the light conditions in a space, some basic terms of light are presented as the 
first thing. 
 
2.5.1 Basic Terms of Light 
As a short introduction to light, some basic important terms are described in the following.  
Luminous flux covers the total amount of light (radiant energy) emitted from a lightsource in 
all directions, and it is presented the unit lumen (lm). The light emitted from a lightsource 
falling on a surface is described as Illuminance which is presented in lm/m2 or lux. The concept 
of illuminance is visualized in Figure 5. 
==PAGE:41==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
18 
 
 
Figure 5: Illuminance is the amount of light falling on a surface. 
 
As opposed to luminous flux, luminous intensity represetns the amount of light emitted in a 
certain direction. The term covers lumionus flux observed in a small cone in a given direction 
where it is divided by the solid angle of the cone. The unit of luminous intensity is the candela 
(cd). The amount of light that is emitted from, passing through, or rejected from an object is 
described as luminance which is given by the unit cd/m2. The concept of luminance is 
presented in Figure 6. 
 
Figure 6: Luminance is the amount of light passing emitted from, passing through or reflected from an object. 
 
 
==PAGE:42==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
19 
 
2.5.2 Human Visual System 
The Human Visual System (HVS) is a collaboration between the eyes and the brain[15]. The 
optical component of the eye prints an image through the sensory cells in the retina where 
light is captured and projected as an image. The image projected is processed and further 
interpreted by the brain. Humans are predators and are therefore provided with frontally 
mounted eyes. Frontally mounted eyes cause the visual field of the eyes to overlap and 
greater detail is captured within this field of view. The predator perspective does also show 
in the limited range of possible head movement and a wide range of eye movement [15], see 
Figure 7. The range of the head movements is 60° to -40° vertically and 55° to -55° horizon-
tally. The range of eye movements has in studies shown to be negatively affected by age [22], 
and it is therefore not easy to define exactly. The eye movements occur depending on the 
contraction and relaxation of the extra-ocular muscles in the eye [15]. 
Figure 7: For humans the head movement range is limited whereas the eye movement range is wide [23][15]. 
 
==PAGE:43==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
20 
 
The eye is consisting of multiple different elements as shown in Figure 8. The outermost layer 
of the eye called the sclera, functions as a protecting layer while it maintains the spherical 
shape of the eye. Under this layer is the choroid layer that contains blood vessels providing 
oxygen and nutrients to the retina. The retina is located underneath this layer and is as men-
tioned the layer where the light gets absorbed and converted to electrical signals that are 
passed on to the brain [15]. 
 
 
Figure 8: Section of eye [15]. 
Image Author: Macrovector, Name: Ophthalmologist eye set 
 
The light enters the eye through the Cornea. The amount of light entering the retina of the 
eye depends on the pupil diameter. The pupil is encircled by the iris and its size is regulated 
by two groups of muscles[15]. The ciliary body produces fluid that is located between the lens 
and the cornea. The fluid removes any waste product produced in this area. Everywhere else 
this job is done by blood however transparency is crucial in this specific area [15].  
After light enters the pupil, it passes through the crystalline lens. The shape of the crystalline 
lens regulates dependent on if the vison is near or distant.  If it is near the lens is round and 
==PAGE:44==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
21 
 
fattened and if it is distant the lens is flattened. The shape of the lens is regulated by the ciliary 
muscles in the eye.  
After passing the crystalline lens the light reaches the retina. The retina composes of four 
types of photoreceptors. The receptors are divided into two classes: rods and cones. The rods 
cover for the photoreceptor type used during limited lighting conditions whereas cones cover 
for three photo receptor types used during abundant lighting conditions [15]. The rods are 
located away from the central points of the retina while the cones are located densely in the 
center of the retina [24]. The rod receptors have the same photopigment which means that 
they have the same spectral sensitivity. The three types of cone receptors have different pho-
topigments which means they have different spectral sensitivity. The cone-photoreceptor-
types are: short-wavelength cones (blue light receptors), medium-wavelength cones (green 
light receptors), and long-wavelength cones (red light receptors)[24]. The long- and medium 
wavelength cones are located mainly in the area called the fovea and the short-wavelength 
cones are located mainly in the area around the fovea. The cones in the fovea have a high 
spectral sensitivity which allows for clear sight [15].  
Where the optic nerve connects with the retina it is possible for light to escape and it does 
therefore not reach the retina. This spot is called the blind spot [15]. 
In perimetry, human vision can be categorized as shown in Figure 9 [9], where all transitions 
between regions are smooth. However, this can be simplified by describing the human vision 
according to the ability of form recognition [9]. The form recognition happens within 2° ec-
centricity of the foveal vision, hence the peripheral vision is any region outside this area, see 
Figure 10.  
==PAGE:45==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
22 
 
 
Figure 9: Human vision in perimetry. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 10: Human vision according to functional form recognition. 
 
==PAGE:46==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
23 
 
2.5.3 Chromatic Perception 
The electromagnetic spectrum covers a wide range of radiation types with different wave-
lengths. Only a small collection of these are visible to the human eye and is perceived as colors 
by the human color vision (HCV) [25], see Figure 11.  
 
 
Figure 11: The electromagnetic spectrum [25]. The visible light ranges from ~380 nm – ~780 nm [15] 
 
The HCV is Trichromatic meaning that it is depending on the three-cone photoreceptors of 
the retina, see section 2.5.2. The cone photoreceptors have different peak points in spectral 
sensitivity based on which photopigment they contain. The short-wavelength cones peak at 
a wavelength of ~ 450 nm, the medium-wavelength cones peak at a wavelength of ~ 540 nm, 
and the long-wavelength cones peak at a wavelength of ~ 560 nm. However, the spread of 
wavelengths that influence the spectral sensitivity of each photoreceptor type overlaps, see 
Figure 12 [15]. 
 
Figure 12: The relative spectral sensitivity of cone photoreceptors [15]. 
 
The different wavelengths of light entering the eye are examined by the cone photoreceptors. 
Depending on the photoreceptor type and its spectral sensitivity the reaction to a certain 
==PAGE:47==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
24 
 
wavelength will be strong or weak. The more wavelengths received and the higher photore-
ceptor sensitivity, the more neural capacity is taken up of the visual system [15]. 
Complementing the Trichromatic theory of the vision is the Opponent-Process theory. To un-
derstand this theory, it is a benefit to understand the term visual illusion [26]. The visual illu-
sion occurs when the brain is tricked to draw certain conclusions based on knowledge and 
past experience rather than the current perceived information, also known as stimulus error 
[26]. An example of this is provided in Figure 13. Staring at the cross in the center of the flag 
for 30 seconds whereafter the gaze point is moved to the right cross in the blank space, a 
visual illusion of color afterimage occurs. The color afterimage occurring is a greenish flag. 
The blank space is white and therefore it reflects all wavelengths perceived as colors by the 
HCV. Since the photoreceptors have produced a lot of signals according to the wavelength 
presenting red, at the point in time where the gaze point is shifted, the photoreceptors will 
not be as sensitive to this wavelength due to adaption. Therefore, the total amount of signals 
received in the brain will lack signals presenting the red color, which creates the illusion of its 
complementary color [26].  
 
Figure 13: Visual illusion on Dannebrog. Stare 30 sec on the left cross and move your gaze to the right cross. This will create 
a vissual illusion of the complementary color (green). 
Based on this the Opponent-Process theory has been created where the output of the cone 
photoreceptors can be divided into three categories. One non-opponent achromatic system 
and two opponent chromatic systems, see Figure 14.  
==PAGE:48==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
25 
 
 
Figure 14: Overview of the human color system that is divided into one achromatic channel and two chromatic channels. 
At the top of Figure 14 is the achromatic system visualized and shows how the achromatic 
channel read inputs from medium- and long wavelength cones.  Secondly is the first of the 
chromatic systems shown. The blue-yellow channel generates the difference between the in-
put from short-wavelength cones and the total sum of input from medium- and long-wave-
length cones. Lastly, is the second chromatic system presented. The red-green channel gen-
erates the difference between the input from medium wavelength cones and the total sum 
of input from short- and long wavelength cones  [15]. It is because of this specific theory color 
contrast and afterimages exist. Chromatic adaptation is therefore important for how a setting 
is perceived, where it can be considered substantial after 15-20 seconds and completed after 
2 minutes [27]. 
Based on the above descriptions color does in other words emerge from the combination of 
wavelengths in light entering the eye. The more light that enters the eye the brighter a specific 
color is perceived and vice versa. 
==PAGE:49==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
26 
 
The CIE International Commissioning on Illuminance has created a method for quantification 
of color called CIE Colorimetry which is recognized internationally [15]. The method does not 
depict colors perceived by the human eye, but it is rather a numerical method for depicting 
colors mathematically. This is because color is a subjective measure which is perceived differ-
ently depending on the individual. CIE Colorimetry is based on color matching that is the 
mathematical ability to create a color based on three color codes [15]. This is possible by the 
use of CIE Color Matching Functions. The CIE Color Matching Functions constitutes three func-
tions representing respectively the long-, medium-, and short wavelengths of light, x(λ), y(λ), 
and z(λ) [15]. Based on the principles behind these functions a CIE Chromaticity Diagram has 
been developed which makes it possible to define a color based on two coordinates or a series 
of color based on three or more coordinates.  
 
2.5.4 Achromatic Perception 
Brightness is a term used to describe a measure of the human spectral sensitivity of light [15]. 
The brightness is higher the more light there is emitted in a certain setting, and it reflects the 
overall illumination. The luminous contrast of a setting describes the difference between the 
brightest and the dimmest regions. The contrast between a specific target and its background 
has great importance to how visible the target is perceived by the HVS. The higher contrast 
the more visible it is perceived  [15]. In Figure 15 the effect of brightness and contrast is visu-
alized. 
 
 
==PAGE:50==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
27 
 
Figure 15: A visualization of the effect of decrease and increase in both contrast and complexity. The images are edited in 
PowerPoint.  
The luminous environment and the perceived environment is connected but it can be influ-
enced by different factors [15]. In the sections above the stimulus of the HVS and its relation 
to both photometry and chromaticity is described. In the following a few examples of factors 
that influence exactly this is described, to specify human perception of an environment.  
The complexity of a setting affects the perceived environment, where the perception of a 
target object is influenced by its surroundings. An example of this is the effect of simultaneous 
brightness contrast given in Figure 16, where the gray object is perceived lighter with a black 
background and darker with a white background [28][15]. 
==PAGE:51==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
28 
 
 
Figure 16: Perception depend on complexity of a setting. A target object is perceived different depending on its surroundings. 
In this figure the gray object is perceived lighter compared to a black background and darker compared to a white background  
[28][15].  
Another factor influencing human perception is the adaption of the HVS which are defined in 
multiple stages. In the mesopic state (state of medium lit conditions) the system is less able 
to discriminate colors compared to when it is in the photopic state (state of well-lit condi-
tions), and in the scotopic state (state of low light conditions) the system is not able to recog-
nize color at all. Furthermore, the sensibility to light changes along with the state of adapta-
tion. This becomes apparent when comparing a lit light source by night and a lit light source 
by day, due to the different levels of adaption to either dim or bright surroundings, where the 
level of perceived brightness of the light changes [15].  
The human perception is capable of recognizing and assessing lighter and darker areas from 
each other with ease in a visual field, however, it has little to no ability to assess gradual 
changes for overall levels of light. For example, the average luminance levels change all 
through the day, but the brightness is perceived as constant. This is due to the light adaptation 
of the HVS [29]. A focus on contrast rather than overall brightness is favored in this study 
since the HVS is significantly more sensitive to variations in ratios of luminance levels rather 
than the absolute luminance levels. 
==PAGE:52==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
29 
 
The human perception can easily assess differences in minor luminance levels between ob-
jects adjacent or close to each other, but the further the objects are from each other the more 
difficult it is to assess this difference in luminance levels. This factor of the human perception 
has more significance the smaller the details are in the luminous environment since larger 
light patterns have shown to be easier differentiable from each other [29].  
Mantiuk R. et al. explains that this is due to the narrow visual field of the foveal region, 2°, 
where the finer details can be observed and that the parafoveal vision, 180°, cannot process 
high-frequency information  [29]. Therefore, assessing detailed differences in luminance lev-
els that is outside the foveal vision is rather difficult.   
Many more factors are affecting human perception than described above, however the fac-
tors presented offer an insight into how complex the visual system is. 
 
2.6 Visual System Assessment 
To account for the complexity of the HVS and human perception, assessment methods have 
been developed to assess a perceived scene or image through photometric values. The as-
sessment methods described in the following sections covers contrast and visual complexity. 
 
2.6.1 Contrast 
There have been developed several contrast measures based on two different approaches: 
The global contrast and the local contrast [30].  
The global contrast measure is the classic approach, where two single points of extreme 
brightness or darkness, ranging from the maximum luminance, Lmax, to minimum luminance, 
Lmin, on a picture is compared to determine the measure of contrast [30] [31].  
Michelson formula was the first measure of global contrast, which only accommodated the 
deviations of luminance. While more recently the measure of the perceived contrast was de-
veloped, which used the standard deviation of image chroma, lightness and high-passed light-
ness [30].  
==PAGE:53==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
30 
 
The measure of global contrast has some disadvantages, mainly its limitation when comparing 
multiple images with varying composition, where the measure cannot predict perceived con-
trast effectively. These limitations led to the development of local contrast measures [31][30].  
The local contrast measure has been developed in multiple different ways, but it mainly quan-
tifies the effect of composition in contrasting areas. Meaning that the contrast of the entire 
image is considered [14][31][30].  
Rizzi et al. have in 2004 developed a method called RAMMG for the measure of achromatic 
local contrast and in 2008 they further developed the method to accommodate color contrast 
by integrating “Tadmor and Tolhurst’s evaluation of a color stimulus.” [3]. 
RAMMG is a multi-level pyramid subsampling approach for analyzing the contrast of digital 
images, where each level has half the resolution of the previous level. A multi-level approach 
has shown to be a crucial feature when trying to mimic the human visual system (HVS) 
[30][32]. Furthermore, a study done by S. Rockcastle and M. Andersen, where global and local 
contrast measures on High Dynamic Range (HDR) renderings were compared with subjective 
user assessment, has shown that local measures predict perceived contrast more reliably than 
global measures. The local measure with the highest correlation with the subjective user as-
sessment was RAMMG [33]. In Figure 17 the procedure of calculating contrast through 
RAMMG is precented followed by a description of the underlying formulars. 
 
 
==PAGE:54==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
31

2.6.2 Visual Complexity 
Studies comparing simple perceptions of brightness and color appearance with subjective as-
sessments to determine the effect of lighting conditions on perception have been criticized 
because of the abstract conditions the subjects were in. These abstract conditions were used 
because of an overfocusing on simple perceptions, where the higher-order perceptions such 
==PAGE:57==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
34 
 
as complexity and interest have shown more accurate for the exploration of the impact of 
lighting conditions [15][34]. Heaps and Handel in 1999 [35] defines the complexity of an image 
as the difficulty of verbally describing the content of the image and later A. Oliva in 2004 
describes visual complexity as not represented by a singular factor but a combination of per-
ceptual aspects such as: “Quantity of objects, clutter, openness, symmetry, organization, and 
variety of colors.” [36]. If the sharpness of an image is increased, more objects will become 
visible making the image significantly more difficult to describe in its entirety. Thereby making 
the sharpness and visibility of an object in a 3D space an adequate measure for assessing 
‘visual complexity’. The study conducted by Zaikina et al. [37] investigated the correlation 
between shapes and detail visibility of an object in a 3D space and a variety of luminance-
based measures to propose and test quantification methods that better predict human per-
ception. Three of the luminance-based measures had a promising correlation with the sub-
jective assessment of the shape and detail distinctness of the 3D object. The measures were: 
“The mean luminance of the object, The standard deviation of the luminances of the object, 
and luminance ratio between an object and its background” [37]. 
In the case of validating at sharpness and viability of the view from a viewpoint, the complex 
stimulus from the office, the shading, and the view itself must be validated in its entirety. 
Therefore, “the object” of validation with the luminance-based measures will be everything 
visible from the viewpoint. From the study by Zaikina et al. [37], the standard deviation seems 
to be the most promising of the three luminance-based measures, since it reflects the varia-
bility of luminances where the mean luminances just defines if the “object” is bright and it 
has a high consistency with the survey results for all the tested variables [37].   
The standard deviation of the pixel luminances of an image is calculated from the RMS equa-
tion proposed by Pavel, et al. in 1987 [4], which has been considered a global contrast meas-
ure [33][31]. By studying both the luminance channels and the chromatic channels gives the 
possibility to investigate the effect of chromaticity on the human’ perception of visual com-
plexity. Therefore, the information gathered from all pixels in this section will follow the equa-
tion (2.5), however when the achromatic visual complexity is calculated, the weighting values 
are 𝛼, 𝛽, 𝛾= 1, 0, 0. In Figure 18 the procedure of calculating complexity through RMS is 
precented along with the underlying formulars. 
 
==PAGE:58==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
35

2.7 Virtual Reality 
The main barrier when conducting experimental studies on user’s experience in luminous 
conditions is the difficulties in controlling the many variables. Through different studies, it has 
been identified that simulated scenes shown on two-dimensional images are sufficient as a 
medium for investigations of the subjective user assessment on architectural spaces and their 
lighting conditions. However, the importance of immersion has been identified by investiga-
tions showing that interactive panoramic view modes are more perceptually accurate [34]. 
Immersion is the sense of “presence” or “being there”, which affect the user’s awareness of 
the real world. Meaning that the immersion effected subject believes that they are a part of 
the immersive media and they forget about their surroundings. The use of virtual reality has 
shown effective as immersive media, since a variety of physiological and emotional reactions 
can be simulated and identified on the subjects [38][34]. However, indications also show that 
in a virtual environment subjects may develop unfavorable physiological reactions such as 
dizziness or discomfort. These reactions may affect the emotions of the subject altering their 
perceived presence [34].  This point is particularly important when conducting user assess-
ments on subjects experiencing virtual reality, where it will significantly impact the results of 
the experiment.  
A study by K. Chamilothori et al. in 2019 investigated the subjective assessments of daylit 
spaces comparing a real and virtual environment. The purpose of this study was to test the 
validity of a virtual environment as a substitute for real environments. The experiment 
showed promising results for “perceptual accuracy, adverse physiological effects, and per-
ceived presence” [34] and the authors concluded that virtual reality might be an adequate    
substitute for real daylit environments when conducting subjective assessments. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:60==
BACKGROUND – CHAPTER 2 
37 
 
2.7.1 HDR Images and Simulations  
The human eye has evolved to the difference between a weight dynamic luminance range of 
10,000:1 [cd/m2]. High dynamic range (HDR) images obtain a much larger dynamic luminance 
range than non-HDR images known as LDR images. This makes HDR images much better 
suited for mimicking human perception [39].  
High dynamic range (HDR) images have proven accurate for luminance examination as a pri-
mary data source. HDR images have been studied on their capability and precision in captur-
ing the luminance values and they have been compared against physical instrumented to pre-
dict their precision for measurements made on chromatic and achromatic targets [40]. These 
studies have proven that HDR images are relatively reliable as a measurement tool for the 
chromaticity and luminance channels.  
Through an analysis of the photographed and simulated HDR images comparing their lumi-
nance data on a weighted variety of luminance-based metrics, made by Zaikina et al. [40]. The 
simulations were carried out using Radiance which is a program capable of creating highly 
accurate lighting simulations and realistic renderings meant to aid researchers, architects, and 
lighting designers. The investigation concluded that there was a mean relative error of 14.78% 
between the two media. A higher precision on the scene recreation for the simulations and 
better rendering quality might very well decrease this mean relative error [40].  
A relative error of 15% is not a substantial issue for the work done in this thesis, however, the 
error should be considered during the analysis of simulated HDR images, when using similar 
simulation programs. 
 
 
==PAGE:61==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
38 
 
3 METHODOLOGY 
In the following, all seps and methodologies conducted in this thesis is presented. As a first 
thing, two pilot studies are presented since they make up the basis of the cause of actions 
taken in the methodology section. 
 
3.1 PILOT STUDIES 
The backbone of this study is two previous conducted theses projects. These projects address 
the topic ‘View Out’ based on VR user assessments from two different angles. The first project 
(Pilot 1) is the thesis “A Parametric Study on Façade Geometries for Optimized View Access Using Advanced 
Rendering Technique” conducted by Jiaqi Xu [14]. The second project (Pilot 2) is the thesis “Explor-
ing Combined Effects of Visual and Audio Environment in Educational Buildings Using Virtual Reality” con-
ducted by Maria Christina Prokou and Maria Stefaniotou [24]. Since those theses are the basis 
of this study, an overview of Pilot 1 and Pilot 2 is presented. 
 
3.1.1 Pilot 1 
The main goal of Pilot 1 is described as follows [14]: 
” The main goal is to establish a workflow of ‘view-out’ quantifying method which include 
knowledge from visual connectivity, pixel-based image processing and environmental psychol-
ogy.” 
The tools used in the study conducted is: Unity creating Virtual Reality (VR), Rhino, Diva for 
Rhino, Grasshopper for Rhino, Ladybug for Grasshopper, Radiance, Python and MATLAB. 
The methodology of the study is divided into the two categories ‘quantification methods’ and 
‘user assessment’. The quantification methods are consisting of four subcategories: View 
quality rating, Visual connectivity assessment, contrast, and visual complexity assessment.  
The view quality rating is a scoring system. The scoring system rates the content of the view 
based on 7 parameters, listed in Figure 19. The visual connectivity assessment determines the 
==PAGE:62==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
39 
 
connection between the occupants’ position (the grid points of the occupant) and the out-
door view (points of the outdoor view), shown in Figure 19. This relation is described by the 
visibility angle. From the visibility angle the visibility percentage can be calculated (visible an-
gle/360° horizontal view). Contrast covers for both local Chromatic and Achromatic contrast 
using the RAMMG method. The visual complexity assessment describes the amount of infor-
mation and density of a scene and covers for both Chromatic and Achromatic standard devi-
ation using the RAMMG method [14]. 
 
The second methodology is the user assessment study. For the user assessment study, ques-
tionnaires were answered by subjects’ pre-experiment (demographic information collecting), 
In-experiment (scene rating) and post-experiment (feedback collection).  The user assessment 
was conducted during an experimental setup with 14 architectural office participants. VR 
scenes were created for an office room in both the Detroit Monroe Blocks and in the Malmø 
Live representing respectively a high- and a low-rise building background. For both buildings, 
13 view scenarios were set up with a variation in different parameters regarding shading con-
ditions: default types, rotation of vertical façade elements, the distance between elements, 
horizontal blind angles, and the use of colored glazing. An overview of the experimental set-
tings is shown in Figure 20.  
Figure 19: Description of the four quantification methods used in Pilot reference project 1: View quality rating, visual con-
nectivity, contrast, and visual complexity assessment.   
Center Image - Author: Jiaqi Xu 
==PAGE:63==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
40 
 
 
From the experiments the following results were found: 
• Facades with the least obstructions are preferred. 
• Tinted glass with a warm-color tone showed higher preferences of all conditions. 
• There is a tendency for high-rise modern architectonic views to be preferred over low-
rise city landscapes.  
• Quantification of the scenes based on view quality as well as view connectivity has a 
strong correlation with positive subjective assessments. 
• Contrast and visual complexity methods did not show strong correlations. 
 
Based on feedback from subjects and experience from the experiment, focus areas for further 
work is described. The room setup for this study contained only a chair, and in further work, 
more realistic interior settings are suggested. This along with more detailed exterior settings. 
To achieve a greater sense of reality during the VR sessions better rendering quality is re-
quired. Furthermore, the subjects participating in the user assessment all had an architectural 
background. To avoid bias a more diverse group of test persons should be collected. At last, 
a script should be developed in Unity for scene switch and automatic result recording to 
achieve a smoother experiment experience.  
Figure 20: Experiment settings for Pilot 1. 13 different scenarios and two different building scenes results in 26 scenes in total.

Malmø Live, Image Rights: Photographer Adam Mørk.

3.1.2 Pilot 2 
The main goal of Pilot 2 is described as follows [24]: 
” The overall objective of this thesis purpose-built experiment that would examine the visual 
contrast, the visual complexity and the satisfaction with the available amount of view. Fur-
thermore, it was desired to examine the perception of sound and more precisely reverberation, 
noise and listening effort in relation to the acoustic parameters and the visual scenes.” 
The tools used in the study conducted is divided into four categories which are – general tools, 
visual environment tools, Audio Simulation tools and Virtual Reality tools. The tools used is: 
• General: MATLAB 
• Visual environment: Revit, Radiance, Rhino, and Luminance 
• Audio simulations: Audacity and Odeon 
• Virtual Reality: Unity 
Pilot 2 investigates the combined effects of the visual and audio environment but since only 
the visual environment is relevant to this study, methodology, results, and further work re-
garding this topic is only stated. 
 
The methodology of the study is divided into the two categories ‘quantification methods’ and 
‘user assessment’. The quantification methods are consisting of the four subcategories: Con-
trast, and visual complexity assessment. 
Like for Pilot 1, Pilot 2 investigated the contrast and visual complexity using the same proce-
dure. Contrast covers for both local Chromatic and Achromatic contrast using the RAMMG 
method. The visual complexity assessment describes the amount of information and density 
of a scene and covers for both Chromatic and Achromatic standard deviation using the 
RAMMG method [24]. 
The second methodology is the user assessment study. For the user assessment study, ques-
tionnaires were answered by subjects’ pre-experiment (demographic information collecting), 
during the experiment (perception on amount of view, perception of visual scenes based on 
==PAGE:65==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
42 
 
acoustics etc.) and after the experiment (feedback collection).  The user assessment was con-
ducted during an experimental setup with 15 participants from different backgrounds. After 
carefully selecting auditorium 54 on the 1. floor of B208 was selected for experiment conduc-
tion. 10 different visual environments were set up and in combination with the audio envi-
ronment a total of 20 combinations were created. The subjects were placed both on the front 
row of the auditorium and on the third row from the back of the auditorium. With 2 repeti-
tions the experiment consisted of 80 different scenarios. An overview of the experimental 
settings is shown in Figure 21. 
From the experiments the following results were found: 
• People prefer higher levels of contrast and complexity, as well as positions on the back 
of the room with better access to view out. 
• There is no significant correlation between the results for chromatic and achromatic 
contrast calculated by algorithms and participants’ responses.  
• People are annoyed by blocked view out and low levels of complexity.  
• Higher levels of complexity are more desired. 
• Contrast and visual complexity methods did not show strong correlations. 
 
Based on feedback from subjects and experience from the experiment, focus areas for further 
work is described. Since the experiment consisted of a lot of repetitions and since the duration 
of the experiment was very long the subjects were challenged to stay focused. Therefore, a 
shorter duration time of the experiment is recommended. Only 15 participants were collected 
and to get stronger results an increased sample size is preferable.  Furthermore, to achieve a 
greater sense of reality during the VR sessions better rendering quality is required. At last, it 
is suggested that subjects get comfortable with the format of the experiment before it starts, 
and therefore tests introducing the format should be shown in VR before the actual experi-
ment. 
==PAGE:66==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
43 
 
 
   
 
 
Figure 21: Experiment settings for Pilot 2. In the bottom a panoramic picture from the interior of the auditorium is shown 
and above the 1. floor plan of B208 where auditorium 54 is outlined. The experiment was conducted for 10 different visual 
environments which in combination with the audio environment, the subject position and repetitions end up at different 80 
scenarios.

3.2 The Experiment Setup 
In this thesis a subjective user assessment study is conducted. A short introduction is provided 
explaining the basics of experimental research, followed by an introduction to the experi-
mental setup of this study. After this, descriptions of background analysis and decisions made 
to set up the experiments are provided. 
 
3.2.1 Experimental Research 
Experimental research is described as “an arranged intervention in the lives of individuals in 
order to assess its impact on them” [41]. In other words, the experimental research aims to 
identify a cause-effect relationship where the cause is characterized by the independent var-
iable (the intervention), and the effect is characterized by the dependent variable (subject 
responses). As a part of characterizing view out, three experiments have been set up to in-
vestigate the effect of different features in a view with a focus on solar shading systems. When 
experimenting, it is important to secure that the circumstances around the experiment are 
presented to the subjects with the right level of detail. This is to make sure that the focus area 
of the subjects is not influenced before the experiment, because it could lead them to disre-
gard their original impressions of the scenes. Therefore, the three experiments described in 
the following sections have been presented to the subjects as one continuous VR experiment 
with scenes displayed in random order. In the following sections, a short introduction to each 
experiment is provided, along with its related independent-, confounding-, and dependent 
variables.  
 
3.2.2 Experiment 1 
The first experiment is built around the independent variables (i.v.) shading conditions, view 
quality, & luminance contrast and the confounding variables color contrast, achromatic com-
plexity, & chromatic complexity. In the first experiment two levels of view quality & lumi-
nance contrast will be investigated, whereas five levels of shading conditions are assessed.  
==PAGE:68==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
45 
 
This experiment aims to detect differences and associations between the dependent variable 
(d.v.) subjective responses and the independent- and confounding variables. Four general hy-
potheses are set up for the first experiment which will be investigated through hypothesis 
testing in section 4.2.1 of the report. The general hypotheses are listed below. 
 
Throughout the sections in the methodology chapter each step towards creating a VR exper-
iment is presented. In the following sections the background for selection of each level as-
sessed for the i.v. of experiment one is described. Hence, a final overview of the experiment 
is presented in section 3.8.1. 
 
3.2.3 Experiment 2 
The second experiment is built around the i.v. shading condition & amount of view. This is 
with two levels of shading conditions and four levels of amount of view.  
This experiment aims to detect associations between the dependent variable (d.v.) subjective 
responses and the i.v. One general hypothesis is set up for the second experiment which will 
be investigated through hypothesis testing in section 4.2.2 of the report. The hypothesis is 
shown below. 
Hypothesis 1.1 
”Contrast affects people’s perception of view”

The third experiment is built around the i.v. greenery conditions, where four different levels 
are assessed. Greenery increases the view quality, and the third experiment is based on this, 
set up to investigate the potential of greenery as shading substituting natural green in the 
view. One general hypothesis is set up for the third experiment which will be investigated 
through hypothesis testing in section 4.2.3 of the report. The general hypothesis is shown 
below.

tion 3.8.3.

3.2.5 Shading Types 
The solar shading types investigated in the experiments were selected based on the process 
described in the following section.   
It is common practice that external shading is selected for office buildings [42], because it 
provides the best shading factor [43]. Therefore, only external shading was considered for 
selection in this thesis.  
In SBi 264 the most common shading types are listed in Table 19 [43]: blinds, horizontal slats, 
moveable shutters with slats, screens, and awnings. Awnings are a shading type usually se-
lected for ground floor shop windows and is therefore not included in this thesis. However, 
the remaining shading types underlie the inclusion of big blinds, small blinds, screens, and 
shutters with greenery. As shown in Appendix 10.12 it is possible with those four shading 
types to investigate all classes of view out according to the Danish Industry Guide for Indoor 
Climate [44].  
The facade elements investigated in 3.1.1 Pilot 1 represents modern architecture where shad-
ing is incorporated in the façade geometry as a design choice. Shading from façade geometry 
exist in many shapes and sizes, but in this thesis, they are represented by movable vertical 
louvres. Furthermore, vertical louvres are included to also investigate the effect different 
types of vertical shading has on the perceived view. 
The five solar shading types selected is visualized in Appendix 10.13. 
For the sake of clarity and to make it easy to compare the shading types, all shadings are 
coated with white color. The specific shading properties is clarified in section 3.3.5. The sizes 
and shapes of each shading type investigated is described in section 3.3.2. 
 
 
==PAGE:71==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
48 
 
3.2.6 View Quality 
To ensure that the independent variable of view quality has a significant effect, two extremes 
of the view quality rating explained in section 2.2 has been chosen for the two views. The only 
restriction for the views is that they would make sense for an office building, which was de-
fined as a “build view”. Through a search on google maps, two locations were selected: Mar-
morbyen towards Sandkaj at Nordhavn and Marstalsgade at Østerbro.    
The view at Nordhavn with a high view quality rating had to contain content far away, which 
introduced the possibility of sky mapping explained in section 3.3.3, and content in form of 
trees close up, which had to be able to be removed due to the scene variations of Experiment 
3. Therefore, the view at Nordhavn would have two view quality scores, one with and one

3.2.7 Luminance Contrast 
Luminance contrast is in this thesis investigated based on two different date and time set-
tings. To gain the greatest effect the time and date selected should reflect the highest and 
the lowest possible luminance contrast. In the selection process 13th of Jan, 28th of Feb, 14th 
of Apr, and 30th of May were considered within the occupied hours 08:00 – 16:00 because of 
their representativeness of the daylight hours over a year [45]. The timespan is selected based 
on a standard 45 h working week and the timeslot is chosen to make sure that the scenes 
displayed in the final VR experiment, do not get too dark.  
Conducting a VR experiment there are certain limitations compared to real-life studies. One 
of them is that the equipment has an upper boundary for displayable light intensities. In this 
study an HTC VIVE PRO EYE headset is used, see section 3.9.3, and brightness up to 133 cd/m2 
can be displayed [46]. Because of this, scenarios with glare sources should be avoided in the 
experiment. In scenes with no shading conditions glare could potentially occur and therefore 
one of these scenes are used to conduct a glare analysis. The tool used to carry out the anal-
ysis is the radiance-based tool Evalglare. The code used is described in Appendix 10.10.9 and 
the results are presented in Appendix 10.14.  
According to “A Sensitivity Analysis on the Effect of BSDF Resolutions for Solar Shading Devices 
Coupled with Practical Measurements” by M. Pedersen and F. Rasmussen the daylight glare 
comfort can be described as “Good class. 95% of the office time the glare is weaker than 
‘perceptible’” when the Discomfort Glare Probability (DGP) limit is ≤ 0.40 [47]. Based on this 
the limit for when a scene is perceived as glary is determined to a DGP limit of 0.40, see Figure 
23.  
The scenes 10:00 o’clock, 12:00 o’clock and 14:00 o’clock in January and February are per-
ceived as glary. Apart from this only 12:00 o’clock in April is perceived as glary. Because of 
this and because the experiment is conducted in May, the dates selected for further investi-
gation is 14th of Apr and 30th of May.  
 
==PAGE:75==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
52 
 
 
Figure 23: Selected instance representing daylight hours over the year. Time and dates with glare issues is marked with  
 
and times and dates with no glare issues is marked with 
[45]. 
 
The final selection of time is decided based on the four parameters: contras level, vertical 
illuminance, average luminance of glare sources, and DGP.  
The vertical illuminance, average luminance of glare sources, and DGP are found through the 
Evalglare analysis where the contrast is determined based on an individual contrast calcula-
tion. The procedure of calculating contrast is described in section 2.6.1 and the MATLAB script 
used to conduct this listed in Appendix 10.10.1.

3.2.8 Amount of View 
Shading used for office buildings is often automatic and gets activated when direct sunlight 
reaches the façade [42]. Office-shading is therefore often movable and shifts position 
throughout the day. As described in section 3.2.3 it is desired to investigate the effect of 
changes in the available amount of view at different shading conditions. The shading condi-
tions selected for this investigation is Big Blinds and Vertical Louvres since their slat geometry 
and measures are the same, see  3.3.2 Shading Geometry.  
==PAGE:77==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
54
