3.2.9 Location in the Room 
The viewpoint is located 5 m from the window. According to “The Building Regulations 
guidelines on corrections according to the 10-pct. rule for daylight” written by the Danish 
Transport, construction and housing Authority, a big contrast will often appear between ar-
eas close to the window and in the back of the room when the room depth exceeds 5-6 m 
[49].  Though this is also depending on the room width, room height, and interior design, 
the depth of the work area is determined to 5 m. The viewpoint is located in the back of the 
work area, so the subject gets the best chances of forming an overall impression of the 
room, the view, and the shading properties.  
==PAGE:80==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
57 
 
The viewpoint is positioned central according to the width of the room and the height is set 
to 1.2 m which corresponds to the average eye level of a seated adult. Additional infor-
mation on viewpoint height is found in section 3.9.4. 
 
3.3 Modelling the Virtual Environment 
The virtual environment is modelled in Rhinoceros version 6 SR34. All objects are structured 
using the organizational tool “Laryers” which makes it easy to switch between scene settings 
and extract scene geometries.  Scene geometries are extracted using the Rhino plug-in DIVA-
for-Rhino v 4.1.0.11. by running a daylight visualization where a geometry.rad file is automat-
ically created.  
 
3.3.1 Room Geometry 
The room is modelled with a width of 15 m, a depth of 7 m, and a height of 2.7 m, as shown 
in Figure 27. The room is facing south, and the window fills out the entire façade area, corre-
sponding to the dimensions 15 m x 2.7 m. The frame of the window is only symbolic and not 
realistic since its only purpose is to create the perceptual impression of a frame. Hence the 
glazing makes up 91% of the window. The frame is reduced, and mullions are excluded so 
they do not intervene with the effect of different shading types on the perceptual impression. 
The floor of the room is located 4.5 m above ground, see Figure 25. This level corresponds to 
the first floor where extra height is added to the ground floor which is not unusual for build-
ings in Copenhagen. 
==PAGE:81==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
58 
 
 
Figure 25: Level of room above ground, room height and façade height. 
Creating the room geometry is a balance between keeping it as simple as possible and includ-
ing the essential key objects. The simpler the scenes are, the lower the computational ren-
dering time gets. Since this project is restricted to certain time constraints, lowering the com-
putational rendering time plays an essential role. However, the more detailed a scene is the 
more realistic the subjects perceptual impression is [50] [51].  
Figure 26: The important balance between modelling simplicity and level of detail. 
==PAGE:82==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
59 
 
The right balance between simplicity and the level of detail is in this study determined to a 
wooden floor and workstations as shown in Figure 27. 
A study reveals that participant satisfaction with brightness is significantly affected by colored 
materials in the room [50]. Both chromatic and achromatic contrast, which plays a key role in 
this thesis, is related to brightness and therefore colors in the room are a crucial part of how 
the participants perceive the room. The satisfaction with brightness and other perceptual im-
pressions are also affected by view access, perceived pleasantness, interest, complexity, and 
overall ambiance [50]. To accommodate the expectation of an office room not only being 
presented by gray-scale color choices, the wooden floor is selected. The procedure of model-
ling the floor is described in section 3.3.5. 
 
Figure 27: Level of detail included in the room geometry and room specifications. 
 
==PAGE:83==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
60 
 
Eight office workstations have been implemented in the scenes, to create a more realistic 
office space. The number of workstations has been carefully selected based on test render-
ings, where eight workstations resulted in acceptable computational time. The furniture is 
modelled according to standard measurements, and they are located so they do not disturb 
the view angle of the windows, see Appendix 10.5. The inclusion of furniture has both a direct 
and an indirect effect. The direct effect is an increased level of scene-complexity, whereas the 
indirect effect is an increased ability among subjects to immerse in the virtual office space 
[51]. Immersion is an important aspect when conducting VR experiments since it has a great 
impact on how realistic the results get. The more the subjects feel like being present in the 
virtual space, the more the results reflect reality.  
 
3.3.2 Shading Geometry 
When creating shading geometry, the foveal region should be considered, see section 2.5.2. 
The distance from the viewpoint to the façade is 5 m, hence the diameter of the foveal vision 
is ~ 0.17 m at the location of the shading, see Figure 28. 
 
Figure 28: Diameter of foveal vision at the window.  
The foveal vision is considered when determining the distance between shading elements of 
Vertical Louvres and Big Blinds. For Small Blinds and Screens standard distances between slats 
and openness factors are normally so small that the foveal vision gets interrupted no matter 
the means.  
==PAGE:84==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
61 
 
Small Blinds  
The geometry of the Small Blinds is created based on the Win2Trade product C80 [52]. This 
shading is as a standard located right in front of the glazing area which is why it is modelled 
as an incorporated part of the window frame. The measures are described in Figure 29. 
 
Figure 29: Measures of Small Blind geometry, based on the Win2Trade product C80 [52]. 
 
Big Blinds  
The geometries of the Big Blinds are created as movable slats over a structural profile. The 
product is produced by Giménez Ganga and the slat type is O-300 [53]. The shading geometry 
measures are visualized in Figure 30 and Figure 31. Apart from the distance between slats 
being selected based on technical data of an existing product, it is also selected based on 
interruption of the foveal vision. Hence the Big Blinds are modelled with a distance between 
slats great enough for minimum one view direction with no interruption in the foveal visual 
field, see Figure 32. 
Figure 30: Measures of Big Blind slat and profile geometry, based on the Giménez Ganga product O-300 [53].

Vertical Louvres 
Vertical Louvres has the same slat constellation as modelled for Big Blinds, however the slats 
are vertical instead of horizontal, see Figure 33. This means that Big Blinds and Vertical Lou-
vres are identical in terms of slat size, slat geometry, and distance between slats. The product 
used is also the Giménez Ganga and the slat type is O-300 [53]. The distance between the 
slats is again modelled great enough for minimum one view direction with no interruption in 
the foveal visual field, see Figure 34. 
 
Figure 33: Measures of Vertical Louvres geometry, based on the Giménez Ganga product O-300 [53].

Screens 
To assign the screens the right properties two layers of material are needed. The first layer 
facing the window is the transmission layer whereas the layer facing the surroundings is the 
fabric layer, see Figure 35. In section 3.3.5 material properties are defined. 
 
Figure 35: Screen geometry setup. 
A walk-through on how a screen (described as roller blind) is modelled, is set up by Mandana 
Sarey Khanie and attached in Appendix 10.18. 
 
Natural green 
For the third experiment shutters consisting of natural green is investigated. This type of shad-
ing does not yet exist on the market, but since views with natural green achieve a higher 
quality score than views without[12], it is worth exploring the potential.  
The frame is created from (0.2 m x 2.7 m) slats, with a distance between slats from center 
point to center point at 0.5 m for the high-density shading and 1.0 m for low-density shading. 
See Figure 36. The slat distance of the high-density-shutters is based on the vertical louvres 
where the foveal vision is considered. 
==PAGE:88==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
65 
 
 
Around each slat, a column of natural green is created using the professional landscape soft-
ware Lands Design version 5.4.0.6748 – Rhino 6. The distance between the slats determines 
the density of vegetation on the shading, whereas the density around each slat remains the 
same. 
To create natural green that reflects the perfect randomness of nature, different modelling 
methods have been tested out using the trial-and-error method. Ignoring the randomness of 
the vegetation could result in a reduced perception of reality in VR, and it is therefore im-
portant that the natural green is created to look as random as possible. A walkthrough of how 
the vegetation is created is given in Figure 37 and Figure 38.  
 
Figure 36: Overview of framework for low-and high-density shutters in experiment 3.

3.3.3 View Geometry 
Skymapping  
To construct the high view quality condition a photo of the view content and the sky can be 
projected onto the virtual sky, following the workflow by Jan Wienold and Kynthia Chami-
lothori [54]. However, the view content which is close up would directly influence the light 
distribution in the room, therefore features in the close view must be modelled. This gives 
the requirement to the given photo that it must only contain features that are far away. The 
Panorama photo taken at Nordhavn that lives up to this requirement can be seen in Figure 39 
and was taken on a cloudy day to dampen the shadows of the building in the view, but still 
make it possible to have direct light entering the building from the sun. The image projected 
is taken with a regular camera. To project the image the LDR image is converted to HDR by an 
online free converter. 
 
Figure 39: Panorama photo of Nordhavn taken with an iPhone 6S by Signe Ebsen Olling the 18/03 – 2021 at 11:45.  
The panorama photo must be processed in a variety of ways to make it suitable for simulating 
the real view at Nordhavn. First, the photo had to be scaled such that the image is not 
stretched and distorted when being projected onto the Radiance sky as seen in Figure 40. The 
resulting image height to width ratio was 0.5. 
 
Figure 40: The rendered view projecting the original unscaled photo and the scaled photo onto the Radiance sky. 
==PAGE:92==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
69 
 
The image was then resized to visualize a distance of 150m to the buildings as the real view 
at Nordhavn would have. This can be seen in Figure 41. It was ensured that the final photo 
used for the projection was centered with the horizon in the middle of the photo. The final 
image used for the projection is shown in Figure 42.  
 
 
Figure 41: The rendered view projecting the scaled and resized photo onto the Radiance sky. 
 
 
Figure 42: The final image projected onto the Radiance sky. 
 
==PAGE:93==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
70 
 
The sky mapping approach used in this project deviates somewhat from the workflow by Jan 
Wienold and Kynthia Chamilothori [54], mainly by projecting a panorama onto the sky instead 
of a fish-eye image. Therefore, the vta_fisheye_projection.cal file made by Jan Wienold can-
not be used. Instead, the skymap.cal file provided by Thomas Bleicher is used [55]. The 
skymap.cal file is specifically made for projecting a panorama onto the Radiance sky. This is 
relevant creating the outside_skymapping.rad where the image is first projected onto the Ra-
diance sky and then making the sky a light source. Projecting image onto the Radiance sky 
affects light conditions of the sky. Where the image is darker the luminance will be lowered 
and therefore calibration of the luminance levels on the sky is necessary to ensure the overall 
luminance levels are the same for the skymapped sky as the standard Radiance sky. This is 
done by rendering HDR images for the scene with and without the image projection, meaning 
the average luminance levels and calculating the ratio between the two HDR images. The 
skymapped sky had an average of 1127 cd/m2 and the standard Radiance sky had an average 
of 3677 cd/m2 resulting in a ratio of 3.26. The calculations and measurements can be seen in 
Appendix 10.2. However, then light from the skymapped sky is increased by 3.26, then the 
color of the projected image becomes very saturated. To counteract this the image was de-
saturated by 45% using photoshop before projecting it onto the Radiance sky. This resulted 
in a change of the luminance levels of skymapped sky and therefore now ratio of 3.21 was 
calculated. The new ratio calculations and measurements can also be seen in Appendix 10.2. 
The following shows the functions used in the outside_skymapping.rad to create the glowing 
and image mapped sky: 
==PAGE:94==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
71

When projecting an image onto a surface the image must be scaled with the “-s” value in the 
material.rad file to recreate the real-world dimensions. This can be done by rendering an im-
age containing a reference object with known dimensions and distance from the render point, 
and the image projected surface at a known distance. The ratio between the real-world an-
gular diameters of the reference object and an object with a known height on the projected 
image is calculated and compared with the ratio on the rendered image between the height 
of the reference object and the same object on the projected image. The angular diameters 
are calculated with the following equation: 
𝛿= 2 ∗arctan ( 𝑑
2 ∗𝐷) 
(3.3) 
𝛿 is the angular diameter 
𝐷 is the distance to the object 
d is the actual diameter of the object 
 
The reference object selected is the window of the office room and its angular diameter is 
found to be 30.2o as shown below:  
𝛿= 2 ∗arctan ( 2.7𝑚
2 ∗5𝑚) = 30.2𝑜 
The height of the object on the projected image is more difficult to find. The bricks on the 
image have a known height; however, they are too small to be measured on the rendering. 
The height of the windows on the projected image is unknown, but the bricks are used to 
determine it. The window height is found to be 2.34m, which is relatively high. This height is 
possible, but it might have consequences on how close-up the buildings will be perceived. It 
would have been optimal if the window could have been measured in person, but since that 
was not possible the height of 2.34m is used for the image scaling. The angular diameter of 
the window on the image is found to be 6.4o as shown below: 
𝛿= 2 ∗arctan ( 2.34𝑚
2 ∗21𝑚) = 6.4𝑜 
The ratio between the two angular diameters was found to be 0.21 and the ratio between the 
height of the two windows on the rendering were the -s value had been set to 20 was found 
==PAGE:97==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
74 
 
to be 0.22. Due to time limitations the scaling was agreed to be sufficient. If done more accu-
rate, then the -s value should have been reduced, thereby making the windows be perceived 
as smaller. This inaccuracy could further alter how close-up the buildings will be perceived. 
The rendered image and the ratio calculations can be found in Appendix 10.3. 
 
Trees  
When quantifying the content of a view the presence of natural green results in additional 2 
points to the overall score. To upgrade the view referred to as “the high-quality view” trees 
are modelled and included. Like for the shutters with natural green, the trees are created with 
the Lands Design software version 5.4.0.6748 – Rhino 6. In views that include trees, the trees 
are located according to Figure 45. 
 
Figure 45: Placement of trees in views. The trees are reduced to half their size to reduce computational time. 
To decrease the computational time a few simplifications were made. The density of the trees 
is reduced by 50 % using the command “ReduceMesh”, the leaves and the stem are converted 
to Nurbs, and the crown diameter is reduced to half the size, see Figure 45. The reduction 
accommodates the position and view angle of the subject which means that the trees from 
that exact position seem whole.  
 
==PAGE:98==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
75 
 
3.3.4 Sky Conditions 
The sky and sun conditions are determent from configurations in DIVA. The weather file used 
is from the Design Reference Year, DRY2001-2010. The minority of information needed by 
DIVA is obtained from the weather file, which mainly provides the geographic location of 56o 
northern latitude and 13o Eastern longitude. By running the rendering type visualization and 
specify the time, date and sky conditions in DIVA, a gensky command is generated. The gensky 
command is used to create the sky.rad file needed to specify the sun conditions of the given 
Radiance rendering. Two sky.rad files were generated, one for the 30th of May at 08:00 and 
another for the 14th of April at 14:00. The reason for these times and dates are explained in 
section 3.2.7. The sky condition is defined to be “Clear Sky with Sun (CIE Clear Sky)” for both 
sky.rad files. The sky.rad file for May is shown below: 
 
# gensky 05 30 08 +s -a 56 -o -13 -m -15 
# Local solar time: 7.91 
# Solar altitude and azimuth: 33.7 -78.7 
# Ground ambient level: 13.5

3.3.5 Materials 
Unless anything else is stated, the materials are defined based on default values for surface 
reflectance defined in the Danish Building Regulation 2018 [56]. The default values are listed 
in Table 6 and the entire material.rad file is found in Appendix 10.1.

Table 6: Default values for surface reflectance defined by the Danish Building Regulation 2018. 
As described in section 3.3.1 Room Geometry a wooden floor is incorporated in the room, 
hence the reflectance of the surface deviates from Table 6. Additional material properties as 
furniture, leaves, stem and shading are descripted in the following sections. 
  
Floor Surface 
To ease up the perception of the interior space a wooden floor is created through image pro-
jection on the floor surface. When projecting the floor, it was assumed that all RGB reflections 
occurred based on the reflectance determined in the code. Hence the reflectance was deter-
mined to 0.20 according to the Danish Building Regulation 2018 [56]. The HDR image was 
projected through the following material property code in radiance: 
==PAGE:101==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
78 
 
 
Figure 46: Illustration of the HDR image projected on the floor. The image size is 2000 x 1000 pixels.. 
Image Author: Polantis, Name: Zebrano wood flooring. 
  
However, later it was discovered that the reflectance occurs as a combination of the values 
defined in the code and the chromatic properties of the image projected. Because of this the 
RGB reflections should have been 1 instead of 0.20 to reflect the actual properties of this 
specific wooden floor. This was corrected for all other image projections but the wooden floor 
was accidently overlooked and not corrected. This means that the floor appears wooden (see 
Figure 46) but it has the properties of a dark carpet.

Shading 
As described in 3.2.5 Shading Types all shading types are coated with a white color. The ma-
terial properties are described below. 
 
For screens the physical law of absorption, transmission, and reflection is complied with: 
1 = Absorption + Transmission + Reflection 
The material properties of the screens are created based on the Insolroll product Kona 10% 
White. The datasheet of this product is not directly available on the web browser, and it is 
therefore attached in Appendix 10.17. The screens are modelled with a direct transmission 
at 0.1, a diffuse transmission at 0.06, a cut off angle at 75°, and a diffuse reflection at 0.6.

Glazing 
The window consists of triple pane glazing with a solar coating which is an existing product by 
Pilkington Spectrum [14][57]. The choice of glazing is selected based on the study Pilot 1, since 
it is reasonable for a south-facing room [14]. The chosen window has the light transmittance 
(Tn) of 66%. To assign the glazing material the right properties the transmissivity is needed. 
The most accurate method for setting up the glazing would be modelling each layer of glazing 
separatly instead of combining them into one. This because the fall in visible light transmit-
tance might be underpredicted (when the three panes of glazing are combined) at high inci-
dence angles of the sun [58]. As a simplification the materials are defined and modelled as 
one layer since the properties of the glazing is not defined for each layer separately. The 
transmissivity (tn) has been determined based on the following equation (3.4) [58]: 
𝑡𝑛= √0.8402528435 + 0.0072522239 ∙𝑇𝑛∙𝑇𝑛−0.9166530661 
0.0036261119
𝑇𝑛
 
(3.4) 
tn is the transmissivity 
Tn is the light transmittance 
 
Based on the light transmittance (Tn) at 66% the transmissivity is determined to be 72%. The 
glazing properties of the window is hence as described in the following: 
 
According to DS 17037 one criteria of ensuring an adequate view out is: “the glazing material 
of the view opening should provide a view that is perceived to be clear, undistorted and natu-
rally colored” [10]. Therefore, only neutral-colored glazing is investigated in this thesis. Using 
glazing with warm or cold color tones could influence the subject responses.

3.4 Rendering Workflow 
3.4.1 Resizing Model Geometry  
The geometry file generated by DIVA for Rhino have all measurements in millimeters, but 
Radiance standard measurements are in meters. If the geometry file is used as it is, the com-
putation time would be significantly longer with no benefits to the rendering quality. To re-
scale the geometry file into meters the Radiance command Xform is used as shown below: 
 
 
3.4.2 Creating Octree File 
For each scene, an octree file has been created from the given scene conditions. An octree 
file is a combination of the sky.rad file, the material.rad file, the outside_skymapping.rad and 
the geometry.rad file into a single file, which is necessary for Radiance to create the HDR-
renderings. The octree file is created with the Radiance command oconv shown below: 
 
 
3.4.3 Radiance Renderings 
To create a 360 degree panorama for the use of the virtual reality experience, a cubemap 
projection was chosen mainly on the fact that rendering the 6 HRD-images for the cubemap 
projection would not cause pixel distortion on the images like other 360 degree projections 
[59]. No pixel distortion on the images is important for the accuracy of the contrast and com-
plexity quantification methods further explained in section 3.5. This type of 360 degree pro-
jection requires 6 rendering for each direction, in this case South, East, West, North, Up and 
Down. The 6 HRD-images were rendered using the rpict command from the program Radi-
ance. The rpict command is built on a variant of variables that can be tailored to a given need. 
The variables will mainly influence rendering quality and computation time. The final com-
mand is a combination between multiple sources and recommendations from the radiance 
xform -s 0.001 ./geometry.rad > geometry_scaled.rad 
oconv material.rad sky.rad outside_skymapping.rad geometry_scaled.rad > 
OCT.oct 
==PAGE:108==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
85 
 
website [51][24][14][60][61]. This final command is mainly optimized based on rendering 
quality and therefore the computation time is rather extensive. Computation time for render-
ing a full scene varied based on how complex the geometry of the scene was. The simplest 
scenes would render for around 48 hours, while the most complex scenes would render for 
around 2 weeks. Some effort was done to reducing the computation time, but most attempts 
had unfortunate effects on the rendering quality and therefore was not used. 
 
 
# South 
rpict -t 15 -vtv -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 0 -1 0 -vu 0 0 1 -vh 90 -vv 
90 -ab 4 -ps 2 -pt .05 -pj .9 -dj .9 -ds .15 -dt .05 -dp 512 -st .15 -aa 
.05 -ar 325 -ad 20000 -as 10000 -lr 8 -lw .00001 -x 12800 -y 12800 
OCT.oct > S.hdr 
# East 
rpict -t 15 -vtv -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 1 0 0 -vu 0 0 1 -vh 90 -vv 90 
-ab 4 -ps 2 -pt .05 -pj .9 -dj .9 -ds .15 -dt .05 -dp 512 -st .15 -aa 
.05 -ar 325 -ad 20000 -as 10000 -lr 8 -lw .00001 -x 12800 -y 12800 
OCT.oct > E.hdr 
# West 
rpict -t 15 -vtv -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd -1 0 0 -vu 0 0 1 -vh 90 -vv 
90 -ab 4 -ps 2 -pt .05 -pj .9 -dj .9 -ds .15 -dt .05 -dp 512 -st .15 -aa 
.05 -ar 325 -ad 20000 -as 10000 -lr 8 -lw .00001 -x 12800 -y 12800 
OCT.oct > W.hdr 
# North 
rpict -t 15 -vtv -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 0 1 0 -vu 0 0 1 -vh 90 -vv 90 
-ab 4 -ps 2 -pt .05 -pj .9 -dj .9 -ds .15 -dt .05 -dp 512 -st .15 -aa 
.05 -ar 325 -ad 20000 -as 10000 -lr 8 -lw .00001 -x 12800 -y 12800 
OCT.oct > N.hdr 
# Up 
rpict -t 15 -vtv -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 0 0 1 -vu 0 -1 0 -vh 90 -vv 
90 -ab 4 -ps 2 -pt .05 -pj .9 -dj .9 -ds .15 -dt .05 -dp 512 -st .15 -aa 
.05 -ar 325 -ad 20000 -as 10000 -lr 8 -lw .00001 -x 12800 -y 12800 
OCT.oct > U.hdr 
# Down 
rpict -t 15 -vtv -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 0 0 -1 -vu 0 1 0 -vh 90 -vv 
90 -ab 4 -ps 2 -pt .05 -pj .9 -dj .9 -ds .15 -dt .05 -dp 512 -st .15 -aa 
.05 -ar 325 -ad 20000 -as 10000 -lr 8 -lw .00001 -x 12800 -y 12800 
OCT.oct > D.hdr 
==PAGE:109==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
86 
 
The variables which had the most visible impact on the rendering quality was a low ambient 
accuracy (-aa), a calculated ambient resolution (-ar), high values for ambient divisions (-ad) 
and ambient super-samples (-as) and by rendering the images with a very high resolution and 
then resizing it. [60]  
Ambient accuracy (-aa) and ambient resolution (-ar) have a substantial effect on the fine-scale 
light distribution, however they have an immense impact on the computation time. The am-
bient accuracy (-aa) should be set as low as possible and might even be set to 0. This will 
greatly reduce the visibility for “splotches” from the ambient light. However, to have a rea-
sonable computation time this value has been set to 0.05. The ambient resolution (-ar) can 
be calculated from the following equation [60]: 
 
𝑆𝑚𝑖𝑛= 𝐷𝑚𝑎𝑥∗−𝑎𝑎
−𝑎𝑟
 ⇔−𝑎𝑟= 𝐷𝑚𝑎𝑥∗−𝑎𝑎
𝑆𝑚𝑖𝑛
 
(3.5) 
𝑆𝑚𝑖𝑛 is the minimum separation for cached irradiances. 
𝐷𝑚𝑎𝑥 is the scene dimension. 
-aa is the ambient accuracy 
-ar is the ambient resolution. 
 
This calculation will ensure a maximum ambient resolution to the given model geometry with-
out influencing the rendering quality, meaning a higher ambient resolution will only result in 
a substantial extension of the computation duration. The scene dimension (Dmax) can be 
gained but using the getinfo command on the geometry.rad file. This value will affect the size 
of the ambient resolution, which makes it quite relevant to make the geometry model as small 
as possible. The geometry.rad used in this project has a scene dimension of 65m. The mini-
mum separation for cached irradiances (Smin) is influenced by how detailed the geometry is. 
For the geometry model used in this project the necessary value for Smin is set to 1cm, which 
is a relatively low value for a building. The ambient resolution (-ar) for this project is then 
calculated [60]: 
−𝑎𝑟= 65𝑚∗0.05
0.01𝑚
= 325 
==PAGE:110==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
87 
 
The use of an ambient file was considered to reduce the overall computation time. To inves-
tigate the effect of an ambient file a test was conducted on a scene with no solar shading and 
low view quality. The investigation contained the computation time to create the ambient file 
and the rendered scene using the ambient file compared to the computation time to create 
the rendered scene without the use of the ambient file. Creating the ambient file took 8.8 
hours of computation time, the scene with the ambient file took 3.4 hours for a total of 12.2 
hours. The scene without the ambient file only took 7.3 hours, which is very unusual since 
creating the ambient file should have taken less computation time than rendering the scene 
without the use of an ambient file. Both scenes were rendered on the same computer and 
the processors were not used for anything else. From the results of the investigation and time 
restrictions, it was decided not to use the ambient file for the final rpict command.  
 
3.4.4 Image Combining 
The 6 HRD-images are combined into one image for the main reason that when tone mapping 
the 6 individual images they all have a slightly different tint, which is very noticeable when 
creating the cubemap projection. When tone mapping all 6 HRD-images simultaneously this 
different tint will not occur. Tone mapping will be explant further in section 3.4.6 
The 6 HRD-images are combined using the following pcompos command: 
 
 
Resulting in an HRD-image like this: 
 
Figure 49: The HDR images for the 6 orientations combined into one HDR image. This image has been tone mapped. 
 
pcompos -a -6 D.hdr E.hdr N.hdr S.hdr U.hdr W.hdr > 6_Images.hdr

3.4.5 Oversampling 
Oversampling is a concept where the images are rendered with a very high resolution and 
then scaled down to a more manageable resolution. This has significant benefits and draw-
backs, the image quality is greatly increased since this removes “pixelation” by smoothing 
the harder edges, at the cost of computation time. A Decade of Radiance Tricks by Mark J. 
Stock [61] showed great results with an oversampling of 4 times higher resolution than the 
final image resolution, hence used in this project.  
The HDR-images are rendered in a resolution of 12800x12800 pixels and then scaled to the 
final image resolution of 3200x3200 pixels with the pfilt command shown below: 
 
 
3.4.6 Tone Mapping 
In real life it is common with a dynamic range of 10,000:1 which is the range of the human 
eye [39]. This range is displayed in the form of HDR images. However, display equipment is 
often not capable of displaying in this range, which is also the case for the VR equipment used 
when conducting experiments in this thesis, see section 3.9.3. Therefore, all images displayed 
are LDR images which only covers a display range of 255:1 [62]. Converting the scene images 
pfilt -x /4 -y /4 -r 0.6 6_Images.hdr > %CurrDirName%_6_Images.hdr 
Figure 50: The left images show a rendering with no oversampling and the right images show a rendering with an over-
sampling of 4 time. Both renderings had a final resolution of 3200x3200 pixels. 
==PAGE:112==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
89 
 
from HDR to LDR is furthermore a necessity for setting up the scenes in Unity. The conversion 
happens through Tone Mapping with the software Luminance HDR 2.6.0, where an algorithm 
dictates the reproduction of the tones in HDR-images to LDR-images [51].  
In a study conducted by Kynthia Chamilothori about “Real versus photograph-based virtual 
environments”, the rank-order in a comparison between Tone Mapping Operators were in-
vestigated [51]. In the study, different real-world daylit scenes were considered through cube 
mapping and the most preferred Tone Mapping method showed to be Reinhard02. Since the 
general setup is somewhat like the one in the current master thesis, the method of Rein-
hards02 is used. In the study conducted by Kynthia the gamma correction is set to 2.0 based 
on luminance measurement at the lens of a headset, however the correction showed to de-
viate a bit from the optimal value at 2.3 for the Oculus CV1 headset used. Because of this and 
because the default gamma value for other similar image converters as ra_tiff and ra_bmp is 
2.2, the gamma value in this thesis is set to 2.2.  
A sub-study conducted by Mikkel Kofoed Pedersen investigated the effect of Auto Levels 
when Tone Mapping, see section 10.16. This study concludes that Auto Levels should be 
turned off when Tone Mapping, which is incorporated in the current study.  
All settings used for the Tone Mapping operator Reinhard02 is shown in Figure 51. 
 
Figure 51: Settings used for Tone Mapping operator Rinhard02 through the software Luminance HDR 2.6.0.

3.5 Quantification Methods 
The rendered HDR images are used for the quantification of the contrast and complexity in-
stead of using the tone mapped LDR images. The HDR images reflect the light level of reality 
and would thereby produce more realistic results for contrast and complexity. However, the 
VR-experiment uses the tone mapped LDR images, meaning that the contrast and complexity 
values experienced by the subjects are not the same as those values that are measured from 
the HDR images.  
Each scene is constructed of 6 images as directions and some of these images will be observed 
more than others during the experiment due to many unknown variables such as view direc-
tion, eye and head movement, etc. To insure a more accurate representation of the contrast 
and the complexity values experienced by the viewer under the experiment, some assump-
tions have been made. The VIVE Pro Eye headset has a limited visual field of 110o [46] and by 
including the sensory impressions from the peripheral vision and ability to relocate the central 
visual field through eye movement, it is assumed that everything within the 110o visual field 
will influence the subjects perception of the contrast and the complexity. Head motion is an-
other limiting factor, Michael C. Albers [23] explains a rectangular guide for a comfortable and 
maximum range for the horizontal and vertical viewing angles. 
 
RECTANGULAR GUIDE 
 
Left/right 
Up 
Down 
 
 
 
 
Comfortable range 
± 30° 
+ 20° 
- 12° 
 
 
 
 
Maximum range 
± 55° 
+ 60° 
- 40° 
Table 7: The horizontal and vertical viewing angles for head motion. 
 
 
==PAGE:115==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
92 
 
By combining the head motion ranges with the 110o visual field from the VR-headset, the 
following visual zones are created: 
RECTANGULAR GUIDE + THE 110° VISUAL FIELD IN VR 
 
Left/right 
Up 
Down 
 
 
 
 
Comfortable visual zone 
± 85° 
+ 75° 
- 67° 
 
 
 
 
Maximum visual zone 
± 110° 
+ 115° 
- 95° 
Table 8: The horizontal and vertical viewing angles for head motion combined with the 110o visual field. 
It is assumed that most participants of the VR experiment would mainly be viewing the scenes 
within the comfortable visual zone and not rotate their bodies, since they will mainly be look-
ing directly in front of them to be able to answer the questionnaire. It is most likely that even 
the comfortable ranges are far larger than most participants would observe, but since no data 
of head/eye tracking will be collected in this project there will be no way to prove this. There-
fore, the comfortable visual zone will be used as a limiting factor when conducting the con-
trast and the complexity values for the scenes.  
Calculating the contrast and the complexity values for only the comfortable visual zone is ra-
ther complicated due to the setup of cubemapping projection. The left image in Figure 52 
shows an example of which pixels should be removed from the different orientations. This is 
with the lighter areas of the images being removed to create the rectangular visual zone, see 
Table 8. When accurately calculating the contrast of a scene with the RAMMG algorithm, ar-
eas in the corners between each cubemapped image should be filled out. In other words, 
pixels are added to the scene. Since there would be either ceiling or floor in these areas, it 
was decided to copy the HDR images of the floor and ceiling into the corners and rotate them, 
this is so they line up with the East and West orientations. The right image in Figure 52 shows 
an example with the inserted images in the corners and which pixels should be removed from 
the different orientations, visualized as lighter areas, to create the rectangular visual zone 
used for the scripts. This solution will influence the quantified contrast and the complexity of 
the scene, but influence is expected and assumed to be minor. Hence, this image constellation 
was selected. 
==PAGE:116==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
93 
 
 
The script for this cropping and combining of the images has been written by Mandana Sarey 
Khanie and has been used for the three photometric quantification MATLAB scripts shown in 
Appendix  10.10. 
 
3.5.1 Contrast  
To carry out the algorithm for the multi-level contrast measure by Rizzi et al. the MATLAB 
script written by Jiaqi is used in this project. The script has been carefully examined for errors 
and the addition of investigating all levels of an HDR-image instead of 4 levels has been edited, 
which is more accurate to the algorithm by Rizzi et al. In addition, the results are automatically 
written in an Excel file. The scripts for the achromatic and chromatic contrast measures can 
be found in Appendix 10.10.1 and 10.10.2. The scripts calculate the contrast in the same man-
ner that is described in section 2.6.1, but they calculate the contrast of the pixels in the cor-
ners and edges of the HDR image separately since they are surrounded by fewer pixels than 
pixels in the center of the image. The corners only have 3 other pixels to compare pixels values 
with and the edges only have 5 pixels surrounding them. This means that there are always 
Figure 52: The pixel area removed from the images to recreate the comfortable visual zone is depicted in a lighter color. The 
viewing angles are shown on the axes. The left is an example of what pixels that should be used for the RAMMG algorithm, 
and the right image have the necessary pixels in the corners to run the script. 
==PAGE:117==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
94 
 
“center pixels”, “edge pixels” and “corner pixels” when investigating a certain level, therefore 
the last level possible to investigate must be at least 3x3 pixels, which is checked for by the 
script.  
3.5.2 Complexity 
To calculate the standard deviation as a quantification method for visual complexity the 
MATLAB script written by Jiaqi is used in this project. The script has been carefully examined 
for errors, however, nothing major was changed apart from the results being written auto-
matically in an Excel file. The script for the achromatic and chromatic visual complexity meas-
ure can be found in Appendix 10.10.3. The scripts calculate the visual complexity in the same 
manner as described in section 2.6.2. 
 
3.5.3 Visual Connectivity 
The visual connectivity will be investigated as a 3D evaluation of the percentage of view out-
side windows of the given office room from the same location as all the scenes rendered. The 
reason for a 3D evaluation of the view instead of the 2D evaluation described in section 
10.10.4is that the 2D evaluation cannot effectively be utilized for the horizontal blinds and 
the complex geometries of the shutters with growing plants. Therefore, this investigation will 
be conducted with a raytracing method using Radiance. The idea behind the new method is 
developed in collaboration with Mandana Sarey Khanie and Mikkel Kofod Pedersen, and the 
script used for investigation was written by Mikkel Kofod Pedersen, see Appendix 10.10.4. 
The rays are created the Reinhart sky discretization twice mirrored on the z-direction to cre-
ate a full 360o sphere. 4,718,594 rays are generated between the given location point and the 
full sphere. Every ray intersected by the geometry will have a value of 0, where all the other 
rays will have a value of 1. A percentile of 1s to 0s is then calculated. To ensure that there are 
no reflections of the rays, the script assigns all materials with a complete black material that 
absorbs all light. Any reflections would decrease the accuracy of the results. The visible 
amount of view from any given location in the room can be calculated using this script, but 
only if all intersecting geometry outside of the window and the glass itself is removed from 
the geometry.rad file. Therefore, a new geometry.rad file is constructed where all intersecting 
geometry has been removed for each investigated shading type. 
==PAGE:118==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
95 
 
3.6 Questionnaire 
3.6.1 Demographic Information 
Before starting each VR-session demographic information is collected. This is done to get a 
better understanding of the subjects and if any external circumstances could potentially in-
fluence the subject responses. The demographic information covers general personal infor-
mation and personal wellbeing and the data is collected through questionnaires created with 
Google Forms. The questionnaire setup is found in Appendix 10.7. 
 
3.6.2 Subjective Responses  
After each scene displayed in VR four questions with five answer options appear, in the order 
shown in Figure 53. The questions and answer options are set up through a script in Unity. 
The script was originally set up by Jiaqi in the study Pilot 1 and has further been developed in 
this thesis with assistance from Software Engineer student Asbjørn Olling. The script is found 
in Appendix 10.10.5. To account for subjects speaking different languages the questions were 
created in both Danish and English, hence extra precautions should be taken. The translation 
is done in collaboration with Cognitive Science student and writer Astrid Olling, to secure the 
most psychological correct translation. Both the English and the Danish version is found in 
Appendix 10.11. Conducting the experiments, the Danish translation however ended up fit-
ting all subjects. 
Figure 53: Questions listed in the order displayed in VR. (English version). 
==PAGE:119==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
96 
 
3.7 Virtual Reality Setup 
The virtual Reality experiment was setup in Unity version 2019.3.15f1 using the unity setup 
and the three scripts made by M. C. Prokou and M. Stefaniotou as a base [24]. Both the unity 
setup and the scripts have been edited in numerous ways to achieve the requirements for 
this study. Every scene is generated by projecting the 6 rendered and processed LDR images 
onto a 6-sided skymap, which is a variation of cupemapping. The “Wrap mode” function for 
all LDR images is set to “Clamp” to remove clear white lines where the images meet on the 
skymap. For each experiment scene, two Unity scenes are created, a view scene with just the 
given view from the office room and a questionnaire scene which is identical to the view scene 
except it has the questionnaire placed as text panels and buttons right under the viewers eye-
height to not obstruct the view. 
 
Figure 54: A questionnaire scene with the text panels and buttons in Danish 
The experiment is started in a demo that has the same structure as the experiment scenes. 
Each view scene is presented for 15 seconds and then changed to the questionnaire scene by 
the “TimerSceneChanger” script. The 15 seconds of displaying the view are enforced to secure 
that the subjects get a chance to study each scene and form an opinion. Furthermore, previ-
ous studies have shown that the chromatic adaptation is considerable after 15 seconds [27]. 
==PAGE:120==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
97 
 
In the questionnaire scene the first question and the 5 representable answers are shown, 
after an answer has been chosen with the controller, then the “LaserClicker” script changes 
the text on the text panels and buttons to the next question and its answers. When all the 
questions have been answered, then a random experiment scene that has not been pre-
sented before will be shown to the viewer. This random scene can be any of the 38-scene 
rendered for the 3 experiments. This is possible since all the experiments have the same ques-
tionnaire. This randomization will reduce experimental bias such as the Selection Bias that 
would occur in a predetermined scene sequence [63]. When all experiment scenes have been 
presented the experiment ends by showing an end scene with a “Thank you for participating” 
note. The demo and end scene have a view from a free unity asset called Sky Series Free by 
Avionx [64]. All answers chosen with the controller are recorded in a text file by a combination 
of the “SaveChoices” and “LaserClicker” scripts. All the scripts can be found in Appendix 10.10.  
 
3.8 Overview of Experiment 
As a part of characterizing view out, three experiments were set up based on the above de-
scriptions and methodologies. In the following sections, a final overview of the three experi-
ments presented in section 3.2, is provided. 
 
3.8.1 Experiment 1 - Overview 
The first experiment is built around the independent variables (i.v.) shading conditions, view 
quality, & luminance contrast and the confounding variables color contrast, achromatic com-
plexity, & chromatic complexity. The experiment consists of twenty different scenes, and all 
i.v. and their respective levels are presented in Table 9. The aim of this experiment is to detect 
differences in and associations of the dependent variable (d.v.) subjective responses caused 
by changes in independent- and confounding variables. This is through assessment of the 
general hypothesis set up in section 3.2.2. 
Shading types consisting of slats do in this experiment have a slat angle at 0° since it provides 
the greatest amount of view, whereas the screens in this experiment are completely drawn 
to detect the full effect of a screen in use. Furthermore, a scene with no shading is included

3.9 Experiment Procedure  
3.9.1 COVID-19 
In march 2020 the World Health Organization (WHO) declared the COVID-19 a pandemic ac-
cording to the Danish Health Authority [65]. As a response to the restrictions, the experiment 
is assigned to high hygiene demands. Facemasks, gloves, and hand sanitizer are available for 
free, and the VR-headset and controller are disinfected between each session. Social distanc-
ing of minimum 2m is complied with when possible, and when it is not facemasks are worn if 
necessary.  
 
3.9.2 Subject Recruitment 
The sample size of an experiment is important to the validity of the data outcome. A too small 
sample size results in low power or low sensitivity, whereas larger sample sizes result in suf-
ficient power [41]. The sample size is often limited by the cost-effectiveness relationship and 
should be considered carefully. In this thesis experiment the limiting factor is time since all 
subjects volunteered to participate in the experiment unpaid. Based on this the optimal sam-
ple size is determined to 30 subjects.   
Due to the COVID-19 pandemic, the subject recruitment was expected hampered and there-
fore alternative measures were used. The majority of the subjects participating in the exper-
iment is family, friends, and acquaintances. Using this procedure leads both to pros and cons 
that should be considered. One formal benefit is that the experiments are conducted in 
smaller groups that accommodates existing restrictions for COVID-19 relations. Another ben-
efit is that the subjects represent different work-related backgrounds and age groups, as op-
posed to the earlier, described study 3.1.1 Pilot 1 where the probability of work-related bias 
is greater and could be an issue.  
One of the downsides is that the samples are not randomly drawn from the Danish popula-
tion, and it might therefore not be representative, hence biases might still occur. These could 
be factors as geographical location, economic status etc. Furthermore, the relationship be-
tween the subjects and the experiment conductors might bias the experimental outcome up-
wards, as they might tend to give more positive feedback conducting the experiment.  
==PAGE:131==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
108 
 
The optimal solution would be to draw the sample randomly from the entire Danish popula-
tion, but due to the above-mentioned circumstances, this was not possible. 
The subjects recruited are all 18+ and are qualified to respond to work-related circumstances. 
In total 32 subjects participated in the experiments. 
 
3.9.3 Equipment 
The equipment used to conduct the experiment is listed in the following section: 
Hygiene 
• Hand sanitizer  
• Gloves 
• Face masks 
VR-setup with HTC VIVE PRO EYE 
• Base station (incl. stand, mounting kit, and power adapter) 
• Link box (incl. USB 3.0 cable, display port cable and Link box power adaptor) 
• Headset with headset cable (incl. cleaning cloth) 
• Controller (incl. power adapter and micro-USB-cable)  
Additional Equipment 
• Foldable ruler 
• Chair 
• Computer (and iPad) 
 
3.9.4 Performing Experiments 
The perfect scenario when conducting an experiment is that all experimental conditions are 
the same during each subject session. This applies to both the VR setup and surrounding real-
life conditions. Because the experiments are conducted at varying locations and because sub-
jects are not identical, this has however not been completely achievable, and compromises 
turned out to be a necessity. 
==PAGE:132==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
109 
 
 A compromise of the experiment is the viewpoint height in VR and thereby the perceived 
dimensions of the scenes [51]. The images used to create the VR-scenes are rendered from a 
viewpoint at 1.2 m. The average eye level of a woman seated is 1.17 m whereas it for men is 
1.22m  [66]. Therefore, the average eye level for a seated adult is ~ 1.2 m. However, in real 
life, this height varies from person to person. People with an eye-height lower than 1.2 m 
might experience that they feel taller than in real life and people with eye heights above 1.2 
m might feel smaller than in real life. This could cause confusion in terms of the perceived 
dimensions of the scenes. The only way to avoid this completely is by creating different scenes 
for each subject based on their seated eye levels. This is too time-consuming, and it has there-
fore not been possible within the limited timeline of this thesis.   
Since the experiment takes place at multiple different locations the surrounding real-life con-
ditions should be carefully considered. The most important surrounding real-life conditions 
are covered by background noise, participant position and design of the experimental chair.  
The optimal condition for the surroundings is described in the following section.  
The background noise should be slim to none. Sudden sounds could interfere with the sub-
jects feeling of presence in VR especially if the sound does not align with the office-setting.  
The subject should be positioned perpendicular to the window. This aligns with the room dis-
played in VR and if for any reason light from the surroundings should enter the VR-headset (if 
it is not tightened enough or if the head shape of the subject does simply not fit), the direction 
of the light should preferably align with the VR scene.  
At last, the chair picked out for the experimental sessions should be chosen based on certain 
parameters. The chair must not be with wheels, since it could result in the subject moving by 
mistake during the experiment. Furthermore, the chair should set the scene for a straight 
sitting position.  
Conducting the experiments, the above-mentioned surrounding conditions have been strived 
for or alternatively, the best possible balance between them was aimed for.  
==PAGE:133==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
110

see Figure 62. Sanitizing the headset special focus is put on the contact points as nose area, 
google skirt, regulation buttons and Velcro strap.

As described in 3.9.1 COVID-19 facemasks, gloves and hand sanitizer are always available to 
the subject, see Figure 63.If the subject does not want to wear gloves, they are kindly asked 
to sanitize or wash their hands. Before the experiment starts, the subjects are presented to 
the procedure of the experiment. The procedure is presented through an experiment folder, 
see Appendix 10.6. Depending on the preferences of the subject, the content is either read 
and discussed or fully presented verbally. All subjects are informed that the experiment is 
about View Out of Windows and the effect on solar shading. The procedure described corre-
sponding to the below explained. First the subject is asked to fill in a formular that collects 
demographic information, see Appendix 10.7. 
The distance from occupant’s eyes to the base station is measured to ~1.2 m and the sub-
ject is relocated if needed, see Figure 64. Before the subject enters the virtual space the use 
of the controller is explained. To set up the best possible virtual conditions for each subject, 
the subject is asked to adjust the headset in three steps: head size, the height of headset, 
Figure 62: Controller, VR-headset, and keyboard surfaces is disinfected between each session.

mental procedure is presented, and demographic information is collected.

and distance between eyes. At last, the controller is handed over and the headphones are 
shut down to reduce the sound level from any unexpected background noises. 
The VR sessions are structured so a scene is displayed for 15 seconds after which questions 
appear. The 15 seconds scene display is enforced to secure that the subjects get a chance to 
study each scene and form an opinion. Furthermore, previous studies have shown that the 
chromatic adaptation is considerable after 15 seconds [27].  
The VR sessions are structured so a scene is displayed for 15 seconds after which a series of 
four questions with five answer options appear, see section 3.6.2  
Before the experiment starts, the subjects are presented to the virtual space through a demo 
scene, see Figure 65. The demo scene is like all other scenes structured with 15 seconds of 
scene display followed by questions. The demo scene is used to make the subject familiar 
with the VR space and the overall experiment structure. Right before starting the demo scene 
the subjects are informed that some scenes might look similar but that none of them is iden-
tical, which is why they should just try to answer the questions to the best of their ability. The 
subjects are furthermore informed, that they may ask as many questions as they want during 
the demo scene after which questions will be answered as shortly as possible. The total num-
ber of VR scenes varies between 33 – 38 (see section 3.10.1) and they are displayed in random 
order.

headset is put on and adjusted.

3.10 Analysis Methodology 
This section covers the analysis methods used to assess the collected data. This includes a 
description of the data type collected, potential normality of the data, and a short description 
of the hypothesis test used in the thesis.  
When the data was collected from the VR experiment, the scenes were shown randomly. 
Therefore, some participants were shown a series that would alter their answers. Some par-
ticipants were presented with only LVQ scenes for the first couple of scenes and gave positive 
responses until the HVQ scene was shown for them, which resulted in a decrease in positive 
responses for LVQ scenes. The opposite was observed if HVQ scenes were shown first. Factors 
like these would influence the subjective responses less the further into the experiment the 
participants were. This was defined as a “learning curve”. To eliminate the steepest part of 
this learning curve, the data collected for the demo and the first three scenes presented to 
the individual participant was not included in any analyses. This exclusion will reduce the 
amount of data and since the scenes are shown randomly it will also cause the scenes to have 
an unequal amount of subjective responses. Additionally, not all scenes were finished for 
around half of the subjective assessments conducted with the VR experiment, because of file 
corruption and the long computational time. Therefore, scenes with High-density shutters 
and the one scene with Small Blinds with HVQ for the 30th of May at 08:00 have significantly 
fewer subjective responses than other scenes. All this will cause unequal reliability for the 
data of different scenes. The total number of responses for each scene is shown in Table 28 
in Appendix 10.19. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:138==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
115 
 
3.10.1 Data Type 
To identify methodologies suitable for the assessment of the collected data it is important to 
detect the data type. The data collection consists of the independent variables “luminance 
contrast, connectedness and shading condition”, the confounding variables “chromatic con-
trast and complexity” and the associated dependent variable “subjective responses”. 
The subjective responses are categorized and ranked in a certain order according to the an-
swer options presented, see section 3.6.2, where the interval between categories is unknown. 
This set of data type is called ordinal data [67].  
The independent variables are ranked in a certain order with quantifiable intervals between 
the categories. Because of this, and because the dataset has a zero-point it is determined as 
ratio scale data [68]. 
Ordinal data types are more limited than ratio scale data types [69]. Hence the ratio scale 
data are converted to ordinal data by categorizing it to make the datasets comparable. Below 
is an overview of achromatic complexity, chromatic complexity, achromatic contrast, and 
chromatic contrast that is each divided into categories of 5 groups based on data set range 
and scene distributions.

Connectedness is converted to ordinal data by categorizing the data based on scene name 
and percentage connectedness both in the first and second experiment, see sections 4.2.1 
and 4.2.2. 
A test can either be parametric or non-parametric. Parametric tests are more powerful and 
are more accurately detecting differences between samples but it is not suitable for all types 
of data. Parametric tests are used when a dataset follows a normal distribution whereas a 
non-parametric test does not consider the data distribution.  
Ordinal data sets are in general considered as non-parametric and it is proved to apply to the 
dataset of this master’s thesis project in the following section. 
 
3.10.2 Test of Normality 
A test for normal distribution of the data is in general not suitable for ordinal data sets. This 
is because the intervals between categories are unknown and might deviate based on subjec-
tive opinions. However, a test of normality has been conducted to show that the data is not 
normally distributed despite an assumption of equal intervals between category groups. 
Listed below are the normality test results presented based on both the Kolmogorv-Smirnov 
and the Shapiro Wilk method. The test results presented below cover for question A, see 
3.6.2, where normality results for questions B, C and D are presented in Appendix 10.20. 
The cutoff value (α) chosen to conduct the analysis is 5%. Text marked with red indicates that 
the test fails to reject the null hypothesis at a significance level of 5% whereas text that is not 
marked indicates that the test fails to reject the null hypothesis at a 5% significance level.  
All analyzes including the test of normality are conducted using the statistical software plat-
form SPSS Statistics.

To analyses the data gathered, hypotheses testing is conducted. For each of the hypotheses 
presented in Table 17, a null hypothesis and alternative hypotheses should be constructed. 
Several different tools are available for hypotheses testing (significance testing) where the 
most suitable ones are presented in the following sections along with important related as-
sumptions. 
 
Friedman test 
The Friedman test is used when the same set of subjects are exposed to different circum-
stances that make up three or more occasions [70]. The test is non-parametric and is related 
to the parametric test one-way ANOVA with repeated measures. A list of assumptions should 
be complied with to detect whether the test is suitable for a particular set of data. Such a list 
has been provided by Lærd Statistics and is described below [70]. 
The Friedman test is in this thesis used to investigate hypothesis 1.3. 
 
Sign test 
The paired-samples sign test is used to detect if there is a significant difference between two 
sets of data, and it is the non-parametric alternative to a “paired-sample t-test”. A list of as-
sumptions should be complied with to detect whether the test is suitable for a particular set 
of data. Lærd Statistics has set up such a list and it is described below [71]. 
[70]   
==PAGE:145==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
122 
 
The Sign test is in this thesis used to investigate hypothesis 3.1. 
 
Chi-square test 
The Chi-square test for association compares two variables to determine a potential relation-
ship between them [72]. Likewise, in the Friedman- and Sign test it is important to check if a 
set of data complies with certain assumptions to make sure that the test is suitable for anal-
ysis. The assumptions are provided by Lærd Statistics and listed below [72]. 
When running a Chi-square test it is important to notice how many cells have an expected 
count less than 5 in the results. For larger tests (>2x2) less than 20% of the cells may have an 
expected count less than 5 for the significance test to be reliable [73]. 
The Chi-square test is in this thesis used to investigate hypotheses 1.1, 1.2, 1.4, and 2.1. 
 
 
[71]  
[72]  
==PAGE:146==
METHODOLOGY – CHAPTER 3 
123 
 
Kendall’s tau-b 
Kendall’s tau-b test is an effect size test describing the strength and direction of a relation 
between two variables [74]. The test is conducted for all hypotheses investigated with the 
Chi-square test if the null hypothesis is not rejected. The assumptions the data should comply 
with is described by Lærd Statistics and listed below [74]. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The Kendall’s tau-b coefficient (τb) ranges from -1 to 1 where a larger value indicates that 
there is a strong linear association between the variables. A negative τb indicates a negative 
correlation whereas a positive value indicates a positive correlation [74]. SPSS tutorials have 
created some rules of thumb for the interpretation of τb which will be used in this thesis [75]: 
• |τb|= 0.07 indicates a weak association 
• |τb|= 0.21 indicates a medium association 
• |τb|= 0.35 indicates a strong association 
 
 
 
 
 
[74]

4.1 Demographic Information 
Prior to conducting the VR-experiments the subjects were asked to fill out a form with their 
demographic information. The number of subjects participating in the experiment is 32 cor-
responding to 100% in the graphs. All subjects attending are above 18. The demographic in-
formation is divided into categories of Personal Information and Personal Well-being in the 
sections below.  
After each experiment all subjects where kindly asked to answer some feedback questions. 
The feedback question and the subjective responses are presented in Appendix 10.9. Fur-
thermore, some subjects provided verbal feedback during the experiment. The feedback 
which was repeated the most related to the LVQ scenes. The subjects felt that the opposite 
building was located to close which let them to feel exposed.

age but still the age group 40-59 is dominating. In Figure 68 the distribution of the subjects is 
listed according to gender. 19 subjects identify as male, 12 subjects identify as female.

4.2 Subjective assessment  
In this section the results of the subjective responses and quantified variables for the 38 
scenes will be presented, analyzed and the 5 hypotheses will be tested for the 3 experiments. 
All tests will be conducted using the statistical software "SPSS Statistics".  
The hypothesis testing for 5 hypotheses for each of the 4 questions (see section 3.6.2) is a 
rather extensive investigations and limitations were necessary. Therefore, the data analysis 
and hypothesis testing will only be conducted on the subjective responses gathered from 
question A and D. These two questions have the strongest link to the 5 hypotheses, since 
question A is directly linked to the perception of the view, and question D is linked to the 
overall scene experience with the added element of time. However, all graphs presented in 
section 4.2 have been constructed for all 4 questions and can be seen in Appendix 10.21. 
Question B and C might help explain some of the tendencies for amount of view and the pre-
ferred shading conditions and should therefore be investigated in future works.  
Figure 77: Subject distribution of current mood. 
Figure 78: Subject distribution of current alertness. 
==PAGE:151==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
128 
 
Furthermore, boxplots have been set up for the distribution of subjective responses, for each 
experiment, and for all questions. The boxplots are likewise found in Appendix 10.21. 
  
4.2.1 First Experiment 
In the first experiment the relation between subjective responses on view perception and a 
variety of independent and confounding variables are investigated. The independent varia-
bles investigated are shading conditions (including amount of view) & achromatic contrast, 
and the confounding variables investigated are chromatic contrast, achromatic complexity, & 
chromatic complexity. The subjective responses investigated are derived from the 4 scenes 
for each of the 5 shading conditions. The scenes are shown and explained in section 3.8.1.   
The best course of action when investigating the measured variables with a Chi-square test 
(see section 3.10.3), would be to investigate each variable for a selection of scenes that have 
the same visual conditions. This selection of scenes could be based on the view quality, point 
in time or the shading condition. The selection is to ensure that all scene variations are mainly 
based on the measured variables and thereby reducing the amount of uncertainties. For ex-
ample, by separating the data based on view quality, the results might produce a higher effect 
size for HVQ than LVQ or vice versa. In this way the results could show if people prefer more 
amount of view in HVQ situations or prefer less amount of view if people in the opposite build 
could look in. Contrast, visual complexity, and view access were first investigated in this way, 
by selecting the data based on view quality and point in time. However, by conducting the 
Chi-Square test in SPSS it was discovered that this selection decreased the distribution of pos-
itive and negative responses for all scenes, resulting in the violation of the 3rd Chi-Square as-
sumption described in section 3.10.3. The results of all these tests are available in Appendix 
10.21.1 at the end of each section. Therefore, the less favored approach was chosen, where 
the relation between subjective responses and the measured variable are investigated for all 
scenes collectively.  
 
==PAGE:152==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
129 
 
Subjective Responses 
This section will present the subjective responses for the first experiment to assess the gen-
eral subjective tendencies. The distribution of the subjective responses for question A is 
shown below on Figure 79 and for question D on Figure 80.  
 
Figure 79: Distribution of first experiment subjective responses to user assessment question A. 
It is observed for question A that scenes with high view quality are rated more positively than 
scenes with low view quality, but there is not a large difference between scenes with the two 
different times and dates. This suggests that the content of the view have a much greater 
effect on the subjective perception of the view than general lighting conditions. There is a 
small difference in the subjective responses of the two lighting conditions, however the dif-
ferences are too small to see any trends’. The shading conditions with the most positive re-
sponses are the scenes with no shading and the shading conditions with the least positive 
responses are the scenes with white screens.

RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
130 
 
 
Figure 80: Distribution of first experiment subjective responses to user assessment question D. 
Some of the same tendencies can be observed for the subjective responses for question D. 
However, there is a larger difference between the two lighting conditions, but the significance 
of this observation is still unclear. Furthermore, the most positive responses are given to the 
scenes with big blinds, but it is mainly due to the decrease in positive responses for the scenes 
with no shading comparing subjective responses of question A and D. In general, the subjec-
tive responses for question D HVQ are rated a little less positively than for question A, but the 
difference is so minuscule that it might be a coincidence. Furthermore, the majority of LVQ 
scenes are rated more negatively in question A compared to question D.

Contrast 
In the first experiment the effect of the independent variable for achromatic contrast and the 
confounding variable chromatic contrast on the dependent variable subjective responses is 
investigated. The general hypothesis investigated in this experiment is as follows: 
 
The achromatic and chromatic contrast is measured for each scene using the script explained 
in section 3.5. To detect any potential tendency between the contrast values and the subjec-
tive responses, combined graphs have been set up presenting both variables. The achromatic 
contrast values with the subjective responses for question A is presented on Figure 81 and for 
question D on Figure 82. The chromatic contrast values with the subjective responses for 
question A is presented on Figure 83 and for question D on Figure 84. 
 
 
Figure 81: Distribution of first experiment subjective responses of question A and the related achromatic contrast (RAMMG) 
of the scene. 
Hypothesis 1.1 
”Contrast affects people’s perception of view” 
==PAGE:155==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
132 
 
 
Figure 82: Distribution of first experiment subjective responses of question D and the related achromatic contrast (RAMMG) 
of the scene. 
From the investigations conducted in section 3.2.7, the achromatic contrast is expected to 
have a higher value for scenes set in April than the equivalent scene set in May. However, this 
is only the case for 6/10 scenes and 2/3 of these cases are scenes with a low view quality, 
which is surprising since the investigations in section 3.2.7 were only conducted on scenes 
with a high view quality.  
The scene with the highest achromatic contrast is “Big Blinds_LVQ_041414” which is rated a 
little more positively than negatively. The scene with the lowest chromatic contrast is “Verti-
cal Louvres_HVQ_041414” which has primarily positive responses. 
Comparing the subjective responses for question A and D to the achromatic contrast will in-
dicate that there is no obvious correlation between subjective responses and achromatic con-
trast. A high and a low contrast for the same view and shading condition (e.g. “Big 
Blinds_HVQ_041414” and “Big Blinds_HVQ_053008”) have close to equal distribution of sub-
jective responses. Scenes with equal contrast (e.g. “No Shading_HVQ_041414” and “White 
screens_HVQ_041414”) can have vastly different distributions of subjective responses from 
each other.  These observations might suggest that there is no correlation, but to be certain 
the significance test Chi-square have been conducted later in this section and its results are 
shown in Table 18. 
==PAGE:156==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
133 
 
 
Figure 83: Distribution of first experiment subjective responses of question A and the related chromatic contrast (RAMMG) 
of the scene. 
 
 
 
Figure 84: Distribution of first experiment subjective responses of question D and the related chromatic contrast (RAMMG) 
of the scene.

RESULTS – CHAPTER 4

For most shading conditions the chromatic contrast is higher for scenes with a low view qual-
ity than the related scenes with high view quality. The scene with the highest chromatic con-
trast is “Small Blinds_LVQ_041414” which is rated equally positively and negatively. The scene 
with the lowest chromatic contrast is “Big Blinds_HVQ_041414” which has primarily positive 
responses. 
Comparing the subjective responses for question A and D to the chromatic contrast will indi-
cate that there is no clear correlation between subjective responses and chromatic contrast. 
This is because scenes with equal amount of chromatic contrast do have different distribution 
of the subjective responses (“No Shading_HVQ_041414” and “No Shading_LVQ_041414”) 
whereas others have close to the exact same (“No Shading_HVQ_041414” and “Small 
Blinds_HVQ_041414”). Equal chromatic contrast and equal distribution of subjective re-
sponses occur when comparing scenes with the same point in time and the same view quality. 
Hence unequal distribution of subjective responses in scenes with the same chromatic con-
trast might occur due to other i.v., confounding variables, or extraneous variables. To test 
whether there is a tendency and potentially its significance a hypothesis test has been con-
ducted using the Chi-square test followed by a test of the effect size with the Kendall’s Tau-b 
test (se section 3.10.3). In Table 18 is the null hypothesis and alternative hypothesis presented 
followed by the results of the statistical hypothesis test and effect size test for the achromatic 
and chromatic contrast for both question A and D. The hypothesis tests use the intervals for 
achromatic contrast presented on Table 14 and for chromatic contrast on Table 15. The meas-
ured achromatic and chromatic contrast for all scenes is presented in Appendix 10.21.1.

The results of the test show that 12% of the cells have an expected count less than five for

reliable.  
The results on Table 18 shows for question A that there is a statistically significant association 
between people’s perception of the view and the achromatic contrast of the scene, X2(16) = 
102.6, p = <0.001, and likewise for question D X2(16) = 86.2, p = <0.001. Meaning that both 
tests reject the null hypothesis at a 5% significance level. The effect size test for question A 
shows a weak positive correlation between people’s perception of the view and the achro-
matic contrast, which was not statistically significant (τb = 0.036, p = 0.308), and a weak neg-
ative correlation with no statistical significance for question D (τb = -0.040, p = 0.259). The Chi-
square test shows that the subjective responses and achromatic contrast changes depending 
on each other. However, the effect size is very small and shows no significance, which indi-
cates that the changes in people’s perception of the view was due to other independent, con-
founding, or extraneous variables.

124.7, p = <0.001, and likewise for question D X2(16) = 86.0, p = <0.001. Meaning that both 
tests reject the null hypothesis at a 5% significance level. The effect size test for question A 
shows a medium to strong positive correlation between people’s perception of the view and 
the chromatic contrast, which was statistically significant (τb = 0.237, p = 0.000), and a weak 
to medium positive correlation with statistical significance for question D (τb = 0.195, p =

Visual Complexity 
In the first experiment the effect of the confounding variables for achromatic and chromatic 
visual complexity on the dependent variable subjective responses is investigated. The gen-
eral hypothesis investigated in this experiment are as follows: 
 
The achromatic and chromatic visual complexity is measured for each scene using the script 
explained in section 3.5. To detect any potential tendency between the visual complexity val-
ues and the subjective responses, combined graphs have been set up presenting both varia-
bles. The achromatic visual complexity values with the subjective responses for question A is 
presented on Figure 85 and for question D on Figure 86. The chromatic visual complexity val-
ues with the subjective responses for question A is presented on Figure 87 and for question 
D on Figure 88. 
 
Hypothesis 1.2 
”Visual complexity affects people’s perception of view”

complexity: “A high and a low visual complexity for the same view and shading condition have 
close to equal distribution of subjective responses. Scenes with equal complexity can have 
vastly different distributions of subjective responses from each other”. Therefore, it is difficult 
to observe an obvious correlation between subjective responses and achromatic visual com-

Shading Conditions 
As a part of the first experiment the effect of the independent variable shading conditions on 
the dependent variable subjective responses is investigated. The general hypothesis investi-
gated is as follows: 
 
In Figure 89 and Figure 90 the subjective responses are categorized based on shading condi-
tions to get a better overview of the difference in subjective response distribution between 
the shading condition categories, respectively presented for question A and question D. Fur-
thermore, the figures make it possible to detect any potential tendency between shading 
conditions and distribution of subjective responses. 
Hypothesis 1.3 
”Different shading conditions affect people’s perception of view” 
==PAGE:167==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
144 
 
 
Figure 89: The distribution of first experiment subjective responses for question A categorized based on shading conditions. 
 
 
 
Figure 90: The distribution of first experiment subjective responses for question A categorized based on shading conditions. 
 
==PAGE:168==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
145 
 
From both Figure 89 and Figure 90 it can be seen that there is a difference in the distribution 
of subjective responses for the different shading conditions. The “White Screen” conditions 
are rated most negatively whereas the “No Shading” and “Big Blinds” conditions are related 
most positively.  
For question A the “No Shading” conditions are rated a little more positive than the “Big Blind” 
conditions whereas the “Big Blind” conditions are rated a little more positive than “No Shad-
ing” conditions in question D. This could be a coincidence, or it could indicate that the subjects 
perceive “No Shading” conditions as most pleasant but “Big Blinds” conditions as more desir-
able for longer periods of working.  
For question A the “White Screen” conditions are rated more negatively than in question D. 
This could be a coincidence, or it could indicate that the subjects, despite of rating the pleas-
antness of the conditions extremely negative, are less negative when it comes to working 
under these conditions for longer periods.   
Another important thing to notice, is that Big Blinds and Vertical Louvres almost identical, 
meaning that the slats of each shading type have the same size, there is the same distance 
between slats, and the same geometry. However, the Big Blinds score overall more positively 
than Vertical Louvres.  
Since it is possible to detect a difference in subject response distribution between different 
shading conditions a hypothesis test is conducted using the Friedman test to detect any po-
tential significance (see section 3.10.3). In Table 20 is the null hypothesis and alternative hy-
pothesis presented followed by the results of the statistical hypothesis test for both question 
A and D. An extended version of this table including mean ranks and median values of the 
scenes is found in Appendix 10.21.1.
