From the results presented in Table 20 it can be concluded that the different shading condi-
tions does affect people’s perception of the view rejecting the null hypothesis at a 5% signifi-
cance level.  
When conducting the Friedman test median values are automatically calculated for each 
scene, see appendix 10.21.1. These reflects the early assumptions of the difference in subject 
response distributions between shading types.  For all scenes settings “White Screens” results 
in the worst median rating, whereas “No Shading” and “Big Blinds” are overall resulting in the 
most positive median values. This is a simplified way of detecting the origin of the differences, 
however a more precise way of doing so is by running Wilcoxon signed-rank test on the dif-
ferent combinations of related groups. Hence this test should be conducted in future work. 
 
Amount of View based on Shading Conditions 
In the first experiment the effect of the independent variable amount of view (based on shad-
ing conditions) on the dependent variable subjective responses is investigated. The general 
hypothesis investigated in this experiment are as follows: 
 
To define the amount of view based on shading conditions the connectedness of each scene 
have been calculated according to the methodology described in section 3.5.3. A scene with 
no shading is included since it represents the greatest possible connectedness for this specific 
room and window geometry with no obstructions of the view.

4.2.2 Second Experiment 
 
Subjective Responses 
In the second experiment the relation between view perception and view access is investi-
gated. The scenes investigated and the related distribution of subjective responses is for ques-
tion A shown in Figure 93 and for question D shown in Figure 94. All scenes investigated have 
a high view quality and luminance conditions according to the 30th of May at 08:00. 
 
 
Figure 93: Distribution of second experiment subjective responses to user assessment question A. 
 
==PAGE:175==
RESULTS – CHAPTER 4 
152 
 
Figure 93 shows that the overall worst rated scene for question A is Big Blinds with 62° up-
wards tilted slats since the subjects only rated the scene “3. Neither”, “4. Unpleasant”, and 
“5. Very unpleasant”. The second worst rated scene is Vertical Louvres with a slat rotation of 
62° East. However, the subjective responses are distributed across all answer options but with 
more answers in the “4. Unpleasant” and “5. Very unpleasant” categories. 
The most positive rated scene is Big Blinds with 0° tilted slats where >60% of the subjects 
rated the scene “1. Very pleasant”. This scene is followed by the scene Big Blinds with 31° 
upwards tilted slats and Vertical Louvres with 0° rotated slats, that has received close to an 
equal distribution of subjective responses. 
 
 
Figure 94: Distribution of second experiment subjective responses to user assessment question D. 
 
Figure 94 shows that the subjective responses are overall distributed by the same tendency 
for question D and question A. In other words, the positive and negative responses are for 
each scene distributed following the same tendency, when the subjects are asked about the 
perceived pleasantness of the scene and their thoughts about working under the presented 
conditions for longer periods.

jects. This could either be caused by coincidence or indicate that the subjects are a little more 
skeptical when they are asked about their opinion on working under these conditions for 
longer periods rather than the actual pleasantness of the scene.

DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
164 
 
5 DISCUSSION 
 
5.1 First Experiment 
 
5.1.1 Contrast  
As stated in the result section the results for achromatic contrast were based on the prelimi-
nary studies expected to have higher values for scenes set in April than the equivalent scene 
set in May. However, this turned out not to be the case for the final results. A few thoughts 
on why that might be are presented before the outcome of the statistical analysis is presented 
and discussed. First of all, the preliminary studies (see section 3.2.7) were conducted based 
on squared 90° images where the final studies were conducted based on rectangular images 
(170° horizontally and 142° vertically). This means that a larger area of the scene is included 
in the final study compared to in the preliminary study and this could potentially affect the 
results. Another potential cause is, that the preliminary study was conducted based on a 
scene with No Shading rather than on all scene types investigated in the final experiment. For 
the final results the achromatic values for No Shading are still higher for scenes set in April 
compared to scenes set in May like expected, and likewise for the Screen scenes. The remain-
ing solar shading types, Vertical Louvres and Blinds obtains unexpected results. This could be 
because Vertical Louvres and Blinds distributes the light in a different way depending on their 
slat angles and the position of the sun.  
 
Testing the null hypothesis “Contrast does not affect people’s perception of view” the results 
indicate that there is a statistically significant association between people’s perception of the 
view and achromatic contrast. For question A the correlation is weakly positive with no sta-
tistical significance whereas for question D the correlation is weakly negative with no statisti-
cal significance. This means that the preference of achromatic contrast changes a lot across 
the presented scenes, hence achromatic contrast is expected not to be a variable influencing 
the subjective responses. This result could potentially be misleading if the dataset violates the 
assumption of a monotonic relationship. This is because the Kendall’s tau-b test assumes a 
==PAGE:188==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
165 
 
monotonic relationship between the data and would potentially miss out on some of the re-
lations in a non-monotonic relationship. Furthermore, it is noticeable that the photometric 
values are determined based on HDR images rather than LDR images (presented to the sub-
jects in VR). This is a choice made since the experiment is set up to detect shading systems 
visual efficiency and performance in real-world environments. Hence it is desirable to take 
real-world dynamic ranges into account (HDR), rather than the displayable equipment range 
(LDR). This specific choice could however lead to the results being misleading. When Tone 
Mapping an image some of the dynamic ranges change from high to low. It can therefore not 
be ruled out that a different conclusion could have been drawn comparing the photometric 
values of an LDR image with subjective responses. To make more accurate conclusions, a 
comparison between the result following each procedure of HDR- and LDR images should be 
investigated. This is applicable for all results comparing photometric values with subjective 
responses.  
  
Testing the null hypothesis “Contrast does not affect people’s perception of view” the results 
also indicate that there is a statistically significant association between people’s perception 
of the view and chromatic contrast. For question A the correlation is medium to strong, it is 
positive, and there is a statistical significance whereas for question D the correlation is weak 
to medium, it is positive, and there is a statistical significance. This means that an increase in 
chromatic contrast will result in a decrease of positive subjective responses, hence the sub-
jects do not like high chromatic contrast in the scenes. A reason for this could be that a view 
with high contrast tires out the HVS, which would result in the HVQ scene (low chromatic 
contrast scene) being preferred over the LVQ scene (high chromatic contrast scene). How-
ever, since scenes with HVQ is preferred over scenes with LVQ the tendency of chromatic 
contrast being associated with subjective responses could be misleading. Based on this it 
could as well be an indication of people preferring one specific view type over the other rather 
than the chromatic contrast in the scenes. To create more certain results, scenes with chang-
ing chromatic contrast levels should be created as a separate independent variable rather 
than as a confounding variable. A potential way of solving this issue could be by investigating 
the effect of different colored solar shadings. 
 
==PAGE:189==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
166 
 
5.1.2 Visual Complexity 
Two analyses were conducted investigating the effect of respectively achromatic complexity 
and chromatic complexity on subjective responses. To investigate the visual complexity the 
null hypothesis “Visual complexity does not affect people’s perception of view” were set up. 
Testing this hypothesis for achromatic complexity, the results came out indicating that there 
is a significant association between people’s perception of the view and the achromatic visual 
complexity of the scene at a 95% confidence level. For both question A and D the correlation 
is weak to medium, it is negative, and it is statistically significant. When testing this hypothesis 
for chromatic complexity, the results came out indicating that there is a significant association 
between people’s perception of the view and the chromatic visual complexity of the scene at 
a 95% confidence level. For question A the correlation is medium negative and statistically 
significant, whereas for question D the correlation is medium to strong, it is negative, and it 
is statistically significant.  
Both results for achromatic and chromatic complexity indicates that people respond more 
positive the higher the visual complexity of a scene is. Since the visual complexity is described 
by the standard deviation, it suggests that the subjects prefer a wide range of spectral colors 
and light levels. In the above section, it was described that chromatic contrast was discovered 
to be negatively associated with people’s perception of the view. Hence complementary col-
ors are preferred but only if their locations are far enough from each other in the perceived 
scene. This is under the assumption that the findings of the association between subjective 
responses and respectively chromatic contrast and complexity are correct.  
 
5.1.3 Shading Conditions & Amount of View 
Two analyses were conducted investigating the effect of respectively shading conditions and 
the amount of view on subjective responses.  
To investigate the amount of view the null hypothesis “The amount of view does not affect 
people’s perception of view” were set up. Testing this hypothesis, the results came out indi-
cating that there is a statistically significant association between people’s perception of the 
view depending on the amount of view available in the scene. For both question A and D, the 
correlation is strongly negative and statistically significant.  However, the result of the test is 
==PAGE:190==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
167 
 
challenged by the fact, that Big Blinds 0° are rated more positive than Small Binds 0° even 
though they have the same available amount of view, see Table 21. To make sure that this 
change is not detected because of changes in chromatic contrast or visual complexity, these 
calculated values have been compared between Small- and Big Blinds. From Figure 83 and 
Figure 84 it can be seen that Big Blinds in average have a higher chromatic contrast than Small 
Blinds. Since chromatic contrast has shown to be negatively associated with the subjective 
responses, it is not because of the chromatic contrast levels the subjects perceive Big Blinds 
more positive than Small Blinds. For both achromatic- and chromatic complexity (see Figure 
85, Figure 86, Figure 87, and Figure 88),  Big Blinds and Small Blinds have for most scenes 
approximately the same values. Therefore, the difference in subjective responses between 
Big- and Small Blinds are most likely not a result of different levels of visual complexity. Since 
this difference in subjective responses is neither caused by visual complexity, chromatic con-
trast, nor amount of view, it is expectedly due to either extraneous variables or the shading 
geometry. For the Small Blinds, it is not possible to observe the view without the foveal vision 
being interrupted whereas for the Big Blinds it is possible to observe the view with no inter-
ruption of the foveal vision. If shading geometry is what causes the change in subjective re-
sponses between Big- and Small Blinds, this could indicate that a shading type is preferred 
when it is possible to observe the view without interruption of the foveal vision. This is how-
ever just one out of many possible suggestions as to what the reason might be behind the 
observed difference in subjective responses. To determine the exact reason this should be 
investigated further. Another noticeable observation is that LVQ scenes mainly showed to be 
positively associated with the subjective responses. In the first place, this was not expected 
since a lot of subjects verbally complained during the experiment that the building in the LVQ 
scenes was too close by. Hence, they indicated that they liked less access to view in exchange 
for feeling less exposed. The result nevertheless suggested that people still prefer more view 
in LVQ scenes. This is the same tendency as found for HVQ scenes, see Figure 79, Figure 80, 
& Table 21, but in general, the subjects replied less positive to LVQ scenes comparing the two.  
  
To investigate the shading conditions the null hypothesis “Different shading conditions does 
not affect people’s perception of the view” were set up. Testing this hypothesis, the results 
came out indicating that the different shading conditions do affect people’s perception of the 
==PAGE:191==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
168 
 
view rejecting the null hypothesis at a 5% significance level. That this significance occurs only 
because of the shading conditions should however be investigated, which is possible by com-
paring Vertical Louvres and Big Blinds. These shading types are almost identical since they 
have the same size, geometry, and distance between slats (see section 3.3.2) however, Verti-
cal Louvres are vertical and Big Blinds are horizontal. As stated in section 4.2.1 Vertical Louvres 
are overall rated more negatively than Big Blinds. To make sure that the change in subjective 
responses is not detected because of changes in chromatic contrast, visual complexity nor 
amount of view, these calculated values were compared between Vertical Louvres and Big 
Blinds. From Figure 83 and Figure 84 it can be seen that Big Blinds in average have lower 
chromatic contrast than Vertical Louvres. Since chromatic contrast has shown to be negatively 
associated with subjective responses, the difference in subjective responses could potentially 
also be caused by chromatic contrast levels. Furthermore, Big Blinds have both higher values 
for chromatic and achromatic complexity compared to Vertical Louvres (see Figure 85, Figure 
86, Figure 87, and Figure 88). Since visual complexity has shown to be positively associated 
with subjective responses, the difference in subjective responses could potentially also be 
caused by visual complexity levels. Additionally, the amount of view available is greater for 
Big Blinds than Vertical Louvres, Table 21.  Therefore, the change in subjective responses 
might also be caused by the available amount of view.  
Since both: chromatic contrast, visual complexity, and amount of view could potentially affect 
the difference in subjective responses observed, it is not possible to conclude that shading 
geometry and thereby appearance of the shading exclusively causes the observed change. 
However, the confounding variables are depending on the i.v., hence changes in the i.v. will 
also result in changes in the confounding variables. Changing the experimental design so con-
trast and visual complexity are investigated as i.v. could result in more conclusive results.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:192==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
169 
 
5.2 Second Experiment 
 
5.2.1 Amount of View based on Slat Angles 
To investigate the amount of view based on slat angles the null hypothesis “The amount of 
view does not affect people’s perception of view” were set up. One of the essential Chi-square 
assumptions turned out to be violated, hence the results can at a first glance not be consid-
ered reliable.  
In the first experiment, the amount of view showed to be associated with perceived pleasant-
ness of the view at a 5% significance level. However, this is disregarding the possible influence 
of confounding- or extraneous variables. Because of this, the results in the second experiment 
are assumed reliable, regardless of the violated assumption. The results hence (once more) 
indicates that there is a statistically significant association between people’s perception of 
the view and the amount of view available in the scene. Furthermore, it indicates that the 
correlation is strong and negative. 
The strength of the correlation is greater when investigating the amount of view “based on 
slat angles” (second experiment) rather than “shading type” (first experiment). This is ex-
pected because of the reduced risk of others confounding- or independent variables influenc-
ing the subjective responses, since only HVQ 30th of May 08:00 is investigated. It could be 
argued that the results are therefore only representative for HVQ scenes since less view avail-
able could be suggested as preferable in LVQ scenes. However, looking at Figure 79, Figure 
80, and Table 21 both HVQ and LVQ appear to have a positive correlation between the 
amount of view available and the perceived pleasantness of the view. It is therefore expected 
that similar tendencies would occur comparing LVQ and HVQ scenes in this experiment, 
where the correlation is however smaller in size for LVQ than for HVQ. 
 
 
==PAGE:193==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
170 
 
5.3 Third Experiment 
 
5.3.1 Greenery Conditions 
To investigate the relationship between subjective responses and greenery conditions the null 
hypothesis “Greenery as shading and natural greenery in the view has the same effect on 
people’s perception of the view” were set up. By testing the hypothesis, it can be concluded, 
that all tests for “matched pairs” of question D fail to reject the null hypothesis at a 5% signif-
icance level. In question A all “matched pairs” with LVQ scenes likewise fail to reject the null 
hypothesis at a 5 % significance level, whereas “matched pairs” with HVQ scenes reject the 
null hypothesis at a 5% significance level.  
In the first- and second experiments it was concluded that the association between the 
amount of view and subjective responses is significant, and that the correlation is positive. In 
the third experiment the density of greenery as shutters is measured in the amount of view 
(connectedness). The two types of shutters investigated has respectively high and low den-
sity. According to the findings in the first- and second experiments it was expected that more 
of the tests would result in rejection of the null hypothesis. However, only HVQ scenes in 
question A resulted in such rejection. Furthermore, all tests conducted on question D failed 
to reject the null hypothesis. Question D represents an office setting hence it is considered a 
more accurate measure of people’s perception of view in a work-related environment.  
The tests failing to reject the null hypothesis (despite variations in view access) might be an 
indication of people perceiving the shutters with natural green as a part of the view. Further-
more, these results indicate that there is a potential for natural green as shading substituting 
natural green in the view. This is especially in a work-related environment. Since there is a 
potential for natural green as shading substituting natural green in the view, further analysis 
should be conducted to strengthen this assumption, and in the long-term ideas for the devel-
opment of such shading systems should be investigated. 
 
 
==PAGE:194==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
171 
 
5.4 General 
The selection of methods used to conduct hypothesis testing is very important. This since it 
determines the accuracy and the detail level of the conclusions. The data analyzed in this 
thesis is ordinal and non-parametric, and since the assumptions of a Chi-square test suited 
the most experimental datasets, this is one of the methods selected for hypothesis testing. 
Due to the Chi-square assumption of maximum 20% cells having an expected count less than 
5 it was however not possible to separate the data for analysis to get more accurate conclu-
sions. Looking at for example achromatic complexity the result might indicate that there is a 
statistically significant association to the subjective responses at a 95% confidence level. How-
ever, since the data is analyzed combined it is hard to tell if this is the full explanation. By 
separating the analysis data into smaller groups, it might result in certain groups not showing 
a significant association to the subjective responses whereas others show a strong significant 
association. In other words, the result based on combined data might indicate something else 
than the results based on separated data, since the separated data would lead to more accu-
rate conclusions.  
This is however not possible, since a larger sample size is required with this specific distribu-
tion of subjective responses due to the assumption of “expected count”. Therefore, more 
accurate results could only be obtained if the data had been collected differently. 
Collecting the data as interval scale or similar, other more accurate methods for hypothesis 
testing could potentially be selected, assuming the data is normally distributed (parametric 
data). To quantify the data as scale data, only the first and last points should be defined. With 
the setup for the collection of subjective responses presented in section 3.6.2, it would be 
possible to substitute the tags with numbers from 1-5 only defining nr 1 and nr 5. However, 
in Pilot 2 some subjects criticized this procedure since they had a hard time defining the num-
bers.  
 
 
==PAGE:195==
DISCUSSION – CHAPTER 5 
172 
 
5.4.1 Partial Conclusion 
For Experiment 1 and 2 (see section 5.1 & 5.2) a significant relation were found between sub-
jective responses and respectively chromatic contrast, achromatic complexity, chromatic 
complexity and amount of view. Hench they are determined to be adequate measures for 
characterization of view out. 
In Experiment 3 (section 5.3) the results showed no significant difference subjective re-
sponses of scenes with greenery in the view and scenes with greenery as shading. Hence it is 
suggested that greenery as shading can substitute greenery in the view. 
 
 
==PAGE:196==
CONCLUSION – CHAPTER 6 
173 
 
6 CONCLUSION 
The conclusion is divided into the sections “Characterization of View Out”, “Greenery as Shad-
ing” and “Future Research and Inspiration”. In the first section, the findings of the first and 
second experiments are presented related to the main research-question. In the second ex-
periment findings of the third experiment is presented related to the secondary research-
question. In the third and final section, the foundation of future research and inspiration is 
presented. 
 
6.1 Characterization of View Out 
So far methods have been developed to quantify energy efficiency and to some extent visual 
comfort, however, only a limited range of methods are available for quantification of visual 
efficiency and performance of a view for appropriate solar shading and solar control selection. 
The main objective of this thesis is therefore to fill out this gap in the field of research. The 
research-question investigated is as follows: 
 “Is photometric values and amount of view applicable for characterization of view out with 
the purpose of appropriate solar shading and solar control selection?”  
Based on the findings in this study it is concluded that the photometric values (chromatic 
complexity, chromatic contrast, & achromatic contrast), and the amount of view are adequate 
measures for characterization of view out. The variables investigated in this partial study are 
categorized as independent- and confounding variables and they are analyzed in relation to 
the dependent variable subjective responses.  
To assess contrast of a view the RAMMG methodology created by Rizzi et al. was determined 
as adequate [3]. This assumption was drawn based on a study by S. Rockcastle & M. Andersen 
where RAMMG among other methods was concluded to best reflect the human perception 
of contrast [33]. Furthermore, the assumption is drawn because it is a multileveled method-
ology that represent the functionality of the HVS. The methodology of RAMMGL is used to 
==PAGE:197==
CONCLUSION – CHAPTER 6 
174 
 
describe achromatic contrast whereas RAMMGLab is used to describe chromatic contrast. See 
section 2.6.1 for background information and 3.5.1 for methodology.  
The visual complexity of a view can be defined as the sharpness of a view. According to a 
study conducted by Zaikina et al. the quantification method RMS by Pavel et al. was pointed 
out as the best method to define the view sharpness and object visibility of an image [37] [4]. 
Hence the RMS method is used to investigate the visual complexity. The methodology RMSL 
is used to describe achromatic complexity whereas RMSLab is used to describe chromatic com-
plexity [4]. See section 2.6.2 for background information and 3.5.2 for methodology. 
To assess the amount of view available in a scene a raytracing method is applied where rays 
are connected, if possible, from point of view to a sky sphere. The script used to conduct the 
analysis created by Mikkel Kofod Pedersen is based on a research study by Irmak Turan and 
C. F. Reinhart where internal visual connectivity is assessed [13]. See section 2.3.1 for back-
ground information and 3.5.3 for methodology. 
The subjective responses were collected by conducting a VR experiment. The experiment pre-
sented to the subjects covers three individual experiments, where the first- and second ex-
periment is presented here. In the first experiment contrast visual complexity, shading condi-
tions and amount of view (based on shading type) were assessed. In the second experiment, 
the amount of view (based on slat angles) was assessed. An overview of the first experiment 
is found in section 3.8.1 and for the second experiment in section 3.8.2.  
 
In total 32 subjects participated in the experiment and 26 scenes (first and second experi-
ment) were created for conduction of VR user assessment. Each scene was modelled in Rhino 
and rendered with Radiance. In the first experiment 5 shading conditions were investigated 
(No Shading, Small Blinds, Big Blinds, Vertical Louvres & White Screens), and in the second 
experiment 2 shading conditions were investigated (Big Blinds & Vertical Louvres).  
In the first experiment each shading condition was investigated for the independent variable 
“View Quality” at two levels (HVQ & LVQ) and likewise “Luminance Contrast” at two levels 
(14th of April 14:00 & 30th of May 08:00). In the second experiment, all scenes were investi-
gated with HVQ on 30th of May 08:00. The background for the selection of view quality is 
==PAGE:198==
CONCLUSION – CHAPTER 6 
175 
 
found in section 2.2.1 and the methodology is described in section 3.2.6. The selection of 
luminance contrast is described in the methodology section 3.2.7. 
Based on the analysis conducted it was proven that people with a 95% confidence level prefer 
high visual complexity levels, high amounts of view available, and low chromatic contrast lev-
els.  
For the chromatic contrast, this finding is however a bit uncertain since the chromatic con-
trasts of the scenes are relative to the view quality of the scenes. Hence, the finding of people 
preferring views with low chromatic contrast could as well be an indication of people prefer-
ring high-quality views. However, it is assumed assessing the results, that the findings occur 
due to changes in chromatic contrast.  
Since people prefer low chromatic contrast but high chromatic complexity it is suggested that 
complementary colors are preferred but only if they are located far enough from each other 
in the perceived scene.  
Furthermore, the results in this study suggest that an interruption of the foveal vision could 
potentially be the cause of one shading type being preferred over another, and that shading 
geometry and thereby shading appearance is not determined to exclusively cause a significant 
relation to the perceived pleasantness of a view. 
In general, people prefer a high amount of view. It is additionally observed that this prefer-
ence occurs for both HVQ and LVQ scenes, hence people like high amounts of view even 
though the view is not attractive and maybe even makes the subjects feel exposed. 
The preference of achromatic contrast changes a lot across different perceived scenes. Hence 
there is no significant positive nor negative correlation between achromatic contrast and sub-
jective responses with a 95% confidence level. 
Based on the above findings, it is concluded that there is a relation between photometric 
values & amount of view, and characterization of view out. It is therefore concluded that the 
photometric values & amount of view are adequate measures for characterization of view 
out.  
==PAGE:199==
CONCLUSION – CHAPTER 6 
176 
 
All the findings and suggestions above are however assuming that limiting and uncertain fac-
tors have not affected the results. These potential factors are described in the discussion sec-
tion 5 and listed in the limitations section 7. Suggestions for future supporting research is 
presented in the future work section 8. 
 
6.2 Greenery as Shading 
When determining the quality of a view, it is evaluated more positive when it contains natural 
green rather than when it does not. Since a view out is broadly not changeable, elements that 
could potentially change the perception of a view without changing the view itself becomes 
interesting. The benefits of shutters with greenery are yet undiscovered despite its potential 
ability to both raise the view quality and protect from any unwanted sunrays. The secondary 
objective of this thesis is therefore to fill out this gap in the field of research. The research-
question investigated are as follows:  
“Can greenery as shading substitute natural greenery in the view?”  
The findings in this study determine that greenery as shading can substitute natural green in 
the view. The independent variable greenery condition is analyzed in relation to the depend-
ent variable subjective responses. The view conditions investigated are compared based on 
the amount of view available in a scene. Likewise, for the first and second experiments this is 
calculated based on a connectedness script by Mikkel Kofod Pedersen. See section 2.3.1 for 
background information and 3.5.3 for methodology. 
The subjective responses were collected by conducting a VR experiment. The experiment pre-
sented to the subjects covers three individual experiments, whereas the third experiment is 
presented here. In the third and final experiment different greenery conditions were assessed 
to determine whether greenery as shading can substitute natural green in the view for view 
quality ratings. An overview of the third experiment is found in section 3.2.4. 
In total 32 subjects participated in the experiment and 12 scenes (third experiment) were 
created for the conduction of VR user assessment. Each scene was modelled in Rhino and 
rendered with Radiance. In the third and final experiment 4 shading conditions were investi-
gated (No Shading – without trees, No Shading – with trees, High Density Shutters, and Low 
==PAGE:200==
CONCLUSION – CHAPTER 6 
177 
 
Density Shutters). For each shading condition the independent variable “View Quality” were 
investigated at two levels (HVQ & LVQ) and likewise “Luminance Contrast” at two levels (14th 
of April 14:00 & 30th of May 08:00). The background for the selection of view quality is found 
in section 2.2.1 and the methodology is described in section 3.2.6. The selection of luminance 
contrast is described in the methodology section 3.2.7. 
Based on the analysis conducted it was proven that there is no statistical difference in the 
distribution of subjective responses comparing greenery as shading and natural green in the 
view. This is at a 95% confidence level. Hence it is concluded that there is potential for natural 
green as shading substituting natural green in the view obtaining the same view quality rating. 
Since this conclusion can be drawn despite variations in view access it is suggested that people 
perceive the shading with natural green as part of the view.  
 
6.3 Future Research and Inspiration 
This thesis and future supporting research will create the base for the development of an 
index for appropriate solar shading and solar control selection based on visual efficiency and 
performance. Today the only quantification methods used for solar shading and solar control 
selection is energy efficiency and to some extent visual comfort. Hence the visual efficiency 
and performance of a window view are not covered despite its strong relation to the per-
ceived pleasantness of the occupant. An index covering visual efficiency and performance will 
close this gap and the missing link for solar shading and solar control selection.   
With this thesis we hope to make people understand the importance and value of expanding 
the bases upon which conclusions are drawn for solar shading and solar control selection. 
More generally we hope this thesis inspires people within the building industry to always ex-
pand their horizon and the basis upon which decisions are made. This is because buildings are 
built for humans and the larger the foundation is for decision making the larger the chances 
are for creating a building that reflects health and wellbeing with humans as the focal point. 
 
 
==PAGE:201==
LIMITATIONS– CHAPTER 7 
178 
 
7 LIMITATIONS 
In this limitation, section awareness is drawn to the limitations of this study. 
▪ When tone mapping the 6 images of each scene a gamma value of 2.2 is used. This 
value is selected based on the study “Real versus photograph-based virtual environ-
ments” conducted by Kynthia Chamilothori and because the default gamma value for 
similar image converters as ra_tiff and ra_bmp is 2.2 (see section 3.4.6). However, the 
optimal way of selecting a gamma value for tone mapping is through investigating the 
specific VR-headset used, which in this study is the HTC VIVE PRO EYE. Such an inves-
tigation covers for displaying different uniform grayscale scenes and measuring the 
associated luminance values, which is a procedure described in the mentioned study 
by Kynthia Chamilothori [51]. 
▪ When using tone mapping the overall brightness should be considered. In real-life-
settings different directions often have a high difference in brightness which requires 
substantial adaptation of the eye. When tone mapping an image, it is adjusted based 
on the average luminance and the eye is only required to adapt to small differences in 
brightness compared to real life. 
▪ When using image projection on the floor, it was assumed that all RGB reflections 
occurred based on the reflectance determined in the projection code. Hence the re-
flectance was determined to be 0.20 according to the Danish Building Code. However, 
later it was discovered that the reflectance occurs as a combination of the values de-
fined in the code and the chromatic properties of the image projected. Because of 
this, the RGB reflections should have been 1 instead of 0.20 to reflect the actual prop-
erties of this specific wooden floor. This means that the floor appears wooden but has 
the properties of a dark carpet. 
▪ When determining whether the contrast and complexity analyzes should be con-
ducted based on LDR or HDR images pros and cons belonging to each image type 
should be considered.  HDR images have a dynamic range that reflects reality whereas 
LDR images reflect only a small range of the real-life dynamic range. However, the LDR 
images are used conducting the VR experiments which is the root of the subjective 
==PAGE:202==
LIMITATIONS– CHAPTER 7 
179 
 
responses collected.  In this study the contrast and complexity analysis are conducted 
based on the HDR images so that real-life contrast and complexity is taken into con-
sideration. 
▪ Collecting the demographic information, the subjects were asked to answer if their 
eyes were limited by any eye disease(s). However, a lot of limitations could potentially 
occur related to the eye, that are not identified as an actual disease. These could be 
limitations caused by color blindness, prisms in the eye, etc. Those parameters could 
have an impact on the overall limitations of the experiment group and should opti-
mally have been covered. Furthermore, subject ignorance in connection with demo-
graphic information collection is not counted for. 
▪ Due to the high rendering time and rendering complications 5/38 scenes was ready 
for the first scheduled subject user assessments. Therefore, all four high-density shut-
ters and Small Blinds-HVQ-053008 does not have as many subjective responses as the 
remaining scenes. Considering that the first 3 scenes were excluded from data assess-
ment, only 14 subjects responded to each of the high-density scenes and 11 subjects 
responded to the Small Blinds-HVQ-053008. The remaining scenes had up to 32 regis-
tered responses. Hence the distribution of subjective responses might be less accurate 
for high-density scenes and Small Blinds-HVQ-053008 since the accuracy grow the 
larger the sample size is. 
▪ In general, a larger sample size would have provided more accurate and detailed re-
sults. In this thesis, the Chi-square turned out to be appropriate for hypothesis testing 
for most tests. However, the assumption “maximum 20% of the cells have an expected 
count less than 5” gets violated if the data is asset in smaller separated groups rather 
than in larger combined groups. More than 100 subjects are expectedly needed to be 
able to separate the data to obtain more accurate results using this method. Other 
methods are available. However, they require a new structure for the data collection 
to be adequate. 
▪ The full quality of a view perceived outside of a window is defined as a correlation 
between content, access, and clarity, see section 2.1. The view content is in this study 
covered by the term view quality defined by Hellinga and Hordijk [12]. The view access 
is covered by the Internal Visual Connectivity introduced by Irmak Turan and C. F. Rein-
hart [13]. At last, view clarity could partially be covered by ‘View Lumen’ developed 
==PAGE:203==
LIMITATIONS– CHAPTER 7 
180 
 
by John Mardaljevic [19]. However, this is yet still only a theoretical concept, and it 
has been excluded from assessment in this study.  
▪ To assign the glazing material of the windows, the right properties of the transmis-
sivity is needed. The most accurate method for setting up the glazing would be mod-
elling each layer of glazing separately instead of combining them into one. This is be-
cause the fall in visible light transmittance might be underpredicted (when the glaz-
ing’s are combined) at high incidence angles of the sun [58]. As a simplification the 
materials are defined and modelled as one layer since the properties of the glazing is 
not defined for each layer separately by the manufacturers.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:204==
FUTURE WORK – CHAPTER 8 
181 
 
8 FUTURE WORK 
In this section, suggestions are provided for potential future research topics along with rec-
ommendations for improvement of the current study procedures. The suggestions and rec-
ommendations are based on the discussion and limitation sections of this study. 
▪ In this thesis, not all variables investigated are independent since some are confound-
ing. This is a simplification of the study to limit the extent of the thesis and thereby 
the number of scenes interpreted. However, more accurate and detailed conclusions 
could be drawn by creating confounding variables as separate independent variables. 
Hence an extended format of this study is suggested as future work. Since the con-
founding variables investigated are chromatic contrast and chromatic complexity, the 
extension of this study could involve different colors of shading devices.  
▪ Conducting the VR experiments, the subjects were asked to answer four questions 
with five answer options, see section 3.6.2. The analysis of the results were however 
simplified by only addressing question A & D. Question B & C could potentially help 
explain some of the tendencies for amount of view and the preferred shading condi-
tions and should therefore be assessed in future work. 
▪ High density shutters in the third experiment had noticeable mold occurring in the 
renderings. This was most obvious in the back of the room however the subjects might 
still have noticed. Hence future work should involve reduction of mold present in all 
investigated scenes. The mold could potentially be reduced by choosing a better ren-
dering quality with the rpict command e.g. -ab 5 instead of -ab 4.  
▪ In this study, the radiance rpict rendering commands were not optimized based on 
computational time rather only based on the appearance of the renderings. Hence 
before conducting any further research it is recommended that these commands are 
optimized based on computational time. 
▪ As stated in the limitation section a gamma value of 2.2 was used when tone mapping 
the HDR images. The selection of this value was partly based on a study conducted by 
Kynthia Chamilothori [51], however, the VR headset used in the reference study was 
==PAGE:205==
FUTURE WORK – CHAPTER 8 
182 
 
not the same as the one used in this study. In future work, it is therefore recom-
mended to investigate if this value is in accordance with the properties of HTC VIVE 
PRO EYE. Such an investigation covers for displaying different uniform grayscale 
scenes and measuring the associated luminance values, which is a procedure de-
scribed in the mentioned study by Kynthia Chamilothori [51] 
▪ Before any further research, it is recommended to detect the exact consequence of 
modelling the window with one layer of glazing rather than three layers of glazing. 
This is because the fall in visible light transmittance might be underpredicted (when 
the glazing’s are combined) at high incidence angles of the sun [58]. 
▪ As a suggestion for another potential indicator for characterization of the view out, 
View Lumen might prove relevant for future investigations. This investigation would 
contain further development of the method and theory described in section 2.1 and 
comparison with subjective responses. 
▪ In the first experiment, the relation between subjective responses (d.v.) and shading 
conditions (i.v.) is investigated using the Friedmann Test. Moreover, the calculated 
medians in the results are used for further interpretation as a simplification. For fur-
ther interpretation of the results, it is recommended to perform an effect size test 
using the Wilcoxon Signed-rank test to detect specific significant relations and 
whether the difference is positive or negative. 
▪ Based on the results of this study it is suggested that an interruption of the foveal 
vision could potentially be the cause of one shading type being preferred over an-
other. However, this is just a suggestion and for more certain conclusions this should 
be investigated further. It is for future work therefore recommended to set up a test 
that specifically investigates this. 
▪ The results of the third experiment in the conducted thesis indicated that greenery as 
shading has the same effect on people’s perception of a view as natural green in the 
view. Hence there is a potential for further analysis of greenery as shading substituting 
natural green in the view. This finding means that views with no natural green in the 
view could potentially obtain a higher view quality by applying greenery as shading.  
▪ In this thesis, the photometric values are determined based on two levels of view qual-
ity and luminance contrast for different shading types. Future development of an in-
dex would however benefit from knowing how to categorize the calculated visual 
==PAGE:206==
FUTURE WORK – CHAPTER 8 
183 
 
complexity & contrast (e.g. high, medium, or low). This could be achieved by analyzing 
a series of scenarios for view quality and luminance contrast instead of only two. 
▪ When assessing “visual efficiency and performance” an interesting aspect to consider 
would be people’s ability to perform in a work-related connection under certain pho-
tometric conditions and at certain levels of the available amount of view. This could 
be done by conducting a performance test at different levels of chromatic contrast, 
achromatic contrast, chromatic complexity, achromatic complexity, and amount of 
view. Any findings of such a study should be compared with findings in this thesis study 
to detect whether view preferences have any link to work-related performance.

# Radiance Material Library

# in the material selection pull down menus. Please note that it is up  
# to you to make sure that the Radiance material is correctly defined as

# copy of this file before modifying it.

10.9 Subject Feedback 
 
 
Hvad var din største 
bekymring eller frygt ved at 
deltage i eksperimentet?
Hvis der er noget ved fremgangsmåden af 
eksperimentet, du synes kunne forbedres, hvad er det?
Hvis der er noget ved 
spørgsmålene der 
præsenteres i VR, du 
synes kunne forbedres, 
hvad er det?
Hvor meget følte du at 
du ‘var der’ i det 
virtuelle rum?
Andre kommentarer
Subject 1
At man kunne svare forkert
Der var mange scener jeg troede var de samme
Synes spørgsmål nummer 
2 godt kunne uddybes
Rigtig meget
Subject 2
Ingen
1 Spørgsmålene kommer meget langsomt 2 man mangler lidt 
oplysninger om, hvor ens plads er i lokalet, i forhold til den 
sollys der kommer ind 3 man mangler at vide, hvilken vej 
solen den går, i forhold til afskærmning i løbet af en 
arbejdsdag 4 udsigten til vand er jo generel mere tiltrækkende 
end over mod noget bebyggelse - og bebyggelse skærmer i 
sig selv mere end udsigten ud over vand 5 Syns de samme 
spørgsmål/udsigts platforme kom flere, det forvirrede lidt :)
Se tidligere svar ;)
Meget
Ingen
Subject 3
At jeg ville blive dårlig
Meget
Subject 4
At man blev svimmel eller fik 
andet ubehag undervejs
Ikke rigtig noget da jeg efterfølgende fik forklaret hvad forsøget 
gik ud på
Intet
Meget
Spændende at være med i

10.10 Scripts 
 
10.10.1 Matlab Script for the Achromatic Contrast Algorithm 
 
%%%%%RAMMGachromatic%%%%%%%% 
%modified 14.06.21 Mandana S. Khanie 
close all 
clc 
clear 
%the input and output directory (to be changed) 
indir = 'C:\Users\s153335\Desktop\Speciale_2021\Scripts\Man-
dana\Resized_HDR_3k\Resized_HDR_3k\'; 
outdir='C:\Users\s153335\Desktop\Speciale_2021\Scripts\Mandana\Photomet-
ric_Results\'; 
%introducing variables 
caverageall=[]; 
RAMMGLall=[]; 
%reading through the given folder system 
cd(indir); 
dirlist = dir('.'); 
isub = [dirlist(:).isdir]; %# returns logical vector 
nameFolds = {dirlist(isub).name}'; 
nameFolds(ismember(nameFolds,{'.','..'})) = []; 
nfiles = length(nameFolds); 
  
for ff=1:nfiles 
     
    shadingfolder=sprintf('%s%s%s',indir,nameFolds{ff},'\'); 
    cd(shadingfolder) 
    dirlist = dir('.'); 
    isub = [dirlist(:).isdir]; 
    nameFoldss = {dirlist(isub).name}'; 
    nameFoldss(ismember(nameFoldss,{'.','..'})) = []; 
     
    scene=nameFolds{ff}; 
     
    nff=length(nameFoldss); 
     
    for nff=1:length(nameFoldss) 
        timefolder1=sprintf('%s%s%s%s',indir,nameFolds{ff},'\',name-
Foldss{nff}); 
        timefolder2=sprintf('%s%s%s%s%s',indir,nameFolds{ff},'\',name-
Foldss{nff},'\',nameFoldss{nff},'_HDR'); 
        cd(timefolder1) 
        cd(timefolder2) 
         
        time=nameFoldss{nff}; 
        File=dir(fullfile('*.hdr')); 
         
        for kk = 1:6 %the number of images are 6. otherwise "length" can be 
used 
            % %reading the hdr files 
            Files=File(kk,1); 
            imagedata = getfield(Files,'name'); 
            sz=size(imagedata); 
==PAGE:268==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
245 
 
            IMG=imagedata(sz(2)-4:sz(2)-4); 
            %reading different images and building one image for the calcu-
lations 
             
            if strcmp(IMG,'D')==1 
                Down=hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'E')==1 
                East=hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'S')==1 
                South= hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'U')==1 
                Up= hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'W')==1 
                West= hdrread(imagedata); 
            end 
        end 
         
        % cropping images for creating two different images, IMG1 with up 
        % and down head movement, IMG2 without up and down head movement. 
        % using imcrop the images are cropped using the accurate input for 
        % the cropping rectangular region.  % [L,rect]=imcrop(IMGV1) was 
        % used to assit finding the cropping region inputs. 
         
        IMGEast=cat(1,rot90(Up,3),East,rot90(Down)); 
        IMGWest=cat(1,rot90(Up),West,rot90(Down,-1)); 
        IMGSouth=cat(1,rot90(Up,2),South,rot90(Down,2)); 
        IMGV1=cat(2,IMGEast,IMGSouth,IMGWest); 
         
        %         VDDown =rot90(imcrop(Down,[0 2418 3200 782]),2); 
        VDEast=imcrop(East,[1422 0 1777 3200]); 
        %         VDEastDown=rot90((imcrop(rot90(Down,2),[0 0 782 1778])),-
1); 
        %         VDEastUp=rot90(imcrop((rot90(Up,-1)),[0 0 1422 1778])); 
        VDSouth=South; 
        %         VDUp=rot90(imcrop(Up,[0 0 3200 1422]),2); 
        VDWest=imcrop(West,[0 0 1422 3200]); 
        %         VDWestDown=rot90(imcrop(Down,[2418 0 782 3200]),-1); 
        %         VDWestUp=rot90(imcrop((rot90(Up,2)),[2418 0 0 3200])); 
         
         
        IMG1=imcrop(IMGV1,[1314 2202 6768 4836]); 
        IMG2=cat(2,VDEast,VDSouth,VDWest); 
         
        for jj=1:2 
            %runing calculations on through the two images 
            my_field=sprintf('IMG%g',jj); 
            A=eval(my_field); 
            Bori=A; 
            %using RGBI to calculate illuminance 
            RGBi=A; 
            %calculating Illuminance 
            R=RGBi(:,:,1); 
            G=RGBi(:,:,2); 
            B=RGBi(:,:,3); 
            E= 179 * (R * .265 + G * .67 + B * .065); 
            Ea=mean(mean(E)); 
            %transfer the image to CIELab 
            Blabori=rgb2lab(Bori); 
            %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
            %size of the input image-level 1 
==PAGE:269==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
246 
 
            %for achromatic only use Light* 
            Lori=Blabori(:,:,1); 
            k=1; 
            H=100; 
             
            while H >= 6 
                %resize the picture 
                Blab=imresize(Blabori,1/(2^(k-1))); 
                Light=Blab(:,:,1); 
                [H, W]=size(Light); 
                 
                %calculate pixel contrast value c 
                %%weighting factor for neighbors 
                a=1/(4*(sqrt(2)+1)); 
                b=1/(4+2*sqrt(2)); 
                %Corner boundaries (corner pixels) 
                c(1,1)=b*abs(Light(1,2)-Light(1,1))+b*abs(Light(2,1)-
Light(1,1))+a*abs(Light(2,2)-Light(1,1)); 
                c(1,W)=b*abs(Light(1,W-1)-Light(1,W))+a*abs(Light(2,W-1)-
Light(1,W))+b*abs(Light(2,W)-Light(1,W)); 
                c(H,1)=b*abs(Light(H-1,1)-Light(H,1))+a*abs(Light(H-1,2)-
Light(H,1))+b*abs(Light(H,2)-Light(H,1)); 
                c(H,W)=b*abs(Light(H,W-1)-Light(H,W))+b*abs(Light(H-1,W)-
Light(H,W))+a*abs(Light(H-1,W-1)-Light(H,W)); 
                %Edge boundaries (edge pixels) 
                for i=1:1 
                    for j=2:(W-1) 
                        c(1,j)= b*abs(Light(1,j-1)-
Light(1,j))+b*abs(Light(1,j+1)-Light(1,j))+a*abs(Light(2,j-1)-
Light(1,j))+b*abs(Light(2,j)-Light(1,j))+a*abs(Light(2,j+1)-Light(1,j)); 
                    end 
                end 
                 
                for i=H:H 
                    for j=2:(W-1) 
                        c(H,j)= a*abs(Light(H-1,j-1)-
Light(H,j))+b*abs(Light(H-1,j)-Light(H,j))+a*abs(Light(H-1,j+1)-
Light(H,j))+b*abs(Light(H,j-1)-Light(H,j))+b*abs(Light(H,j+1)-Light(H,j)); 
                    end 
                end 
                 
                for j=1:1 
                    for i=2:(H-1) 
                        c(i,1)=b*abs(Light(i-1,1)-
Light(i,1))+a*abs(Light(i-1,2)-Light(i,1))+b*abs(Light(i,2)-
Light(i,1))+b*abs(Light(i+1,1)-Light(i,1))+a*abs(Light(i+1,2)-Light(i,1)); 
                    end 
                end 
                 
                for j=W:W 
                    for i=2:(H-1) 
                        c(i,W)= a*abs(Light(i-1,W-1)-
Light(i,W))+b*abs(Light(i-1,W)-Light(i,W))+b*abs(Light(i,W-1)-
Light(i,W))+a*abs(Light(i+1,W-1)-Light(i,W))+b*abs(Light(i+1,W)-
Light(i,W)); 
                    end 
                end 
                %centers (remaining pixels that are not corner pixels) 
                for i=2:(H-1) 
                    for j=2:(W-1) 
==PAGE:270==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
247 
 
                        c(i,j)= a*abs(Light(i-1,j-1)-
Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i-1,j)-Light(i,j))+a*abs(Light(i-1,j+1)-
Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i,j-1)-Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i,j+1)-
Light(i,j))+a*abs(Light(i+1,j-1)-Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i+1,j)-
Light(i,j))+a*abs(Light(i+1,j+1)-Light(i,j)); 
                    end 
                end 
                %mean value of the pixel contrast at each level 
                caverage(k)=mean2(c); 
                c=[]; 
                k=k+1; 
            end 
            %collecting data 
            caverageall=[caverageall;caverage]; 
            RAMMGL=mean(caverageall')'; 
            RAMMGL=mean(RAMMGL); 
            RAMMGLcollect=[{scene},{time},{my_field},{RAMMGL},{Ea}]; 
            RAMMGLall=[RAMMGLall;RAMMGLcollect]; 
        end 
        % saving the mat file and xlsx file 
        filename= sprintf('%s%s',outdir,'RAMMG_achromatic\'); 
        save(filename,'RAMMGLall') 
        filename2 = sprintf('%s%s',outdir,'Contrast_achromatic.xlsx'); 
        writecell(RAMMGLall,filename2) 
    end 
end

        cd(timefolder2)

        File=dir(fullfile('*.hdr')); 
         
        for kk = 1:6 %the number of images are 6. otherwise "length" can be 
used 
             
            % %reading the hdr files 
            Files=File(kk,1); 
            imagedata = getfield(Files,'name'); 
            sz=size(imagedata); 
            IMG=imagedata(sz(2)-4:sz(2)-4);

249 
 
             
             %reading different images and building one image for the cal-
culations 
            if strcmp(IMG,'D')==1 
                Down=hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'E')==1 
                East=hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'S')==1 
                South= hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'U')==1 
                Up= hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'W')==1 
                West= hdrread(imagedata); 
            end 
        end 
         
        % cropping images for creating two different images, IMG1 with up 
        % and down head movement, IMG2 without up and down head movement. 
        % using imcrop the images are cropped using the accurate input for 
        % the cropping rectangular region.  % [L,rect]=imcrop(IMGV1) was 
        % used to assit finding the cropping region inputs. 
         
        IMGEast=cat(1,rot90(Up,3),East,rot90(Down)); 
        IMGWest=cat(1,rot90(Up),West,rot90(Down,-1)); 
        IMGSouth=cat(1,rot90(Up,2),South,rot90(Down,2)); 
        IMGV1=cat(2,IMGEast,IMGSouth,IMGWest); 
         
        %         VDDown =rot90(imcrop(Down,[0 2418 3200 782]),2);

        VDSouth=South;

        VDWest=imcrop(West,[0 0 1422 3200]); 
        %         VDWestDown=rot90(imcrop(Down,[2418 0 782 3200]),-1); 
        %         VDWestUp=rot90(imcrop((rot90(Up,2)),[2418 0 0 3200])); 
         
         
        IMG1=imcrop(IMGV1,[1314 2202 6768 4836]); 
        IMG2=cat(2,VDEast,VDSouth,VDWest); 
         
        for jj=1:2 
            my_field=sprintf('IMG%g',jj); 
            A=eval(my_field); 
            Bori=A; 
            
            %transfer the image to CIELab 
            Blabori=rgb2lab(Bori); 
            %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
            %size of the input image-level 1 
            %for achromatic only use Light* 
            Lori=Blabori(:,:,1); 
            k=1; 
            H=100; 
             
            while H >= 6 % 5 step 
                %resize the picture 
                Blab=imresize(Blabori,1/(2^(k-1))); 
==PAGE:273==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
250 
 
                L=Blab(:,:,1); 
                a=Blab(:,:,2); 
                b=Blab(:,:,3); 
                %set weight for each channel 
                alpha=0.33; 
                beta=0.33; 
                gamma=0.33; 
                %calculate P value for pixels 
                P=alpha*L+beta*a+gamma*b; 
                [H, W]=size(L); 
  
                %replace all L with P since it is chromatic method 
                L=P; 
                %calculate pixel contrast value c 
                %%weighting factor for neighbors 
                a=1/(4*(sqrt(2)+1)); 
                b=1/(4+2*sqrt(2)); 
                %boundaries 
                c(1,1)=b*abs(L(1,2)-L(1,1))+b*abs(L(2,1)-
L(1,1))+a*abs(L(2,2)-L(1,1)); 
                c(1,W)=b*abs(L(1,W-1)-L(1,W))+a*abs(L(2,W-1)-
L(1,W))+b*abs(L(2,W)-L(1,W)); 
                c(H,1)=b*abs(L(H-1,1)-L(H,1))+a*abs(L(H-1,2)-
L(H,1))+b*abs(L(H,2)-L(H,1)); 
                c(H,W)=b*abs(L(H,W-1)-L(H,W))+b*abs(L(H-1,W)-
L(H,W))+a*abs(L(H-1,W-1)-L(H,W)); 
                for i=1:1 
                    for j=2:(W-1) 
                        c(1,j)= b*abs(L(1,j-1)-L(1,j))+b*abs(L(1,j+1)-
L(1,j))+a*abs(L(2,j-1)-L(1,j))+b*abs(L(2,j)-L(1,j))+a*abs(L(2,j+1)-L(1,j)); 
                    end 
                end 
                 
                for i=H:H 
                    for j=2:(W-1) 
                        c(H,j)= a*abs(L(H-1,j-1)-L(H,j))+b*abs(L(H-1,j)-
L(H,j))+a*abs(L(H-1,j+1)-L(H,j))+b*abs(L(H,j-1)-L(H,j))+b*abs(L(H,j+1)-
L(H,j)); 
                    end 
                end 
                 
                for j=1:1 
                    for i=2:(H-1) 
                        c(i,1)=b*abs(L(i-1,1)-L(i,1))+a*abs(L(i-1,2)-
L(i,1))+b*abs(L(i,2)-L(i,1))+b*abs(L(i+1,1)-L(i,1))+a*abs(L(i+1,2)-L(i,1)); 
                    end 
                end 
                 
                for j=W:W 
                    for i=2:(H-1) 
                        c(i,W)= a*abs(L(i-1,W-1)-L(i,W))+b*abs(L(i-1,W)-
L(i,W))+b*abs(L(i,W-1)-L(i,W))+a*abs(L(i+1,W-1)-L(i,W))+b*abs(L(i+1,W)-
L(i,W)); 
                    end 
                end 
                %centers 
                for i=2:(H-1) 
                    for j=2:(W-1) 
                        c(i,j)= a*abs(L(i-1,j-1)-L(i,j))+b*abs(L(i-1,j)-
L(i,j))+a*abs(L(i-1,j+1)-L(i,j))+b*abs(L(i,j-1)-L(i,j))+b*abs(L(i,j+1)-
==PAGE:274==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
251 
 
L(i,j))+a*abs(L(i+1,j-1)-L(i,j))+b*abs(L(i+1,j)-L(i,j))+a*abs(L(i+1,j+1)-
L(i,j)); 
                    end 
                end 
                %mean value of the pixel contrast at each level 
                caverage(k)=mean2(c); 
                c=[]; 
                k=k+1; 
            end 
             
            caverageall=[caverageall;caverage]; 
            RAMMGL=mean(caverageall')'; 
            RAMMGL=mean(RAMMGL); 
             
            RAMMGLcollect=[{scene},{time},{my_field},{RAMMGL}]; 
            RAMMGLall=[RAMMGLall;RAMMGLcollect]; 
        end 
        % saving the mat file and xlsx file 
        filename= sprintf('%s%s',outdir,'RAMMG_chromatic\'); 
        save(filename,'RAMMGLall') 
        filename2 = sprintf('%s%s',outdir,'Contrast_chromatic.xlsx'); 
        writecell(RAMMGLall,filename2) 
    end

10.10.3 Matlab Script for the Chromatic and Achromatic Vis-
ual Complexity Algorithm 
 
%%%%%Complexity&Image Qualitychromatic%%%%%%%% 
%modified 14.06.21 Mandana S. Khanie

            %reading different images and building one image for the calcu-
lations 
             
            if strcmp(IMG,'D')==1 
                Down=hdrread(imagedata);

                East=hdrread(imagedata); 
            elseif strcmp(IMG,'S')==1

            elseif strcmp(IMG,'U')==1 
                Up= hdrread(imagedata);

        % cropping images for creating two different images, IMG1 with up 
        % and down head movement, IMG2 without up and down head movement. 
        % using imcrop the images are cropped using the accurate input for 
        % the cropping rectangular region.  % [L,rect]=imcrop(IMGV1) was 
        % used to assit finding the cropping region inputs. 
         
        IMGEast=cat(1,rot90(Up,3),East,rot90(Down)); 
        IMGWest=cat(1,rot90(Up),West,rot90(Down,-1)); 
        IMGSouth=cat(1,rot90(Up,2),South,rot90(Down,2)); 
        IMGV1=cat(2,IMGEast,IMGSouth,IMGWest);

1);

        VDSouth=South; 
        %         VDUp=rot90(imcrop(Up,[0 0 3200 1422]),2); 
        VDWest=imcrop(West,[0 0 1422 3200]); 
        %         VDWestDown=rot90(imcrop(Down,[2418 0 782 3200]),-1); 
        %         VDWestUp=rot90(imcrop((rot90(Up,2)),[2418 0 0 3200])); 
         
         
        IMG1=imcrop(IMGV1,[1314 2202 6768 4836]); 
        IMG2=cat(2,VDEast,VDSouth,VDWest); 
         
        for jj=1:2 
            %runing calculations on through the two images 
            my_field=sprintf('IMG%g',jj); 
             
            A=eval(my_field); 
             
            nik=niqe(A); 
            brisk=brisque(A); 
            pik=piqe(A); 
            contrastValue=max(rgb2gray(A))-min(rgb2gray(A)); 
            contrastValueMean=mean(contrastValue); 
            contrastValueStd=std(contrastValue); 
            %using RGBI to calculate illuminance 
            RGBi=A; 
            %calculating Illuminance 
            R=RGBi(:,:,1); 
            G=RGBi(:,:,2); 
==PAGE:277==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
254 
 
            B=RGBi(:,:,3); 
            L= 179 * (R * .265 + G * .67 + B * .065); 
            La=mean(mean(L)); 
            %transfer the image to CIELab 
            Bori=A; 
            Blabori=rgb2lab(Bori); 
            %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
            %size of the input image-level 1 
            %for achromatic only use Light* 
            Lori=Blabori(:,:,1);

putational time reduction) 
            %B=imresize(A,0.05); 
             
            %If resizing the image is not necessary 
            B=A; 
             
            %transfer the image to CIELab 
            Blab=rgb2lab(B); 
            %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
            %for achromatic P=L* 
            PL=Blab(:,:,1); 
            %elements for calculation-WH 
            [W, H]=size(PL); 
            %elements for calculation-PL average 
            PLaverage=mean2(PL); 
            %calculate the standard deviation for all values 
            for i=1:W 
                x1=0; 
                for j=1:H 
                    x0=(PL(i,j)-PLaverage)^2; 
                    x1=x1+x0; 
                end 
                X(i)=x1; 
            end 
            Y=sum(X); 
            %output the standard deviation of achromatic value 
            CPL=sqrt(Y/(W*H)); 
            %             fprintf(fidA,'%s ',imagedata); 
            %             fprintf(fidA,'%s\n',SPL(end)); 
            %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
            %for chromatic P=L* 
            L=Blab(:,:,1); 
            a=Blab(:,:,2); 
            b=Blab(:,:,3); 
            %set weight for each channel 
            alpha=0.33; % the combination should be 1. cross check with the 
paper. 
            beta=0.33; 
            gamma=0.33; 
            %calculate P value for pixels 
            PLab=alpha*L+beta*a+gamma*b; 
            %elements for calculation-PL average 
            PLabaverage=mean2(PLab); 
            %calculate the standard deviation for all values 
==PAGE:278==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
255 
 
            for i=1:W 
                x1ab=0; 
                for j=1:H 
                    x0ab=(PLab(i,j)-PLabaverage)^2; 
                    x1ab=x1ab+x0ab; 
                end 
                Xab(i)=x1ab; 
            end 
            Yab=sum(Xab); 
            %output the standard deviation of chromatic value 
            CPLab=sqrt(Yab/(W*H)); 
             
            COMPLEXcol-
lect=[{scene},{time},{my_field},{CPL},{CPLab},{nik},{brisk},{pik},{con-
trastValueMean},{contrastValueStd},{La}]; 
            Complexall=[Complexall;COMPLEXcollect]; 
        end 
         
        % saving the mat file and xlsx file 
        filename= sprintf('%s%s',outdir,'Complexityall'); 
        save(filename,'Complexall') 
        filename2 = sprintf('%s%s',outdir,'Complexity.xlsx'); 
        writecell(Complexall,filename2) 
    end 
     
     
end

10.10.4 Connectedness.bat by Mikkel Kofod Pedersen 
 
@echo off 
SET RAYPATH=C:\Radiance\lib 
PATH=C:\Radiance\bin 
 
:: Set view point x, y, z. 
set vpX=7.5 
set vpY=5.0 
set vpZ=5.7 
 
:: Set Reinhart discretization. Higher number means higher precision. 
set mf_sun_pos=128 
 
:: Set bubble radius. This should be larger than the distance 
:: between the view point and the furthest geometry in the model. 
set radius=50 
 
:: Write a file called bubble.rad with a glow material. 
( 
echo void glow bubbleGlow 
echo 0 
echo 0 
echo 4 1 1 1 0 
echo bubbleGlow bubble sphereGeometry 
echo 0 
echo 0 
echo 4 
echo %vpX% %vpY% %vpZ% 
==PAGE:280==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
257 
 
echo %radius% 
) > bubble.rad 
 
:: Write black material 
( 
echo void plastic black  
echo 0  
echo 0  
echo 5 0 0 0 0 0 
) > blackMaterial.rad 
 
:: Create octree. Exclude outside geometry such as ground, but include exterior of the build-
ing. 
:: IMPORTANT: All glazing surfaces should be excluded as well. 
xform -s 0.001 ./geometry.rad > geometry_scaled.rad 
xform -m black ./blackMaterial.rad ./geometry_scaled.rad | oconv -f -w - bubble.rad > 
model.oct 
 
:: --------------------------------------------- 
:: EVERYTHING BELOW THIS SHOULD NOT BE CHANGED. 
:: --------------------------------------------- 
 
:: Calculates the number of sun position by the Reinhart discretization (MF:x). 
set /A num_sun_pos=144*%mf_sun_pos%*%mf_sun_pos%+1 
 
:: Multiplies the number of sun positions by two. 
set /A tot_num_sun_pos=%num_sun_pos%*2 
 
:: Writes a file of view rays to use in rtrace. The second is the negative in z-direction (bottom 
hemisphere). 
==PAGE:281==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
258 
 
:: The rays are centered at the view point and the direction is the direction to centre point of 
the  
:: discretized sky patches. 
cnt %num_sun_pos% | rcalc -e MF:%mf_sun_pos% -f reinsrc.cal -e Rbin=recno -o "%vpX% 
%vpY% %vpZ% ${Dx} ${Dy} ${Dz}" > viewrays.rays 
cnt %num_sun_pos% | rcalc -e MF:%mf_sun_pos% -f reinsrc.cal -e Rbin=recno -o "%vpX% 
%vpY% %vpZ% ${Dx} ${Dy} -${Dz}" >> viewrays.rays 
 
:: Gives the number of view rays that intersects with the bubble as a percentage of the total 
number of rays. 
:: With -ov in rtrace the output will be 1 for every ray that intersects with bubble, because it 
has a 
:: glow material of 1 1 1. Rays that intersects other geometry will be 0. Piping the results into 
"total" 
:: will count the list of ones and zeros 
echo Reinhart sky discretization is MF:%mf_sun_pos%. 
echo Total number of rays is %tot_num_sun_pos%. 
echo Percentage of rays that leaves the building [%%]: 
rtrace -w -h -fa -ov model.oct < viewrays.rays | rcalc -e "$1=$1" | total | rcalc -e 
"$1=$1/%tot_num_sun_pos%*100" 
pause

using System.Collections;

using UnityEngine;

public class LaserClicker : MonoBehaviour 
{

    public Text Option1;

    public Text Option4;

    private void Awake()

    }

        choices = e.target.name; 
        questions = QuestionBox.text; 
        CreateText();

        {

lig?"; 
            Option1.text = "1. Meget tilfredsstillende"; 
            Option2.text = "2. Tilfredsstillende"; 
            Option3.text = "3. Hverken eller"; 
            Option4.text = "4. Utilfredsstillende"; 
==PAGE:283==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
260 
 
            Option5.text = "5. Meget Utilfredsstillende"; 
        } 
        else if (QuestionBox.text == "B. Hvad synes du om mængden af udsigt, der er

        { 
            QuestionBox.text = "C. Hvor behagelig er typen af solafskærmning?"; 
            Option1.text = "1. Meget behagelig"; 
            Option2.text = "2. Behagelig"; 
            Option3.text = "3. Hverken eller"; 
            Option4.text = "4. Ubehagelig";

        else if (QuestionBox.text == "D. Hvad ville du synes om, at arbejde under

        { 
            QuestionBox.text = ""; 
            CreateSpaces(); 
            SceneLoad(); 
        } 
 
    } 
 
    public void SceneLoad() 
    { 
        if (sceneIndexs.Count < 1) 
        { 
            SceneManager.LoadScene(2); //Set END scene 
            return; 
        } 
        int index = Random.Range(0, sceneIndexs.Count); 
        int theSceneIndex = sceneIndexs[index]; // Access Scene 
        sceneIndexs.RemoveAt(index); 
        playedScenes.Add(theSceneIndex); 
        SceneManager.LoadScene(theSceneIndex); 
 
    } 
 
 
    void CreateText() 
    { 
 
        //Path of the file 
        string path = System.Environment.GetFolderPath(System.Environment.Special-
Folder.MyDocuments) + "/QuestionnaireAnswers/Lars.txt"; 
         
        //Create file if it does not exist 
        if (!File.Exists(path)) 
        { 
            File.WriteAllText(path, "Answers: \n\n"); 
             
        } 
 
==PAGE:284==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
261 
 
        //Content of the file 
        string content = ":" + questions + ":" + choices + "\n"; 
 
 
        //Add some text to it 
        currentscene = SceneManager.GetActiveScene(); 
 
        File.AppendAllText(path, currentscene.name); 
        File.AppendAllText(path, content); 
        
    } 
 
    void CreateSpaces() 
    { 
        string path = System.Environment.GetFolderPath(System.Environment.Special-
Folder.MyDocuments) + "/QuestionnaireAnswers/Lars.txt"; 
        string createspaces = "\n\n"; 
        File.AppendAllText(path, createspaces); 
    } 
 
 
}

10.10.7 TimerSceneChanger.cs 
 
using System.Collections; 
using System.Collections.Generic; 
using UnityEngine; 
using UnityEngine.SceneManagement; 
using System; 
 
public class TimerSceneChanger : MonoBehaviour 
{ 
    public int sceneIndex; 
    // Start is called before the first frame update 
    void Start() 
    { 
        StartCoroutine(ChangeScene(sceneIndex)); 
    } 
 
 
    public IEnumerator ChangeScene(int sceneIndex) 
    { 
        if (sceneIndex ==156) 
        { 
            yield return new WaitForSeconds(15f); 
            SceneManager.LoadScene(sceneIndex); 
        } 
      yield return new  WaitForSeconds(15f); 
      SceneManager.LoadScene(sceneIndex); 
 
    } 
}

10.10.8 Batch Script for the Glare Evaluation  
 
Ambient.bat 
 
 
 
S.bat 
 
 
 
 
 
 
erase ambient1.amb 
xform -s 0.001 ./geometry.rad > geometry_scaled.rad 
oconv material.rad sky.rad outside_skymapping.rad geometry_scaled.rad > OCT.oct 
rpict -t 15 -vth -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 0 -1 0 -vu 0 0 1 -vh 180 -vv 180 -x 64 -y 64 -ps 4 -
pt .10 -pj .9 -dj .5 -ds .25 -dt .25 -dc .5 -dr 1 -dp 256 -st .5 -ab 3 -aa .2 -ar 256 -ad 2048 -as 
1024 -lr 4 -lw .05 -af ambient1.amb OCT.oct > ambient.hdr 
erase ambient.hdr 
start S.bat 
exit 
for %%* in (.) do set CurrDirName=%%~nx* 
echo %CurrDirName% 
erase %CurrDirName%_S.hdr 
rpict -t 15 -vth -vp 7.500 5.000 5.700 -vd 0 -1 0 -vu 0 0 1 -vh 180 -vv 180 -x 1000 -y 1000 -
ps 4 -pt .10 -pj .9 -dj .5 -ds .25 -dt .25 -dc .5 -dr 1 -dp 256 -st .5 -ab 3 -aa .2 -ar 256 -ad 
2048 -as 1024 -lr 4 -lw .05 -af ambient1.amb OCT.oct > S.hdr 
ren S.hdr %CurrDirName%_S.hdr 
erase ambient1.amb 
evalglare -d -c viz.hdr S.hdr > results.txt 
pause 
exit

10.10.9 Matlab Script: Luminance Contrast – Date and Time  
 
%%%%%RAMMGachromatic%%%%%%%% 
%readin the rendered images 
clear 
%the input directory 
indir = 'C:\Users\s154193\Desktop\JENS\Contrast\JAN_FEB_APR_MAY'; %change 
this path 
caverageall=[]; 
cd(indir); 
% writing a txt file of names 
fid=fopen('C:\Users\s154193\Desktop\JENS\Con-
trast\JAN_FEB_APR_MAY\RAMMGL_Results.txt','a+'); % change this part: C:\Us-
ers\masak\Dropbox\DTU2020\StudentThesis\MariaMaria\test 
%reading the mat files 
File=dir(fullfile('*.hdr')); 
for kk = 1:size(File) 
     
    Files=File(kk,1); 
    imagedata = getfield(Files,'name'); 
     
    A=hdrread(imagedata); 
    %resize the image (the resize is just to reduce the calculation time in 
    %this casesa and 0.05 is reached through trial and error).  
    %Bori=imresize(A,0.05); 
    Bori=A; 
    %transfer the image to CIELab 
    Blabori=rgb2lab(Bori); 
    %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
    %size of the input image-level 1 
    %for achromatic only use Light* 
    Lori=Blabori(:,:,1); 
    %elements for calculation-WH (width and height) 
    %[Wori Hori]=size(Lori); 
    %level number N : the level number is about the precision of the 
    %algoriuthm. devide the size of the image to small. 4 has been set due 
    %to computational time.  
    k=1; 
    W=100; 
     
    while W >= 6 
        %resize the picture 
        Blab=imresize(Blabori,1/(2^(k-1))); 
        Light=Blab(:,:,1); 
        [W, H]=size(Light); 
        H=W; 
        %calculate pixel contrast value c 
        %%weighting factor for neighbors 
        a=1/(4*(sqrt(2)+1)); 
        b=1/(4+2*sqrt(2)); 
        %Corner boundaries (corner pixels) 
        c(1,1)=b*abs(Light(1,2)-Light(1,1))+b*abs(Light(2,1)-
Light(1,1))+a*abs(Light(2,2)-Light(1,1)); 
        c(1,W)=b*abs(Light(1,W-1)-Light(1,W))+a*abs(Light(2,W-1)-
Light(1,W))+b*abs(Light(2,W)-Light(1,W)); 
        c(H,1)=b*abs(Light(H-1,1)-Light(H,1))+a*abs(Light(H-1,2)-
Light(H,1))+b*abs(Light(H,2)-Light(H,1)); 
        c(H,W)=b*abs(Light(H,W-1)-Light(H,W))+b*abs(Light(H-1,W)-
Light(H,W))+a*abs(Light(H-1,W-1)-Light(H,W)); 
==PAGE:288==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
265 
 
        %Edge boundaries (edge pixels) 
        for i=1:1 
            for j=2:(W-1) 
                c(1,j)= b*abs(Light(1,j-1)-Light(1,j))+b*abs(Light(1,j+1)-
Light(1,j))+a*abs(Light(2,j-1)-Light(1,j))+b*abs(Light(2,j)-
Light(1,j))+a*abs(Light(2,j+1)-Light(1,j)); 
            end 
        end 
         
        for i=H:H 
            for j=2:(W-1) 
                c(H,j)= a*abs(Light(H-1,j-1)-Light(H,j))+b*abs(Light(H-
1,j)-Light(H,j))+a*abs(Light(H-1,j+1)-Light(H,j))+b*abs(Light(H,j-1)-
Light(H,j))+b*abs(Light(H,j+1)-Light(H,j)); 
            end 
        end 
         
        for j=1:1 
            for i=2:(H-1) 
                c(i,1)=b*abs(Light(i-1,1)-Light(i,1))+a*abs(Light(i-1,2)-
Light(i,1))+b*abs(Light(i,2)-Light(i,1))+b*abs(Light(i+1,1)-
Light(i,1))+a*abs(Light(i+1,2)-Light(i,1)); 
            end 
        end 
         
        for j=W:W 
            for i=2:(H-1) 
                c(i,W)= a*abs(Light(i-1,W-1)-Light(i,W))+b*abs(Light(i-
1,W)-Light(i,W))+b*abs(Light(i,W-1)-Light(i,W))+a*abs(Light(i+1,W-1)-
Light(i,W))+b*abs(Light(i+1,W)-Light(i,W)); 
            end 
        end 
        %centers (remaining pixels that are not corner pixels) 
        for i=2:(H-1) 
            for j=2:(W-1) 
                c(i,j)= a*abs(Light(i-1,j-1)-Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i-
1,j)-Light(i,j))+a*abs(Light(i-1,j+1)-Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i,j-1)-
Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i,j+1)-Light(i,j))+a*abs(Light(i+1,j-1)-
Light(i,j))+b*abs(Light(i+1,j)-Light(i,j))+a*abs(Light(i+1,j+1)-
Light(i,j)); 
            end 
        end 
        %mean value of the pixel contrast at each level 
        caverage(k)=mean2(c); 
        c=[]; 
        k=k+1; 
    end 
    %RAMMGL=mean(caverage); 
    caverageall=[caverageall;caverage]; 
    % filenameall={filenameall;imagedata}; 
    RAMMGL=mean(caverageall')'; 
    fprintf(fid,'%s ',imagedata); 
    fprintf(fid,'%s\n',RAMMGL(end)); 
end

10.16 Tone Mapping Operator Reinhard02, 
Gamma Value and Auto Levels 
 
A sub-study conducted by Mikkel Kofod Pederesn. 
In the picture below, the effect of various settings in Luminance HDR and pfstools is investi-
gated. As shown, the first row of images is dark. The rendering is on the 21st of March at 
12:00 with a sunny sky in a south facing office. 
Comparing this scene with a real-life east facing office room at 15th of March 16:52 (no di-
rect light or electrical lighting), the virtual scenes are perceived as darker than the real-life 
room.  
If the gamma is set to 2.2 and Auto Level is unchecked, the image created with Luminance 
HDR reflect the image created with pfstool.

10.18 Screen Modelling by Mandana S. Khanie 
10.18.1 Roller blinds guide 
 
Material set up:  
----------------material description------------------------------ 
void glass fabric_outerlayer 
0 
0 
4 1 1 1 1.05 
 
void BRTDfunc shading 
10 0.0 0.0 0.0 
tau(DIRTRANS,4.0,2.9,CUTOFF) 
tau(DIRTRANS,4.0,2.9,CUTOFF) 
tau(DIRTRANS,4.0,2.9,CUTOFF) 
0  0  0  
roos_fabric.cal  
0 
9 DIFREFL DIFREFL DIFREFL DIFREFL DIFREFL DIFREFL DIFTRANS DIFTRANS DIFTRANS  
Replace the placeholders in BRTDfunc by: 
DIRTRANS: direct transmission for perpendicular incidence (=tau_dir , visible) (range 0-1) , 
typical 0.00-0.05 (values >0.08 have definitive a glare problem) 
DIFTRANS: diffuse transmission for perpendicular incidence (=tau_diff, visible) (range 0-1) , 
typical 0.05-0.30 The summation of diffuse and direct transmission define the light transmis-
sion value.  
CUTOFF: cutoff angle of the fabric (0-90 degree). The reasonable values for cutoff angle is 
50-90 degree where as the 75 degree is typically used.  
DIFREFL: diffuse reflection (0-1). The reasonable values  are between 0.05-0.60. Typical 
roller blinds have the following colors : 0.05 (black), 0.3 (grey), 0.5 (white). 
According European standard EN14500/14501, the manufacturer should deliver direct and 
diffuse transmission.  
 
The cut-off-angle will probably be included in the revision of before mentioned standard. If 
the cutoff angle is not mentioned then 75° is a typical value. Normally a cutoff angle be-
tween 50-90 degree are available on the market.   
 
In order to fulfill the physical law: 1=ABSORBTION+TRANSMISSION+REFLETION, the trans-
mission and reflection values should be selected carefully to fall into the mentioned law. 
Consider that absorption is between 0.01-0.95.  
 
==PAGE:310==
APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10 
287 
 
A standard trans material, RGB and transmission can be calculated according to the material 
properties.The flow-chart here can be used: http://www.schorsch.com/en/software/ray-
front/manual/transdef.html 
The calculation file roos_fabric_cal should be either in your local directory OR in the radi-
ance-lib directory. 
 
The model set up:  
Three geometry layers are needed.  
 
 
1) a polygon as a glazing material  
2) a polygon between glazing and the shading, material name "fabric_outerlayer", size =size 
of fabric 
3) the polygon for the fabric, material "shading"

APPENDIX – CHAPTER 10

10.18.3 Roos_fabric.cal 
{ 
 
trans_refl.cal - A. Roos model for transmission  
 
adapted model for reflection 
 
 
 
by J. Wienold Sep 2012, ISE 
        modified for Fabric transmission with cut-off-angle-input by J. Wienold, Apr. 2016 
         
        cut: Cut-Off angle in degree 
 
} 
 
 {normalized angle of incidence } 
z0 = acos(abs(bound(-1,Rdot,1)))*0.636619772368; 
  
 {power function} 
pow(a,b) =  if(a,exp(b*log(a)),0); 
   
 {Roos model parameters } 
a = 8; 
b(q) = 0.25/q;  
c(p,q) = (1-a-b(q)); 
 
alpha(q) = 5.2+0.7*q; 
beta = 2; 
gamma(p,q) = (5.26+0.06*p)+(0.73+0.04*p)*q; 
 
 {roos transmission model} 
t1(t0,p,q,cut) = t0*(1-a*pow((z0+1-cut/90),alpha(q))-b(q)*pow((z0+1-cut/90),beta)-
c(p,q)*pow((z0+1-cut/90),gamma(p,q))); 
tau(t0,p,q,cut) = if ((cut/90.0-z0),t1(t0,p,q,cut),0);
